Board 7
Mark Taimanov – Wolfgang Uhlmann [D 80]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 d5
Taimanov:
Uhlmann is among those grandmasters who unchangingly defends his creative convictions. His opening repertoire is not wide, but it has been worked out to perfection. Therefore I did not doubt that the Grünfeld Defence - one of Uhlmann’s ‘hobby horses’ - would certainly be encountered in the games of our duel. The task of preparation consisted in the necessity to find a variation which, even to such an opening expert as Uhlmann, would prove to be a surprise.
4.Bg5 Ne4 5.Bh4
Taimanov:
This was my preparation. Theory in the main examines the continuation 5.Bf4 or 5.cd5 Ng5 6.h4. In my opinion, the bishop stands very well at h4, since it later exerts pressure on the e7-pawn. I had already employed this idea twice - against Savon in the most recent championship of the USSR, and against Filip in the tournament at Wijk aan Zee - and both times with success, but I knew that these games had still not been the focus of attention of the theoreticians and I reckoned on an element of surprise. As the further course of events showed, I was mistaken in my assumption, but only in part.
5...c5 6.cd5
Uhlmann:
(!)
6...Nc3
Taimanov:
If 6...Qa5, then 7.Qd3!.
7.bc3 Qd5
Uhlmann:
7...Qa5 would be followed by 8.Qd2 Bg7 9.e3 with a slightly better game for White.
8.e3
8...cd4
Taimanov:
All of these moves were played very quickly by Uhlmann, which was evidence of his preparedness for my variation. However, after this last exchange I understood that Uhlmann had assessed it incorrectly. There now arises an endgame which, despite its seeming simplicity, contains no little ‘venom’.
9.Qd4
Taimanov/Uhlmann:
(!)
9...Qd4 10.cd4
Taimanov:
White’s advantage takes shape from several small plusses - a slight advantage in development, a firm pawn centre and the eventual possibility of pressure on the pawns at e7 and b7. Do not dare to claim that this is sufficient for decisive play for the win, but White probably has many chances to present ‘problems’ for the opponent. At any rate, in preparation for this match, I very much wanted to obtain this endgame.
10...Nc6
RR: Subsequently, the plan 10...e6(!), followed by the development of the bishop on the diagonal f8-a3, was found for Black here. - Griffin.
11.Bb5 Bd7 12.Nf3 Bg7 13.0-0 e6 14.Rab1
Taimanov:
(!)
14...0-0
15.Nd2
Uhlmann:
(!)
Taimanov:
Black has made only natural developing moves and, it seems, does not experience any difficulties. Meanwhile, his position is already difficult. There is threatened the manoeuvre Nd2-e4 (or Nd2-b3), forcing a weakening of Black’s position, and to counter these is not a simple matter. Possibly, Uhlmann had played inaccurately? It is difficult to say, and moreover it would not have been practical to seek an improvement for him while the match was still in progress. We were faced with playing one more Grünfeld Defence, and Uhlmann’s task was to himself find a better method of development. But perhaps one does not exist, and the entire system with the exchange of queens is faulty?
15...f5
Taimanov:
Both now and previously, the move ...a7-a6 would, after the withdrawal of the bishop, have only laid bare new weaknesses in Black’s position.
16.Nb3 b6
Taimanov:
Since to permit the knight to c5 would have been altogether bad, he has to agree to a new weakening.
17.Rfc1 Rac8 18.Ba6 Rce8
Uhlmann:
If 18...Rb8?, then 19.Bg3 e5 20.Bb5, and White would win the e5-pawn.
19.Bb7
Taimanov:
White’s initiative develops of its own accord. It is not in Black’s power to prevent an invasion along the c-file.
19...Nd8 20.Rc7 Rf7
Taimanov:
Naturally, Black would have been sorry to immediately part with a pawn, but perhaps, rather than going over to ‘deep’ defence it was worth trying to enliven the pieces with 20...Nb7 21.Rb7 Bc6 22.Ra7 Ra8 23.Ra8 Ra8 24.Nc1 b5.
Uhlmann:
Sacrificing a pawn might have given Black a good defensive opportunity:
20...Nb7 21.Rb7 (not 21.Rd7 because of 21...Rf7 22.Rf7 Kf7 23.Rc1 Bf6 with equality) 21...Bc6 22.Ra7 Ra8 23.Ra8 Ra8 24.Nc1 b5 with good drawing chances.
21.Ba6 Ba4 22.Rbc1 Bf8 23.R1c4 Rc7 24.Rc7 Nc6
Taimanov:
For the time being Black has managed to retain the material balance, but one feels that, under the pressure of the white pieces, his fortifications could collapse from one minute to the next.
25.Bc4
Taimanov:
(!)
25...Bg7
Uhlmann:
25...Bd6 would be followed by 26.Rb7.
26.Rc8
Taimanov:
(?) This outwardly spectacular move, winning a pawn, deserves not praise, but censure. I simply ‘sold myself short’, in trading my huge positional advantage for a single pawn. During the game I was aware of the fact that strengthening the pressure by means of 26.Bg3 was more in keeping with the demands of the position, but the desire to have a ‘bird in the hand’ took precedence over the demands of reason.
Uhlmann:
(!?) Probably, 26.Bg3 would have been more precise, as Black could not have done anything to counter the threat of Rc7-c8; 26...Kh8 would not do because of 27.Be6.
26...Rc8 27.Be6 Kf8 28.Bc8 Nb4 29.Nc1 Kf7
Taimanov:
(!) Suddently the character of the struggle has changed. Now the black pieces become active, while White’s prove to be isolated and disunited. And, although the extra pawn at d4 as if ‘smooths over’ White’s chagrin, the win has become very problematic.
30.Bg3
Taimanov:
Otherwise 30...f4! is unpleasant.
30...Bf8 31.Bb7
Uhlmann:
(?) This definitely lessens White’s chances of victory. A much better move would have been 31.Bb8, since on 31...Nc6 he would have had ready the reply 32.Bd7.
31...Ke6 32.Bb8 Kd7
Taimanov/Uhlmann:
(!)
33.Bf3
Taimanov/Uhlmann:
It is clear that both now and at the following moves, it was impossible to take the pawn at a7 on account of the reply ...Kd7-c7.
33...Bb5 34.Bd1 a5 35.a4 Bc4
Taimanov:
As previously, White retains a material advantage, but already now it is obvious that for the pawn Black has active piece play, giving him good chances of salvation.
36.g4
Taimanov
: (!) The best plan of counter-play. White strives to create pawn weaknesses in the enemy camp. After other continuations Black would, in general, have had no concerns.
36...b5
Taimanov:
Black’s main trump is the passed pawn.
Uhlmann:
A very dangerous passed pawn has been created; this represents a good chance for Black.
37.gf5 gf5 38.Be5 Nc6
Taimanov:
With this natural move (winning a tempo!) Black significantly complicates his defence. Correct was 38...Nd5!, which after 39.ab5 Bb5 40.Bc2 Ke6 41.Nd3 a4 retained for Black chances of a succesful resistance.
Uhlmann:
(?!) 38...Nd5 might have been better.
39.Bh8 Ba3 40.ab5 Bc1
Taimanov:
More tenacious was 40...Bb5, for example: 41.Nb3 a4 42.Nc5 Bc5 43.dc5 Ke6. The recent poor decisions were taken by Uhlmann in time trouble.
41.bc6 Kc6 42.Ba4 Kd5
Taimanov:
The opinion was expressed that with 42...Bb5 it was still possible to save the game, since White is given nothing by 43.d5 Kc5 44.Bd4 Kb4. However, it is not clear how Black would have held on after the simple 43.Bc2.
Uhlmann:
(?) 42...Bb5 should have been played, since the reply 43.d5 is not dangerous because of 43...Kc5 44.Bd4 Kb4, giving equal chances.
White would have had to play 43.Bc2, but after 43...a4 44.Bf5 a3 45.Bh7 Bc4 Black would still have had excellent drawing chances.
43.Bc2
Taimanov:
Here too this manoeuvre is very strong.
43...Ke6 44.Kg2
Taimanov:
The threat of transferring the king to f4 presents Black with irresolvable difficulties.
44...Ba3 45.Be5 Bf8 46.Bc7 Bd5 47.f3 Bb4 48.Kg3
Taimanov:
In this position the game was adjourned. As analysis showed, for White’s victory, mere technical accuracy is sufficient.
48...Kf6
Taimanov:
If 48...Bd6 49.Bd6 Kd6, then simplest of all is 50.Kf4 Be6 51.e4 fe4 52.fe4.
49.Be5 Kg6 50.Kf4 Be6 51.Ba4
Uhlmann:
(!)
Taimanov:
(!) Not only blocking the pawn, but in some cases threatening a check at e8.
51...Bf8 52.e4
Taimanov:
Naturally, not 52.Be8 Bf7 53.Bf7 Kf7 54.Kf5 in view of 54...a4, and the pawn becomes dangerous. Instead now the game is won automatically. There still followed:
52...Bh6 53.Kg3 fe4 54.fe4 Bd2 55.d5 Be1 56.Kf3 Bh3 57.Be8 Kh6 58.Bf6
Black resigned.
1 : 0
[Taimanov/Uhlmann]
Board 8
Milan Matulović – Mikhail Botvinnik [B 06]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.e4 g6 2.d4 Bg7 3.Nf3 d6 4.Bc4
Matulović:
At this point in my game against Botvinnik at the ‘Liberation of Belgrade’ tournament in 1969, I played 4.Nc3. I tried the more aggressive text move at the Zonal Tournament in Athens, 1969, against Hübner.
4...Nf6 5.Qe2
Botvinnik:
A popular variation of the Pirc-Ufimtsev Defence. White’s king’s bishop is very active and Black should limit its role with ...c7-c6 followed by ...d6-d5. This plan Black has succesfully carried out in the games Gipslis - Botvinnik and Matanović - Botvinnik.
RR: The first of these encounters took place, in the 3rd USSR People’s Spartakiad (Moscow, 1963); the second is referenced below. - Griffin.
5...c6 6.Bb3 0-0 7.Bg5
Matulović:
I also played this move in the above-mentioned game against Hübner at Athens. It is more usual to play 7.0-0, as for instance, in the game Matanović - Botvinnik, Belgrade 1969.
Botvinnik:
Matulović ‘s invention. White prevents the above-mentioned plan by Black, since now in some variations the e7-pawn will be pinned (the advance ...e7-e6 is an essential link in this plan). But at h4 White’s queen’s bishop will occupy a not entirely succesful position.
7...h6 8.Bh4 e5
Botvinnik:
Thus, White retains an active bishop at b3. Black’s counter-play consists in the manoeuvre ...Nh5-f4.
9.de5 de5 10.Nbd2
Botvinnik:
Naturally, not 10.Ne5 Qa5. Now the e5-pawn is in need of immediate defence.
10...Qc7 11.Nc4 Nh5 12.Bg3
Matulović:
This move is forced. White cannot allow the knight to remain at f4, since after 12.0-0-0 Nf4 13.Qf1 Bg4 Black would have good chances.
Botvinnik:
Cleverly played. After 12...Bg4 13.Be5! Be5 14.Nce5 Nf4 15.Qd2 White’s advantage is obvious. Therefore Black has no choice.
12...Nf4
Matulović:
(!) An excellent move. Although it doubles Black’s pawns and opens the path to White’s e-pawn, Black correctly foresees that eventually Black’s bishop will have the chance to operate. 12...Ng3 would have been weak because of 13.hg3 b5 14.Ne3 with the idea of 15.0-0-0, and White has good prospects of an attack with 16.g4 etc. 12...Bg4 is not good either, as White would win a pawn.
13.Bf4 ef4 14.0-0-0
Matulović:
(?!) A ‘normal’ move. More precise would have been 14.e5 and 14...Bg4 15.Qe4 Bf3 16.gf3 with good chances for White. After playing the move in the text, after which a tempo is lost, Black gains the opportunity to become active.
Botvinnik:
Somewhat more active was the immediate 14.e5. Long castling has the shortcoming that White has to reckon with a possible attack on the b2-square. Black managed to carry out an analogous plan in the game Medina - Botvinnik (Palma de Mallorca, 1967).
14...Bg4 15.e5
Botvinnik:
In the case of 15.h3 Bf3 16.Qf3 Nd7 17.Nd6 Ne5 18.Qf4 Rfd8! White is left minus a piece.
15...Nd7 16.Qe4 Rad8
Matulović:
(!) An excellent move. Black now completes the development of his pieces and equalises, with better prospects ahead.
Botvinnik:
This is naturally stronger than 16...Bf3 17.gf3 Ne5 18.Ne5 Qe5 19.Qe5 Be5 20.Rd7!.
17.Qf4 Bf3 18.Qf3 b5 19.Ne3 Ne5 20.Qg3 a5 21.a3 Kh7
22.Ng4
Matulović:
(?) An illogical move, taking White into a semi-ending with bishops of opposite colour, but at the expense of many tempi, which Black will exploit to become active on the queen-side. A much better move would have been 22.f4! Nd7 23.f5 Be5 24.Qf3 Bf4 25.Kb1, and White would certainly not be in a worse position.
Botvinnik:
White exchanges knights in order to ease the defence, but this is a mistaken decision. Correct was 22.f4 Nd7 23.f5!, offering the exchange of queens and weakening the position of the black king.
22...h5 23.Ne5 Be5
Matulović:
Naturally, Black does not wish to exchange queens since the position of White’s king is very insecure, and Black has good prospects for attacking White’s castled position, despite the bishops of opposite colour*. White is unable to take the initiative, and Black has a solid advantage.
RR: Rather than “despite”, it is precisely because of the presence of opposite-coloured bishops that Black’s attack against the b2-square will prove to be so strong. - Griffin.
24.Qf3 Kg7 25.Rhe1
Matulović:
White’s only chance lay in exchanging pieces, first of all the queens and then the rooks. Therefore the right move was 25.Rd8 Rd8 26.Rd1 etc. Later, White will have to seek a reduction in the material, but it will already be too late.
25...Bf6 26.c3 c5
Botvinnik:
The attack on the b2-pawn begins. White’s last hope consists in the exchange of heavy pieces. Nothing was given by 26...Qh2 on account of 27.g3 or 27.Qc6.
27.Rd8 Rd8 28.g3
Matulović:
White must now lose a tempo to protect the pawn; Black uses this to organise a decisive attack. The opposite-coloured bishops gives Black the better chances.
Botvinnik:
More circumspect was 28.h3. The king-side pawns on dark squares can become an object of attack.
28...c4 29.Bc2 b4
Matulović:
This should have decided the game within a few moves.
30.cb4 ab4 31.ab4
31...Qb6
Matulović:
Carelessness, allowing White to offer resistance. After 31...Qa7! White would have had no means of defence, since 32.Qa3 would have been followed by 32...Qf2, while after 32.Kb1 Ra8 33.Qa3 Qd4! 34.Qc3 Qc3 Black would have won the rook.
Botvinnik:
Perhaps simpler was 31...Qa7! 32.Kb1 Qd4 33.Qc3 Qf2 34.Qe3 Qh2, or 32...Ra8 33.Qa3 Qf2 (33...Qd4 34.Rd1 Qe5 35.f4) 34.Qe3 Qh2.
32.Rd1 Qb4
Matulović:
Black is content with having one more pawn as the pieces are reduced. After 32...Re8! White would have been in a hopeless position, since the b4-pawn is indefensible. After the text move White still has some hope of saving himself.
Botvinnik:
(?) A serious mistake. After 32...Re8! (during the game Black examined only 32...Rb8) 33.Rd7 Qb4 34.Qd5 Re1 matters conclude with mate, if White avoids the variation 33.Qa3 Qf2.
33.Rd8 Qb2 34.Kd1 Bd8 35.Qd5 Qf6
36.Ke2
Matulović:
(?) A mistake due to time pressure. After 36.f4! White would have exploited the chance offered to him, since after 36...c3 37.Bb3, by bringing pressure to bear on f7 he could have held Black in check. Instead now Black’s pieces become very active.
Botvinnik:
Greater chances of a succesful defence were given by the immediate 36.f4 Qa1 37.Ke2 Bf6 38.Qc4 Qg1 39.Bb3. Now however, 36...Qc3 37.Bg6! will not do.
36...Bb6 37.f4 Qc3 38.Qe4 Bd4
Botvinnik:
A new blunder. After 38...Bg1 39.f5 gf5 40.Qf5 Qe3 41.Kf1 Bh2 Black wins a second pawn. Here with 38...Bd4 39.Kf1! Qa1 40.Kg2 Qg1 41.Kh3 Qf1 42.Qg2 White could still have retained hopes of saving the game. The following move leads to the loss of two further pawns.
39.f5
Matulović:
White opens the position at his own expense. By 39.Kf1 White could probably have continued his resistance, although Black would still have good winning chances. Now White loses two pawns, and that is the end of the game.
39...gf5 40.Qf5 Qe3 41.Kf1
Botvinnik:
Or 41.Kd1 Bc3 and mate is inevitable. The above-mentioned game Medina - Botvinnik concluded with precisely such a mate.
41...Qg1 42.Ke2 Qh2 43.Kd1 Qg1 44.Ke2 Qe3 45.Kf1 Qg3 46.Qh7 Kf8
White resigned.
0 : 1
[Botvinnik/Matulović]
Board 9
Mikhail Tal – Miguel Najdorf [B 48]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cd4 4.Nd4 e6
Tal:
The old love of the Argentinian grandmaster - the system with 2...d6 and 5...a6 - has been replaced with a new. In recent times Najdorf has played the Paulsen Variation often, enthusiastically and with success.
5.Nc3 Qc7 6.Be3 a6 7.Bd3 Nf6 8.0-0 Ne5
Tal:
The plan chosen by White is very popular. Now, in this position, 8...Nd4 9.Bd4 Bc5; and 8...b5 are also encountered.
Najdorf:
Up to this moment the game proceeds along well-known lines.
9.h3
Tal:
Deserving attention is 9.Be2, leaving, in a number of cases, the square h3 free for the queen, which could from there display noticable activity on the queen’s flank. The move 9.Be2 was tested in a series of games at Palma de Mallorca (1969), although the first conclusions have still to be drawn. For the time being White does not depart from the beaten path.
Najdorf:
In the game against Penrose (Palma de Mallorca, 1969), on 9.Be2 I played 9...b5 10.f4 Nc4 11.Bc4 Qc4 12.e5 Nd5 13.Nd5 Qd5 with good prospects for Black.
9...Bc5
Tal:
This continuation gained popularity after the 23rd game of the World Championship match, when Petrosian employed it against Spassky. In general, it seems, it has been studied by Leningrad chessplayers.
10.Kh1
Tal:
(?) Coming to the game, I intended to continue here 10.Na4 Ba7 11.c4!?, and the capture of the pawn is fatal: 11...Nc4 12.Bc4 Qc4 13.Rc1 Qb4 14.Ne6!. At the board, however, I changed my mind, and began to test a continuation rejected by theory, 10.f4?? Nc6 11.Nf5 Qb6 12.Nd6 Kf8 13.Bc5 Qc5 14.Kh1 Qd6 15.e5, and then decided that in the first round - which is, as a rule, an unlucky one for me - such a gambit would not do, and I instead played a meaningless move. Stronger is 10.Qe2, as Spassky played in the above-mentioned game.
RR: - Subsequently, the move 10.Kh1 came to be regarded as the main line here. - Griffin.
Najdorf:
If 10.f4, then 10...Nc6 would have been a good answer. The move in the text is a new one, as until now 10.Qe2, together with 11.Nb3, was usually played. The aim of the text move is quite clear: after a later f2-f4 the knight can now move from d4 and then the bishop at e3 will not be captured with check.
10...d6 11.f4 Ned7
Tal:
In reply to 11...Nc6 there could follow 12.Be2, as well as the sharper 12.e5!?
Najdorf:
11...Nc6 seems more natural, after which 12.e5! Bd4 (12...de5 13.Ne6! - cf. White’s 10th move) 13.Bd4 Nd4 14.ef6 gf6, and as compensation for the pawn White has good attacking chances, regardless of the fact that with 15.Ba6 he could regain the pawn. Also after 13...de5 14.fe5 Ne5 15.Ne4! White retains the better position. Against Tal I did not wish to attempt such a hazardous course.
12.Qf3
Tal:
This is also probably not the best. I did not reply 12.g4 on account of 12...h6. The position of the queen on the long light-squared diagonal provokes a natural reaction by Black.
12...b5
Tal:
(!)
Najdorf:
With no fear at all! 13.e5 would now be followed by 13...Bb7 14.ed6 Qb8! (not 14...Bd6 because of 15.Ndb5!) 15.Qf2 Bd6 and Black has a good position.
It should also be noted that White could not play 15.Qg3 with the intention of winning a pawn, as after 15...Bd6 16.Qg7? Rg8 Black would take at g2.
13.Nb3
Tal:
It was in a below-par mood that I played this move. White recognises that he has gained nothing from the active play on the king’s flank. Instead of this move I examined 13.e5 Bb7 (possibly, 13...de5 is also not bad) 14.Ne4 Ne4 15.Be4 Be4 16.Qe4 d5 with a comfortable game for Black. In reply to 13.Rad1 (with the idea, on 13...Bb7, of sacrificing a piece at b5), good is either 13...b4 or 13...Rb8 (as pointed out by Najdorf).
13...Bb7
Najdorf:
Owing to the fact that White’s queen has been posted at f3 (rather than e2), Black had no need to be apprehensive of the exchange at c5 followed by e4-e5.
14.a4 b4 15.Ne2
15...Be3
Tal:
Of course, one cannot speak of Black having an advantage, but every Sicilian player, when making the first move, could only dream of such a position. Now Black, in my opinion, ought to simply castle (15...0-0), not determining his plans for the time being and not ‘disarming’ White’s inactive pieces that are crowded together in the centre. At some point there would arise the possible breaks ...e6-e5 or ...d6-d5.
Najdorf:
In Tal’s opinion, 15...0-0 would have been slightly better. I intended to delay castling as long as possible, so as to “give my opponent the address of my king” at the last possible moment.
16.Qe3 Nc5
Tal:
Had White been forced to exchange at c5, Najdorf’s plan would have been fully justified. However...
17.Ng3
Tal:
...it becomes clear that there is no serious threat - a capture, both at b3 and at d3 - cedes the c-file, disadvantageously for Black.
17...Rc8
Tal:
Evidently, more accurate was 17...0-0.
18.Qe2
Tal:
(!) In some cases, the a6-pawn proves to be under attack, while the variation 18...Nd3 19.cd3 Qc2 20.Qc2 Rc2 21.Na5 and 22.Rfc1 secures White an advantage in the endgame.
Najdorf:
From this moment on Black must consider the possibility of e4-e5, as well as the defence of his pawn at a6.
18...0-0
Tal:
The sharp 18...h5 19.e5 h4 20.ef6 hg3! could have led to a position where the black king finds itself in greater danger than its White rival.
19.Nc5 Qc5
20.Rad1
Tal:
Nothing is given either by 20.Ba6 Ba6 21.Qa6 Qc2 or by 20.e5 de5 21.fe5 Nd7 22.Rae1 (still worse is 22.Bh7 Kh7 23.Qd3 Kg8 24.Qd7 Rc7) 22...Qd4. Perhaps it was all the same worth deciding on 20.e5.
Najdorf:
20.Ba6 would not be good because of 20...Ba6 21.Qa6 Qc2 22.Rac1? Qc1 and Black has the better position; if White plays 20.e5 then 20...de5 21.fe5 Nd7 may follow and after 22.Bh7 Kh7 23.Qd3 Kg8 24.Qd7 Rc7 White would have nothing. With the move in the text White prevents the knight from retreating to d7.
20...a5
Tal:
Only after this inaccuracy can one speak of any sort of serious advantage for White.
Najdorf:
(?) Weakly played. After 20...d5! 21.ed5 (if 21.e5 then 21...Ne4!) 21...Nd5 22.Ne4 Qe7 23.Qh5 h6 24.f5 ef5 25.Qf5 g6 Black can succesfully organise his defence.
21.e5
Tal (!)
21...de5 22.fe5 Nd5
Tal:
A difficult, but practically forced decision. Losing is 22...Nd7 23.Bh7 Kh7 24.Rd7.
Najdorf:
22...Nd7 would be followed by 23.Bh7 Kh7 24.Rd7 Bc6? 25.Qh5 followed by capturing at the f7-square.
23.Ne4
Tal:
White agrees to transposition to an endgame (it is the first round!).
23...Qe3
Najdorf:
Black must not remain passive, as White will open an attack.
24.Qh5
Tal:
In reply to 24.Qg4 there could follow 24...Qh6 25.Nf6 Kh8! 26.Nd5 Bd5 27.Rf4 f5 with unclear consequences. Now Black’s reply is forced - the attack threatens to become irresistible.
24...Qh6
Tal:
(!)
Najdorf:
White is now forced, because of the threat of 25...Ne3, to exchange queens. However, he retains the better position. It should be mentioned that 24...h6 would be weak because of 25.Rf3, as would 24...g6 because of 25.Qh4.
25.Qh6 gh6
Tal:
Black’s position is very unpleasant - the weaknesses at the squares d6 and f6 and the backward pawn at f7 determine a significant advantage for White. However, I was not able to find a clear-cut plan for the realisation of this advantage.
26.Rf3
Tal:
Also not bad was 26.Nf6 Nf6 27.Rf6; also not bad was 26.Nd6. It is quite possible that had I chosen one of these continuations and failed to win the game, then in the commentary it would have been noted: also not bad was 26.Rf3.
26...Rc7
Najdorf:
Probably 26...Bc6 would now be better, so as to limit the action of the knight, which will move to d6.
27.Rdf1
Najdorf:
With the strong threat of 27...-- 28.Rg3 Kh8 29.Nf6!, and after 29...Nf6 30.Rf6 Black would be helpless.
27...Ne7
Tal:
(!)
28.Rg3 Kh8
Najdorf:
28...Ng6 would not be good, because of 29.Nf6 Kh8 30.Nh7! etc.
29.Nd6
Tal:
To begin with, I intended 29.Nf6. After this, bad was 29...Nf5 30.Bf5 ef5 31.Rf5 Rc2 32.Rh5 Bc8 33.Rh6 Bf5 34.Rh5 Rc1 35.Kh2 Bc2 36.h4. On 29...Ng6 strong is 30.Nh7! Rg8 31.Bg6 fg6 32.Nf8! g5 33.Rf6. At the last moment I refrained from the move 29.Nf6, since I could not assess precisely the consequences of the move 29...Rd8 30.Nh7 Nf5, but obviously, this should have proved to be in White’s favour. In playing the move 29.Nd6 I was in a very optimistic mood - the f7-pawn is under attack, while on 29...Ng8 there follows the deadly move 30.Bc4! Bc8 (otherwise the e6-pawn is undefended) and after 31.b3 Black proves to be in an original zugzwang.
29...Nc6
Tal:
the only move. White does not have time to invade at f6 on account of the capture at e5.
Najdorf:
The only defence. 29...Ng6 would not be good because of 30.Bg6 hg6 31.Rg6 Rc2? 32.Nb7 etc. After 29...Ng8 30.Bc4! Black would almost be stalemated!
30.Re3
Najdorf:
For a while White must halt his attack to protect his pawn. However, 31.Rf6! is immediately threatened.
30...Kg7
Najdorf:
(!) Now 31.Rf6 would certainly be followed by 31...Ne5!.
Tal:
Again preventing 31.Rf6.
31.Rf4 Ne7
Tal:
The oldest participant in the match conducts a difficult defence in excellent fashion - the wandering knight is now ready to occupy an ideal position at the g6-square. Now at White’s disposition there was a little combination with the win of a pawn - 32.Rf7 Rf7 33.Ne8, but it seemed to me that after 33...Kf8 34.Nc7 Nd5 the activity of Black’s rook and bishop gives him considerable saving chances. However, it was probably necessary to play this way - after the continuation chosen in the game there is simply nothing left for White.
32.Bb5
Najdorf:
(?) White misses a chance of winning a pawn with 32.Rf7 Rf7 33.Ne8 Kh8 34.Nc7. He could continue with 34...Nd5 35.Nd5 Bd5 36.Kg1, but it is not quite certain that White would win this endgame. After the move played, however, White achieves much less.
32...Ng6 33.Rd4
Tal:
In reply to 33.Rf2 there could follow 33...Bc6, whereas now in reply to this move unpleasant is 34.Rc4.
33...Bd5
Tal:
(!) An absolutely correct exchange sacrifice - 34.Ne8 Re8 35.Be8 Rc2 leads to Black’s clear advantage.
34.c4
Najdorf:
With 34.Ne8 Re8! 35.Be8 Rc2 it might be said that Black has the better prospects.
34...bc3 35.bc3 Rc5
Najdorf:
(!) The threat is 36...Ba8, winning the e-pawn.
36.Ne8 Kh8 37.Nf6
Tal:
An amusing oversight. White sets the trap 37...Ne5?? 38.Re5 Bg2 39.Kg2 Re5 40.Nd7, overlooking that 38.Nd5 wins a piece immediately.
37...Rfc8 38.c4
Najdorf:
White has no choice, and must continue as follows.
38...Bc4
Tal:
Leading to a forced draw. 38...Bc6 39.Bc6 R5c6 40.Ree4 Rc5 also looks acceptable.
39.Ne4
Najdorf:
39.Nd7 might also have been played, likewise with a draw.
39...Bb5
Tal:
(!) Avoiding, for the last time, 39...Re5 40.Bc4! f5 41.Nd6!.
40.Nc5 Rc5 41.ab5
Najdorf:
The game was adjourned, but a draw was agreed without the game being continued.
½ :½
[Najdorf/Tal]
Playing Hall before the first round, Dom Sindikata Belgrade
Board 10
Borislav Ivkov – Paul Keres [C 75]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 d6
Ivkov:
Many games played recently have shown that Black finds it rather hard to equalise in the different variations of the Classical Closed System. Maybe that is the reason that Keres sometimes resorts to the Steinitz Defence Deferred; or perhaps it is due to the fact that it is slowly falling out of use, unlike the modern variations, the analysis of which may go as far as the 30th move.
5.0-0
Keres:
A modern continuation, which began to be employed after the fear of the 5...Bg4 6.h3 h5 had been permanently overcome.
5...Bd7 6.c3
Ivkov:
After 6.c4 Nf6 7.Nc3 Be7 8.d4 Nd4 9.Nd4 ed4 10.Bd7 Nd7 11.Qd4 Bf6 the position has been simplified too early and White’s advantage is hardly noticeable.
Keres:
In recent times the move 6.d4 has also been encountered often, in view of the fact that the pawn sacrifice 6...b5 7.Bb3 Nd4 8.Nd4 ed4 9.c3 gives White the possibility of developing a dangerous initiative. After the move in the text, Black has a great choice of continuations.
6...Nge7 7.d4 Ng6 8.Nbd2 Be7 9.Re1 0-0
Keres:
Another possibility was the continuation 9...h6 10.Nf1 Bg5 with the exchange of dark-squared bishops.
10.Nf1
10...ed4
Ivkov:
(!?) An interesting idea: Black abandons the centre of his own accord, reckoning on countering with an attack on White’s central pawns.
Keres:
In keeping with the subsequently-constructed plan 10...Nh4 was now more expedient.
11.cd4 Bf6
Ivkov:
Black had at his disposal the good move 11...Re8, with the idea of playing to gain a tempo.
Keres:
An illogical reply. The preliminary exchange in the centre could be justified only in connection with 11...Bg4. After 12.Bc6 bc6 13.Ne3 Bd7 Black would have had compensation, in the pair of bishops, for his restricted position.
12.Ne3 Re8 13.Bc2 Nh4
Keres:
In the given case, also possible is 13...Nb4 14.Bb1 c5, but I did not like the position after 15.d5 a5 16.a3 Na6 17.Nc4. With the text move, White retains a significant spatial advantage.
14.Nh4 Bh4 15.Nd5
15...Ne7
Ivkov:
Keres considered for quite some time the possibility of striking with 15...f5, but sensibly refrained. White would have two good replies: 16.Bf4, and 16.e5! with definite superiority.
Keres:
In connection with 15...f5 I did not like the eventual sacrifice of a pawn with 16.e5 de5 17.de5 Re5 18.Re5 Ne5 19.Bf4!, besides which, also not bad was 16.Bf4, which Ivkov suggested after the game.
16.Nc3
Ivkov:
Exchanging the second pair of knights would obviously not suit White. After 16.Nc3 it becomes clear that the white knight could have occupied that square in one move, instead of making a circle: Nd2-f1-e3-d5-c3. It is, of course, true that Black has made similar manoeuvres: ...Ne7-g6-h4, and has thereby withdrawn from the centre. All in all, the position is a normal one, with White holding an advantage.
16...Ng6
Keres:
The loss of a piece by 17.g3 Bf6 18.e5 was threatened.
17.g3 Bf6
18.Be3
Ivkov:
(?!) This would seem to be a logical developing move, but it is a mistake, costing White almost all of the advantage that he had. After 18.f4 White would have had a still greater superiority in the centre.
Keres:
After this move Black finally begins to breathe more freely. Clearly, stronger was 18.f4, after which for Black there hardly remained anything better than 18...c5 19.dc5 Bc3 20.bc3 dc5. All the same, despite the move in the text, White’s position is better.
18...c5
Keres:
(!) Sacrificing a pawn, Black to a significant extent frees himself from the pressure. The acceptance of the sacrifice, 19.dc5, after 19...Ne5! with the threats of 20...Bg4 and 20...Nc4, is fairly unpleasant for White.
19.f4
Ivkov:
Because of the weakness of the f3-square it was impossible to play 19.dc5 Ne5 20.f4 Bg4 21.Qd6 Nf3 22.Kf2 Ne1 23.Qd8 Rad8 24.Re1 Bc3!, and White stands no better.
19...cd4 20.Bd4 Bc6
Ivkov:
Maybe it would have been better to play 20...Nf8 right away.
Keres:
Judging from everything, more accurate was the immediate 20...Nf8. White threatened 21.Bf6 Qf6 22.e5 etc.
21.Bb3 Nf8
Keres:
Threatening, with 22...Bd4 23.Qd4 Ne6 and ...Nc5, to obtain counter-pressure against the e4-pawn.
22.Bf6 Qf6 23.Nd5
23...Qd8
Ivkov:
After 23...Qb2 24.Re2 Qa3 25.Nc7 Qc5 26.Rf2 Black would not have had adequate compensation for the exchange.
Keres:
The exchange sacrifice 23...Qb2 24.Re2 Qa3 25.Nc7 Qc5 (25...Bb5 26.Rc2) 26.Rf2! is not correct.
24.Qh5
Keres:
White has a spatial advantage, but his weakened pawn structure gives Black reasonable counter-chances.
The attack begun with the move in the text is parried by Black without difficulty.
24...Nd7
Ivkov:
(!)
Keres:
On the possible 24...Ne6, the reply 25.f5! would have been very unpleasant.
25.Rad1 Nc5
Keres:
The continuation 25...Nf6, after 26.Nf6 Qf6 27.e5 de5 28.fe5 Qg6 29.Qg6 hg6 30.e6!, led to an insignificant advantage for White.
26.Bc2 g6 27.Qg5
Ivkov:
White is forced to exchange queens, though at any rate he is still better off in the endgame.
Keres:
After 27.Qh6 White is threatened with material loss after 27...Bd5 28.Rd5 Qb6, and this same manoeuvre would have been employed on 27.Qf3. Both opponents were already experiencing a shortage of time.
27...Qg5 28.fg5 Bd5 29.Rd5 Re6
Keres:
On 29...Rad8 the reply 30.e5 would have been very unpleasant.
30.b4 Nd7 31.Bb3 Ne5
Keres:
If 31...Rae8, then 32.Rd4 Ne5 33.Be6 Nf3 34.Kg2 Nd4 35.Bd5 with the better game for White.
32.Rc1
Ivkov:
(?) We each had only 7-8 minutes before the time control, which was the reason I did not play the intended 32.Kg2!.
In that case Black would have had to solve some rather difficult problems, which would not have been so easy in the time scramble. For instance, after 32.Kg2 one of the possibilities was: 32...Nc6 33.Rdd1 Re7 34.Rd6 Nb4 35.e5 Rae8 36.e6 fe6 37.Rde6 Re6 38.Re6 Re6 39.Be6 and the superiority of the white bishop over the knight, as well as the quick movement of White’s king to the centre, leaves no doubt about whose position is better.
32...Nc6 33.b5 ab5
Keres:
Still simpler was 33...Na5.
34.Rb5 Re7 35.Rd1 Ra5
Ivkov:
(!) After this there are no longer any dangers for Black.
36.Ra5
Keres:
On 36.a4 sufficient would have been 36...Rb5 37.ab5 Na7 with a level game.
36...Na5 37.Bd5 Rc7
Ivkov:
(!)
38.Rb1 Kf8 39.Rb5 Nc6 40.Rb6 Ke7
Agreed drawn.
½ : ½
[Ivkov/Keres]
ROUND 2
Tuesday 31th March, 1970
The Soviet team gained a decisive victory in Round 2, to open up a three-point lead over their opponents.
The highlight of the round was Spassky’s spectacular victory on top board. He punished Larsen for some highly provocative opening play, and the Danish grandmaster was forced to resign after only 17 moves.
On the second board, Fischer won in fine positional style against Petrosian. On the black side of a symmetrical English opening, he introduced a plan of blockading White’s passed but isolated pawn (...Nc6-a5, ...Bb7-c6 and ...Na5-b7) that subsequently became standard in this set-up. Petrosian soon had to acquiesce in the loss of the pawn, and while he retained drawing chances, ultimately the strain of defending the position proved too much and Fischer scored his second successive victory.
On third board Portisch, on the white side of a Nimzo Indian Defence, gained another highly promising position against Viktor Korchnoi and missed a clear win at the 27th move. With both players in time trouble, a draw was agreed four moves later.
In Polugaevsky v. Hort, the Czechoslovak grandmaster chose an offbeat opening set-up and White soon gained a clear positional advantage. However, White went wrong at the 25th move, and in typically resourceful fashion Hort managed to stay in the game by means of a pawn sacrifice that gave him good practical chances on account of having the safer king in a complicated middlegame position. With both players in time trouble, a draw was agreed at the 30th move.
The Gligorić v. Geller, as in so many of the previous encounters between these players, saw a King’s Indian Defence. Neither player made any particular headway and a draw was agreed before the time control.
Smyslov won a very fine game with the white pieces against his American opponent on board 6. Reshevsky obtained a somewhat cramped game from the opening, and after he ceded important squares with 17...e5?, he was soon reduced to passive defence. Though the game lasted 63 moves, the victory of the ex-World Champion was never in any real doubt.
On the seventh board Taimanov scored a second successive victory over Uhlmann. The East German grandmaster employed his patented system against the Queen’s Indian Defence, involving a pawn sacrifice. Taimanov declined the offer, and gained easy equality. In the resultant middlegame he gradually took full control of the position, and decided the game with a direct attack on the white king.
On the next board Matulović equalised effortlessly on the black side of a Maróczy Bind against Botvinnik and even stood somewhat better in a position where, somewhat inexplicably, he did not even try to exploit his advantage.
On board 9, Miguel Najdorf scored a very fine win with the white pieces against Tal after the Latvian turned down a draw offer in the early middlegame arising from a Semi-Tarrasch Defence. White immediately offered a pawn and gained a strong attack in return. The game was adjourned in a position where there was a forced win for White, and on the resumption Najdorf made no mistake.
On the last board, Keres and Ivkov played what was perhaps the most complicated and difficult game in the event. On the black side of a Spanish Game, Ivkov obtained a difficult position and saw his pawn structure badly compromised. A further mistake left him with little alternative but to sacrifice a piece in an attempt to stay in the game. At several points, Keres failed to choose the best continuation, and the game was adjourned in an endgame where White has two knights against a bishop, with Black having two extra pawns and good practical drawing chances. In the highly intricate play, Keres eventually found a way to win to give his team victory in the match by the score 6 : 4.
Board 1
Bent Larsen – Boris Spassky [A 01]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.b3
Spassky:
Larsen willingly employs this incorrect opening. He aims to transfer the weight of the struggle from the opening to the middlegame, in passing displacing the opponent from the usual opening lines.
1...e5 2.Bb2 Nc6
Larsen:
In the twenty-odd games that I have opened with 1.b3, until now nobody has played this way.
3.c4 Nf6 4.Nf3
Larsen:
After 4.g3 d5 Black would have a good position.
4...e4 5.Nd4 Bc5
Spassky:
The shortcoming of White’s opening set-up is that Black can develop his forces without hindrance, at the same time consolidating his position in the centre.
6.Nc6
Larsen:
6.e3? would have been followed by 6...Bd4 7.ed4 d5, with advantage for Black.
6...dc6
Larsen:
The position has now become Nimzowitsch’s variation of the Sicilian Defence with colours reversed:
(to the line)
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nf6 3.e5 Nd5 4.Nc3 Nc3 5.dc3. In this position Nimzowitsch, in his time, played 5...b6?, which was later displaced by 6.e6!. White has an extra tempo in comparison with the above position, and besides it is not quite certain that the bishop’s position at c5 is the best one.
7.e3 Bf5 8.Qc2 Qe7 9.Be2
Larsen:
This move is weak as it closes the square e2 to the knight in certain variations.
9...0-0-0
Spassky:
Black completes the mobilisation of his forces. He has reinforced the e4-square and now prepares an offensive on the king’s flank, simultaneously intending pressure, along the d-file, against White’s backward pawn.
10.f4
Larsen:
(??) A very weak move that loses the game. Apart from the usual line of development, another possibility was 10.a3.
Spassky:
(?) A mistake, after which it is already hardly possible to save White’s position? How can it be explained? It seems to me that Larsen associated this move with the idea 11.Bf6 Qf6 12.Nc3. However, White’s plan meets with a refutation.
10...Ng4
Larsen:
White has no adequate defence. Had White decided upon 11.0-0, it would have been followed by 11...Qh4.
RR: 11...Rd2! is immediately decisive: 12.Nd2 Ne3 13.Qc1 Nf1 14.Kf1 Qh4 etc. 12.h3 h5 with an attack, while 11.Bg4 would have been followed by 11...Qh4 - Griffin.
Spassky:
(!) This simple move puts White in a critical position.
11.g3 h5
Spassky:
Very tempting appears the rook sacrifice 11...Rd2. After 12.Nd2 Ne3 13.Qc3 Rd8 Black has a formidable attack. However, to demonstrate a win for Black is very far from simple. Therefore I preferred a more solid path.
12.h3
Spassky:
It is difficult to advise White of anything. Thus, on 12.Nc3 there could follow 12...Rd2! with decisive effect.
12...h4
Spassky:
(!) Sacrificing a piece, Black opens the position of the white king.
13.hg4
Larsen:
After 13.Bg4 Bg4 14.hg4 hg3 15.Rg1, 15...Rh2 is again a winning move.
Spassky:
Larsen thought for almost an hour over his move. But there is no salvation. On 13.Bg4 there could follow 13...Bg4 14.hg4 hg3 15.Rg1 Rh1! 16.Rh1 g2 17.Rg1 Qh4 18.Ke2 Qg4 19.Ke1 Qg3 20.Ke2 (20.Kd1 Qf2 21.Qe4 Qg1 22.Kc2 Qf2 and wins) 20...Qf3 21.Ke1 Be7 and mate is inevitable.
13...hg3 14.Rg1
14...Rh1
Larsen:
(!) White had placed all his hopes on 14...Qh4, which would have been followed by 15.Rg2 Qh1 16.Bf1 Bg4 17.Qe4 Rhe8 18.Be5 f6 19.Nc3 and White might try to resist.
15.Rh1 g2 16.Rf1
Larsen:
If White plays 16.Rg1 then 16...Qh4 17.Kd1 Qh1 etc. would follow.
Spassky:
The move 16.Rg1 was no better. After 16...Qh4 17.Kd1 Qh1 18.Qc3 Qg1 19.Kc2 Qf2 20.gf5 Qe2 21.Na3 winning most simply of all is 21...Bb4. During the game I was attracted by another possibility, that is 21...Qd3 22.Qd3 ed3 23.Kc3 Be3 (23...a5 24.Nc2!) 24.de3 d2 25.Rd1 Rh8!, and Black promotes one of his pawns to a queen.
16...Qh4 17.Kd1 17...gf1=Q
White resigned.
0 : 1
Spassky:
After 18.Bf1 Bg4 mate is inevitable.
In the commentary to the game I have made use of an idea of grandmaster D. Bronstein and indicated the time spent in thought over each move. It seems to me that chronometry often makes it clear, over what the chess player was thinking; what were his doubts and hesitations. In a word, chess fans obtain the possibility to penetrate, to some extent, into the internal secrets of the laboratory of the chess master.
[Larsen/Spassky]
Board 2
Tigran Petrosian – Robert Fischer [A 37]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.c4 g6 2.Nc3 c5
Fischer:
(!) I knew that Petrosian had to play to win, so I chose the safest follow-up.
3.g3
Fischer:
In the case of 3.Nf3 Bg7 4.d4 cd4 5.Nd4 Nc6 is no problem for Black.
3...Bg7 4.Bg2 Nc6 5.Nf3
Fischer:
5.e3 is better but after 5...e6 it is almost a drawing variation.
5...e6
Fischer:
(!) Now it is Black who is playing for the centre space.
6.0-0 Nge7
7.d3
Petrosian:
If desired, it was possible to play 7.e3 followed by d2-d4, which, however, after the natural retort by Black 7...d5, would have led to a symmetrical pawn configuration. All this has already been met with in tournament practice and as a rule represents one of the ways for the opponents to tacitly agree to a draw. After defeat in the first game, I was naturally aggressively inclined, although already here I was aware that with the disposition of forces intended by Black, the stereotyped plan of plan on the queen’s flank promised nothing good.
How often even experienced chessplayers forget that after a loss, a draw is a much more pleasant result than a second defeat. At the present moment I too forgot this.
Fischer:
Even more active is 7.e3 0-0 8.d4 cd4 9.Nd4 Nd4 10.ed4 d5 11.cd5 Nd5 12.Qb3 Qb6! as in the second match between Botvinnik and Smyslov in 1957, a game which ended in a draw. Although Reshevsky tried to win this endgame against Robert Byrne, it ought to be an easy draw for Black if he plays correctly.
RR: Here Fischer is referring to the 19th game of the Botvinnik - Smyslov match, and the game Reshevsky - R. Byrne (US Championship, 1965). - Griffin.
7...0-0 8.Bd2 d5 9.a3 b6 10.Rb1 Bb7
Fischer:
I played the same position with opposite colours against Benko with the other side to move, and since I tried the same plan that Petrosian tried here, I knew that it would not succeed.
RR: It may be assumed that the game referred to here is Benkő - Fischer (US Championship, New York 1958).- Griffin.
11.b4
Fischer:
(?) 11.cd5 Nd5 12.Nd5 Qd5 was perhaps better, but it is difficult to say whether White equalises.
11...cb4 12.ab4 dc4 13.dc4 Rc8
Petrosian:
White already feels uncomfortable. The weaknesss of the c-pawn is unpleasant. Probably best of all was 14.Nb5, intending to play c4-c5, and in the case of 14...a6, to retreat the knight to a3, agreeing to a slightly worse position.
Fischer:
Now Black has a clear plan as to how to proceed against White’s ‘hanging’ pawns on the queen-side.
14.c5
Fischer:
In the case of 14.Qa4 Nd4! Black has the initiative, and after 14.Ne4 Black has an excellent answer in 14...Na5!. On other moves Black might play 14...a5!.
14...bc5 15.bc5 Na5
Fischer:
(!)
16.Na4
RR: Subsequent practice showed that the best continuation for White is 16.Nb5!, and on 16...Rc5 17.Qa4 with compensation for the sacrificed pawn.- Griffin.
16...Bc6
Petrosian:
(!) Black begins a siege of the c5 pawn. White’s misfortune consists in the fact that it is not so simple to find an antidote against this. Black has no weaknesses, and therefore it is necessary to seek tactical tricks, in order to somehow avoid immediate material loss.
Fischer:
(!) Now White’s c-pawn is in danger.
17.Qc2
Petrosian:
The first attack is repulsed. Impossible is 17...Ba4 18.Qa4 Rc5 19.Bb4, and the black rook cannot find a stable place to stop on the fifth rank.
17...Nb7 18.Rfc1 Qd7 19.Ne1
Petrosian:
It may readily be noted that, as previously, Black is promised nothing by the direct pursuit of the c5-pawn, in view of the fact that both the pawn at a7 and the knight at b7 prove to be under attack. Fischer continues to strengthen the pressure.
Fischer:
A clever move. After 19...Ba4 White moves into a drawing position with 20.Rb7.
19...Nd5 20.Nb2 Bb5 21.Ned3
Petrosian:
21.Nbd3 was not much better, on account of the same reply 21...Bd4.
Fischer:
Someone suggested 21.Nbd3 as a better move, but I simply wanted to reinforce my position with 21...a6 22.Qb3 Nd8 and 23...Nc6 with pressure.
21...Bd4
Petrosian:
Now the fall of the c5-pawn is inevitable and for White there remains only the possibility of selling it ‘dearly’.
Fischer:
Now the game enters a new phase. Black will win a pawn.
22.Qb3 Nc5 23.Nc5 Rc5 24.Rc5 Bc5 25.Nd3
Fischer:
White could not try 25.Bd5? Qd5 26.Qb5? Bf2!.
25...Bd3 26.Qd3
Petrosian:
White has lost a pawn, but has definite consolation in the form of the two bishops. He ought to have adhered to temporising tactics, waiting for active operations by the opponent.
26...Rd8 27.Bf3 Qc7 28.Bg5
Petrosian:
Stronger was 28.Be1. Surprisingly, White begins to simplify the position.
28...Be7 29.Be7 Qe7 30.Qd4
Petrosian:
(??) A terrible move. Any of the continuations 30.Ra1, 30.Qa6 or 30.Bd5 was better. After the obvious reply by Black, it becomes clear that White has a ‘bad’ bishop.
Fischer:
White could simplify with 30.Bd5 Rd5, but it would leave White without any counter-play.
30...e5
Petrosian:
(!)
Fischer:
(!) It is hard for Black to win with an extra pawn on the edge of the board, so he tries to build up chances on the other side as well.
31.Qc4 Nb6 32.Qc2 Rc8 33.Qd3 Rc4
Fischer:
(!) Threatening 34...e4, and 34.Rb6? does not work because of the intermediate 34...Rc1.
34.Bg2 Qc7
Fischer:
34...Qc5! was more active and better.
35.Qa3 Rc3 36.Qa5 Rc5 37.Qa3
Fischer:
37.Qa6! Rc1 38.Rc1 Qc1 39.Bf1 Qc7 40.Qa5 was better, with a long fight ahead.
37...a5 38.h4 Nc4 39.Qd3 Nd6 40.Kh2
Fischer:
With this move White avoids the exchange of rooks on the 1st rank.
40...Kg7
Petrosian:
The time control has been reached. The nightmare for White has come to an end. Looking at the previous 10-12 moves, somewhow I cannot believe that it was I who played them. White’s position is hopeless. The black pieces have taken up invulnerable positions and White’s bishop plays the role of an extra. The a-pawn is ready to move forward.
Fischer:
(?) An unecessary preventive move. 40...h5 was better, not allowing 41.h5, but White misses this opportunity.
41.Rd1
Fischer:
(?) The sealed move. After 41.h5 I did not see a clear winning plan, as White still has counter-chances on the king-side. Black cannot play 41...gh5 because of 42.Rd1! and 43.Qf5; 41...a4 would have been followed by 42.Qa3!.
41...Ne8 42.Qd7
Petrosian:
White has to try his luck in an endgame. If he tries to retain as many pieces as possible, then he can easily come under attack. For example: 42.Qa6 Nf6 43.Ba8 (so that the bishop should not be blocked in after ...e5-e4) 43...a4! 44.Qa4 Ra5 45.Qc6 Qa7 and Black wins.
Fischer:
White could not wait until Black consolidated his position on the king-side with 42...Nf6.
42...Qd7 43.Rd7 Nf6
Petrosian:
(?) Black retained a great advantage after 43...Nc7, after which there was no way for the white rook to occupy a position behind the passed pawn.
Fischer:
Black should have played simply 43...Nc7 with the advance of the a-pawn and an easy win. If 44.Be4 a4 45.Bb1 Nb5 46.Ba2 Rc7 etc.
44.Ra7 Ng4 45.Kg1 Rc1 46.Bf1 Ra1 47.e4
Petrosian:
(!) It transpires that everything is not yet so simple. In some cases an attack on the f7-pawn is possible.
Fischer:
(!) After 47.e3 Black would win easily with 47...e4 and 48...Ne5.
47...a4 48.Kg2 Ra2
49.Rf7
Petrosian:
Without detailed analysis, one cannot express an opinion on this move. But there was another move - a more promising chance in practice - 49.Kg1. After all, the f2-pawn is essentially inviolable: 49...Rf2 50.Bh3 Rf3 51.Kg2, or 49...Nf2 50.Bc4 Nh3 51.Kh1 Rf2 52.Be6!. On 49...h5 there follows 50.Bh3 with the same idea: 50...Nf2 51.Be6. In the case of the exchange at g4 the rook endgame promises White good drawing chances.
But how is one to know where the greatest practical chances lie?
Fischer:
(?) An attractive move, but after the game Petrosian suggested a much better one - 49.Kg1!. 49...Rf2 does not work because of 50.Bh3 Rf3 51.Kg2 with a draw, and after 49...Nf2 50.Bc4! Nh3 51.Kh1 Rf2 52.Be6! is also uncertain for Black, so the only way is 49...a3 50.Bh3! h5 51.Bg4 hg4 but the endgame is probably a draw. Also interesting is 49.Kf3!?.
49...Kf7 50.Bc4 Ke7 51.Ba2 a3
Fischer:
The endgame is lost for White, since his pawn structure on the king-side is weak.
52.Kf3 Nf6 53.Ke3 Kd6 54.f4
Fischer:
54.Kd3 Kc5 55.f3 Nh5 is hopeless too, or 55.Be6 Ng4!, forcibly weakening the king-side, and if 56.f4 Kd6 and 57...Nf6, or 56.Ke2 Kd4.
54...Nd7!
Fischer:
Black wants to win a piece. The rest of the game is clear.
55.Bb1
Petrosian:
(??) It was possible to fight for a draw by manoeuvring with the bishop on the long diagonal a2-g8. White’s defensive idea consists in having, at the moment when Black plays ...Nc5, the move Bc4, and on ...Na6 - Kd2. The move in the game, however, is equivalent to capitulation.
55...Nc5 56.f5 Na6 57.g4 Nb4 58.fg6 hg6 59.h5 gh5 60.gh5 Ke6 61.Kd2 Kf6 62.Kc3 a2 63.Ba2 Na2 64.Kb2
Fischer:
64.Kc4 would have been followed by 64...Nc1 65.Kd5 Nd3.
64...Nb4 65.Kc3 Nc6 66.Kc4 Nd4
White resigned.
0 : 1
Fischer:
67.h6 would have been followed by 67...Nf3.
[Fischer/Petrosian]
Board 3
Lajos Portisch – Viktor Korchnoi [E 55]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 e6 3.Nc3 Bb4 4.e3 0-0 5.Bd3 d5 6.Nf3 c5 7.0-0 dc4 8.Bc4 Nbd7 9.Qb3
Portisch:
(!?) In comparison with the usual 9.Qc2 this move holds an element of surprise, which was confirmed by the length of time Korchnoi used to consider the next move.
Korchnoi:
(!?) A rarely-encountered continuation. Theory maintains that the strongest is 9.Qc2, but the move in the text has its pluses.
9...a6
Korchnoi:
Black aims at a complicated struggle, but he does not succeed in equalising. Theory advises 9...cd4 10.ed4 Bc3 11.bc3 b6, or 10.Qb4 dc3 11.Qc3 b6 - possessing the two bishops, White stands more actively, but with accurate play Black can equalise the chances.
10.a4
10...Ba5
Portisch:
(!?) Theory suggests at this point 10...Nb6.
Korchnoi:
Deserving attention is another plan: 10...Qe7 11.Rd1 Rd8 and then perhaps 12...Nf8. However, in this case too Black does not have an easy game.
RR: Interestingly, at the end of 1970 the game Tukmakov - Korchnoi (from the 38th USSR Championship, in Riga) continued 10...Qe7 11.Rd1 Ba5 12.Qc2 cd4 13.ed4 Nb6 14.Ba2 h6 15.Ne5 Bd7 16.Bb1 Rfd8 17.Rd3 and White developed a strong attack. - Griffin.
11.Rd1 cd4 12.ed4 Qb6
Portisch:
(!) After 12...Nb6?! 13.Ne5! Black would have had difficulty extricating himself.
Korchnoi:
On 12...Nb6 White could reply 13.Bg5 with an obvious advantage. Therefore, he has to play some rather unnatural moves.
13.Qc2
Korchnoi:
The exchange of queens would have eased Black’s defence.
13...Qc7 14.Bd3
Korchnoi:
Not 14.Qe2 in view of 14...Bc3 15.bc3 Nb6 16.Bd3 Nbd5! and Black, threatening 17...Nc3 or 17...Nf4, satisfactory resolves the problems of his position.
14...h6
Portisch:
(?!) This is a dangerous weakening. The right move would be 14...b6!, after which the sacrifice of a pawn with 15.Qe2!? Bc3 16.bc3 Qc3 would be doubtful. Instead 15.d5!? followed by 15...ed5 16.Nd5 would give White the advantage, but with 15...Bc3 16.de6 Be5 (16...Ne5 17.ef7 with an unclear follow-up) 17.ed7 Bd7 would give Black an equal position.
RR: Here 18.Bh7 Nh7 19.Qc7 Bc7 20.Rd7 would seem to be strong. - Griffin.
Korchnoi:
A questionable move. The weakening of the king’s flank later proves to be perceptible. Better would have been 14...b6. Admittedly, in this case I was worried by the fate of the bishop at a5, which due to the threat of b2-b4 could prove to be in a desperate position...
15.Bd2 Bb4
Portisch:
It would still be correct to play 15...b6!?, after which I intended to play 16.Rac1 Bb4 17.Qb3, and Black would have had an extra, useful, tempo in comparison with the way the game developed.
Korchnoi:
For White there had already arisen the serious threat of 16.Qc1 followed by the sacrifice of the bishop at h6. Therefore, Black intends to return with the bishop for the defence of the king’s flank. (16.Qc1 Re8!)
16.Qb3
Portisch:
(!) This was better than 16.Rac1, which could have been followed by 16...Qa5.
16...Bd6
Korchnoi: 16...Qa5?! would have been followed by 17.Bf4! which, together with Nf3-e5, would have been unpleasant for Black.
17.Rac1 Qd8 18.Bb1 b6
Portisch:
This is a critical position, where White has two plans with promising chances. The first one can be seen in the later development of the game; the other begins with 19.Ne4!? with the following possibilities: 19...Ne4 20.Be4 Ra7! 21.Ne5 Be5 22.de5 Nc5 23.Qe3 Ne4 (23...Rd7 would be weaker because of 24.Bc2, with a clear advantage for White) 24.Qe4 Qd5! 25.Qd5 ed5 and although with 26.Be3 or 26.Bb4 White could win a pawn, he most probably would be unable to win because of the bishops of opposite colour.
19.Ne5
Korchnoi:
(!) The signal to attack! White’s forces are now co-ordinated for the storming of the opponent’s king’s flank. It is unfavourable to take the knight: 19...Ne5 20.de5 Be5 21.Bh6 followed by 22.Be3 with advantage. Also good was 19.Ne4.
19...Bb7
Portisch:
19...Ne5?! would not be a good move because of 20.de5 Be5 21.Bh6 with complications that are to White’s advantage.
20.Bf4 Qe7 21.Re1
Korchnoi:
Threatening 22.Ng6! fg6 23.Re6!
21...Rfe8
Portisch:
(!) A very important defensive move, since in some variations Ne5-g6!? is threatened.
22.Qc2 Rac8
Korchnoi:
It was possible to defend against the immediate threat to the black king with 22...Nf8, but White could then continue the attack without hurrying - 23.Qd2, then Re1-e3-g3 etc. Therefore Black leaves the square f8 free for the possible flight of his king.
23.Nd7
Portisch:
After 23.Qd3 Nf8 I could not see a definite continuation of White’s attack.
23...Qd7
Portisch:
Black could also have played 23...Bf4!? and after 24.Nf6 Qf6 25.Qh7 Kf8 26.Nd5!? Bd5 27.Rc8 Rc8 28.Qh8 Ke7 29.Qc8 Qd4 it is doubtful whether White could win.
Korchnoi:
(?) Weak! After 23...Bf4! 24.Nf6 Qf6 25.Qh7 Kf8 26.Nd5! Bd5 27.Rc8 Rc8 28.Qh8 Ke7 29.Qc8 Qd4 Black does not losing in view of the centralised position of his pieces. Or 25.Nd5 Bh2 26.Kh1 Bd5 27.Qc8 Kf8 with a complicated struggle.
24.Be5 Qd8
Korchnoi:
(?) This should have led to a rapid loss.
More tenacious was 24...Be7 25.d5! Rc3.
25.Bf6 Qf6 26.Qh7 Kf8
27.d5
Portisch:
(?) Now, 27.Nd5? would be a weak move because of 27...Bd5 28.Rc8 Rc8 29.Qh8 Ke7 30.Qc8 Qf4! and Black would have the better prospects, but 27.Be4! Be4 28.Ne4 would lead directly to victory, as 28...Rc1 would be followed by 29.Qh8 Ke7 30.Qe8! with an extra exchange for White.
Korchnoi:
(?) Deciding immediately is 27.Be4! Be4 28.Ne4 Rc1 (what else is there to be done?) 29.Qh8 Ke7 30.Qe8 Ke8 31.Nf6 etc.
27...Ke7
Korchnoi:
Here too 27...Rc3 deserved attention.
28.Rcd1
Portisch:
If 28.de6 fe6 29.Be4! Be4 (not 29...Rh8? as White would have the advantage after 30.Bb7 Rh7 31.Nd5 Kf7 32.Nf6 Rc1 33.Rc1 Kf6 34.Rc6!) 30.Qe4 Kf8 31.Qb7 then Black, with 31...Qf4! 32.g3 Qb4! can maintain his position within the margins of a draw.
Korchnoi:
(?) White begins to play very uncertainly and lets slip the advantage. Good was 28.de6 fe6 29.Be4, and also 28.Ba2.
28...Rh8 29.Qd3 Rhd8
Portisch:
(!) It is interesting that Korchnoi finds the best replies even when pressed for time.
30.Qe2 Bc5 31.Ba2
Portisch:
Another possibility was 31.de6!? but after 31...Rd1 32.ef7 Kf7 the pair of bishops would compensate Black for the lost pawn.
31...Kf8
Portisch:
The opponents agreed to a draw, at Black’s suggestion. White no longer has the better position, as Black could answer 32.Ne4 with 32...Qe5.
For the remaining moves before the time control Korchnoi has only one minute and I only two, and in the event of play continuing, anything could have happened.
Therefore, in my opinion a draw was quite correct.
Korchnoi:
Black has defended his king while maintaining material equality. In circumstances of serious mutual time trouble, the opponents agreed to a draw.
½ : ½
[Korschnoi/Portisch]

Lev Polugaevsky – Vlastimil Hort [A 28]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.c4 e5 2.Nc3 Nc6 3.Nf3
Polugaevsky:
Another method of development is 3.g3 g6 4.Bg2 Bg7 5.Rb1 with a complicated struggle.
3...Nf6 4.e3
Polugaevsky:
White could follow a different path: 4.d4 ed4 5.Nd4 Bb4 6.Bg5, which was often encountered in the practice of Mikhail Botvinnik.
4...d6
Polugaevsky:
Black turns along King’s Indian lines. More reliable was 4...Be7 5.d4 ed4 6.Nd4 d5.
Hort:
4...Bb4, which might have been better, could also have been played. Black’s whole plan is doubtful.
5.d4 Bg4
Polugaevsky:
An original, but perhaps the best, course in the given situation.
6.d5 Nb8 7.e4 Be7 8.h3 Bh5
Polugaevsky:
(!) Only with this method does Black have counter-chances. In the case of 8...Bf3 9.Qf3 White’s advantage is undisputed.

9.Be3 a5 10.Bd3

White could play 10.Be2, but then he would have had to reckon with 10...Bf3 11.Bf3 Nfd7 followed by 12...Bg5.
10...Na6 11.g4 Bg6 12.Qe2

White plays in strictly positional style and does not attempt the possibility of 12.c5, with unclear consequences.
12...Nd7 13.h4 h5 14.g5 Ndc5 15.Bc2 c6

There has arisen a complicated position that is favourable for White, who possesses greater space.
16.a3 0-0 17.Nd2

17...cd5 18.cd5
Hort:
Weaker would be 18.Nd5 Nc7, after which Black gains a stronghold in the centre.
18...Rc8
Hort:
At this moment it might have been better for Black to play 18...Qb8 followed by ...Rc8. However, the position is very complex and it is not easy to weigh up the chances.
19.Rb1
Polugaevsky:
(!) A strong move, forcing Black to reckon the whole time with the threat of b2-b4. In search of counter-play, Black takes the correct decision to undermine White’s pawn chain on the king’s flank.
Hort:
Oddly enough, White’s move does not threaten 20.b4, to which Black would have the good answer 20...Ne6!. However, the move eliminates the possibility of Black playing 19...Qd7.
19...f6
Polugaevsky:
(!)
20.Rg1 Kh7
Hort:

21.Rg3
Polugaevsky:

Hort:
This seems much too artificial. White already had a much better position and could make the necessary manoeuvre in this position, 21.Nf1-g3-f5, retaining the advantage.
21...Qe8
Hort:
Time pressure, and the complications have just started. Black threatens 22...a4.
22.Nc4 b5
Polugaevsky:

23.gf6
Polugaevsky:

Hort:

23...bc4
Hort:
What else?
24.fe7 Qe7
25.Rg5
Polugaevsky:

Hort:

25...Nd3
Polugaevsky:
(!) Black sacrifices a pawn, gaining a strong initiative.
Hort:

26.Bd3 cd3 27.Qd3 Nc5 28.Bc5 Rc5

(!)
30.b4

In this position the opponents agreed to a draw. For the pawn Black has strong pressure. An interesting struggle lay ahead. But experiencing a shortage of time for thought, the opponents decided not to tempt fate.

Board 5
Svetozar Gligorić – Efim Geller [E 92]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
Geller:
In my meetings with Gligorić the King’s Indian Defence is a frequent guest. And this is natural, since we are both ‘incorrigible’ King’s Indian supporters. This is perhaps why the player with White has found it difficult to achieve anything significant, since it has long been known that the most difficult thing is to fight against oneself. In the present game too the opening turned out be ineffective for Gligorić, and I succeeded in seizing the initiative. But subsequently, Gligorić sensed the danger in good time, and this is also understandable; the methods of conducting an attack for Black are well known to him. Not chasing after the ‘ghosts’ of an ephemeral offensive on the queen’s flank, he nearly nipped in the bud Black’s offensive on the king’s. And a drawn result became natural.
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 Bg7 4.e4 d6 5.Nf3 0-0 6.Be2 e5 7.d5 a5
Geller:
(!)
8.Be3
Gligorić:
At this point it is usual to play 8.Bg5 or 8.0-0. As ...a7-a5 has already been played, White tries to continue along lines similar to those arising from 7.Be3.
Geller:
This is a ‘provocative’ move, and it essentially represents a loss of time.
8...Ng4
Gligorić:
A reasonable answer. Otherwise White could redeploy his pieces, starting with 9.Nd2.
9.Bg5 f6 10.Bh4 Na6 11.Nd2
Geller:
Here the knight does not have prospects and therefore White ought to have first castled, and then carried out the manoeuvre Nf3-e1-d3.
11...Nh6
RR: Later, in a game v. Petrosian at the Chigorin Memorial tournament (Sochi, 1977) Geller was to prefer the alternative 11...h5. - Griffin.
12.f3
Gligorić:
The bishop at h4 is not very well posted, and is there only temporarily, to prevent ...f6-f5 or to weaken the square f5 after ...g6-g5. The text move allows the bishop to come back towards the queen’s wing, and sets up a natural pawn chain in the centre.
12...Bd7
Gligorić:
It is characteristic of Black’s tactics that he is not in a hurry to put his knight on c5, as White would then have been able to more quickly bring pressure to bear by means of b2-b3-b4.
13.0-0 Nf7 14.Nb3
Gligorić:
The knight at d2 is in a passive position and may come under attack from the bishop at h6; something has to be done with it.
14...b6 15.Nc1 Nc5 16.Nd3
Geller:
The knight has arrived at d3 from f3 not in two, but in four moves! It is understandable that White must now go over to defence.
16...Qe8
Gligorić:
White does not take the knight, as after 16...Nd3 17.Bd3 the manoeuvre would have given White a strategic advantage.
17.b3 f5 18.a3 Bh6 19.Bf2
Gligorić:
Preventing the activation of the bishop via e3.
19...Qe7 20.Qc2
Gligorić:
20.b4 ab4 21.ab4 Ra1 22.Qa1 Nb3 etc. was still impossible. Now there is the threat of 21.b4, but Black promptly takes counter-measures, putting pressure on the e4-square.
20...fe4
Geller/Gligorić:
(!)
21.fe4 Ng5 22.Nc5
Gligorić:
Thus, White has failed to achieve his main purpose, which was that he should bring pressure to bear on the queen’s wing with his pawns.
22...dc5
Gligorić:
(!) In this way Black gets the square d6 for his knight, while the weak e5-pawn is easy to protect. After 22...bc5 Black’s a-pawn would be weak, and it would be easier for White to advance b3-b4.
23.a4
Geller:
(!) White refrains from aggression on the queen’s flank and aims to provoke simplification.
Gligorić:
After 23.Qd3, 23...a4 would have been inconvenient.
23...Nf7 24.Qd3 Nd6 25.Bf3
Gligorić:
(!) So that 25...Bf4 can be answered by 26.Ne2.
Geller:
On the immediate 25.Be3 there would have followed 25...Bf4 or 25...Qh4.
25...Rf7
Geller:
Playing 25...Qg5 Black could retain the advantages of his position, but now the game rapidly proceeds to a draw.
26.Be3 Bg5 27.g3!
Geller/Gligorić:
(!)
27...Raf8 28.Bg2 Kg7 29.Rf7 Rf7 30.Qd2 Be3 31.Qe3 h5 32.Nd1
Gligorić:
(!) As a pair of rooks have been exchanged, White has no worries about the f-file, which is now easily protected. In the meantime, Nd1-f2-d3 is threatened, but Black can easily defend e5.
Agreed drawn.

[Geller/Gligorić]

Wolfgang Uhlmann – Mark Taimanov [E 17]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 e6 3.Nf3 b6 4.g3 Bb7 5.Bg2 Be7 6.0-0 0-0 7.d5
Uhlmann:
(!?) An interesting and hitherto little-studied pawn sacrifice.
Taimanov:
Uhlmann’s ‘patent’, which has brought him no little success. I had expected this variation, and after preparation, intended a new reply.
7...ed5 8.Nd4
RR: A decade later, in the 12th game of his Candidates’ Semi-final match v. Korchnoi in Buenos Aires, Polugaevsky introduced the idea 8.Nh4!. This has subsequently been considered the critical line here. - Griffin.
8...Nc6
Taimanov:
In my opinion, this is the most reasonable decision. Rather than aiming at a material advantage, Black seeks to mobilise his forces as rapidly as possible.
Uhlmann:
Black decides against defending the pawn by 8...Bc6 or 8...c6, since in both cases White would have some attacking chances.
9.cd5 Nd4 10.Qd4 c5 11.Qd3
Taimanov:
Clearly, after 11.dc6 dc6 White cannot count on anything.
11...d6 12.Nc3
Taimanov:
As a result there has arisen a position in the spirit of the Benoni Defence, but in a version favourable for Black. Firstly, the exchange of a pair of knights facilitates his manoeuvring; secondly, White cannot prevent the activation of the black queen-side pawns, since on 12.a4 all the same good is 12...a6 13.Nc3 b5 14.ab5 ab5 15.Ra8 Qa8. Thus, on this occasion the opening duel has not brought White success.
RR: In the 6th game of the above-mentioned match, Polugaevsky continued instead 12.a4 a6 13.Na3 b5 14.Bf4 b4 15.Nc4 a5 16.e4 Ba6 17.Qc2 Bc4 18.Qc4, etc. - Griffin.
12...a6 13.Bf4
Uhlmann:
It would have been better to play 13.a4, so as to prevent Black from taking action on the queen’s wing.
13...b5 14.a4
Taimanov:
Uhlmann aims to provoke a weakening of the light squares on the opponent’s queen-side, in order to then establish himself with his pieces at the c4-square.
The following stage is devoted to a struggle for this point.
14...b4 15.Nd1
Taimanov:
Also in the case of 15.Nb1 a5 16.Nd2 Ba6 17.Nc4 Nd7 18.Qc2 Bc4 19.Qc4 Nb6 20.Qb5 Bf6 Black has good piece play.
15...a5 16.b3 Ba6 17.Qc2 Nd7 18.Be4 g6 19.Bd3
Uhlmann:
This position, with the possibility of a blockade on the c4-square, was White’s line of strategy.
However, as can be seen from the further development of the game, the threat of a breakthrough at c4 in conjunction with threats along the half-open e-file gives Black the advantage.
19...Nb6 20.Bh6
Taimanov:
Since the immediate 20.Ne3 is bad on account of 20...g5, Uhlmann is ready to spend a tempo so as to carry out his plan. The move 20.e4, reliably consolidating the d5-pawn, had its shortcomings: 20...Bf6 21.Rc1 Bd3 22.Qd3 c4!.
20...Re8 21.Ne3
Taimanov:
White has finally completed the intended disposition of his forces and, it would seem, achieved success in the struggle for the key c4-square.
However, the activity of his pieces and the prospects of pressure along the e-file, more than compensate Black for this concession.
21...Bf6 22.Rad1 Qc8
Taimanov:
(!) A good move. The queen is now ‘looking’ towards two important squares - h3 and a6, and it prevents the move Ne3-g4.
23.Kg2 Bd3 24.Rd3
Taimanov:
A natural decision, but hardly the best. Better was 24.Qd3, intending in some cases to become active with Qd3-b5.
24...Ra7
Taimanov:
Here Uhlmann thought for a long time, from which it may be concluded that it is already not easy to find a good plan for White. Indeed, the threat of tying down his pieces with the move 25...Rae7 looks unpleasant, while a way to activate his pieces is not apparent. Therefore the German grandmaster took a realistic decision. With his following move he decided to simplify the game and to occupy himself with the consolidation of the position.
25.Nc4
Taimanov:
At this point I declined the offered draw. I understood that Black’s advantage is insignificant, but considered that the fighting resources had still not been exhausted.
25...Nc4 26.Qc4 Rae7 27.Rf3 Re4 28.Qb5 Qd8
Uhlmann:
(!) After this move it is very difficult for White to find a satisfactory plan of play. The weaknesses in White’s position - the points e2 and d5 - are more serious than the weaknesses a5 and d6 in Black’s camp.
Taimanov:
(!) All the same, this position is of course more promising for Black. He possesses the e-file, has the eventual threat of ...c5-c4, and can attack the ‘detached’ d5-pawn. However, for the time being White’s defensive possibilities are perfectly sufficient, and I think that after the correct move 29.Re3 the game would have ended peacefully. But Uhlmann feared the reply 29...g5, and he chose a different move, which proved to be clearly weaker.
29.Be3
Taimanov:
(?)
Uhlmann:
29.Re3 would have been a slightly better move.
29...Bd4
Taimanov:
(!)
30.Bd4 Rd4 31.Rd3
Taimanov:
The only move. On 31.e3 there would have followed not 31...Rd5 in view of 32.Rf7! Kf7 33.Qc4, but simply 31...Rd2, with a dangerous initiative for Black.
31...Rde4 32.e3 h5
Uhlmann:
It is very difficult to parry this wing attack effectively. Besides, White was already pressed for time.
Taimanov:
Despite the simplification, Black’s initiative has sharply increased during the last moves. White’s pieces lack co-ordination and occupy clumsy positions, and this complicates the defence.
33.h4 Kg7
Uhlmann:
(?!) A much better continuation would have been 33...g5, so as on 34.hg5 to reply 34...h4.
Taimanov:
A delay which could have cost Black his advantage. I naturally intended to attack the white king with the move 33...g5, but decided to make this move ‘in all comfort’, opening the 8th rank for the possible transfer of the rook to the h-file.
However, it is well known that one must not lose time in the attack and my opponent could now have prepared to repel the assault with the move 34.Re1. Then on 34...g5, possible is the reply 35.f3 and the worst for White is behind him. To my good fortune, Uhlmann underestimated the danger and the following move that he played fails to penetrate the subtleties of the position.
34.Rc1
Uhlmann:
(?) With this, White’s last possibility of defence is gone. He should have played 34.Re1 so that 34...g5 could be answered with 35.f3.
Taimanov:
(?) After this ‘obvious’ reply White’s game quickly rolls into the abyss.
34...g5 35.hg5 h4 36.Rc4

36...Qg5 37.Re4 Re4 38.Qd7
38...Qg6
Taimanov:
(!) It is pleasant to play such a move. It combines defence of the d6-pawn with threats along the diagonal h7-b1. The game is decided.
39.Rd1 hg3 40.Rg1 Rh4
Taimanov:
(!)
White resigned.
