Board 8
Mikhail Botvinnik – Milan Matulović [B 36]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.c4 c5 2.Nf3 g6 3.e4
Botvinnik:
As often occurs, from the English Opening there is obtained the Maróczy Variation of the Sicilian Defence.
Matulović:
Quite unexpected from Botvinnik, who rarely opens with the king’s pawn. He obliges me to play Simagin’s Variation of the Sicilian Defence. Was this a psychological tactic?
3...Nc6 4.d4 cd4 5.Nd4 Nf6 6.Nc3 d6
Botvinnik:
Matulović plays the opening in exemplary fashion. Black does not agree to exchange of knights at the d4-square, simultaneously complicating of the development of the c1-bishop at the square e3 (Be3 provokes the reply ...Ng4). In this way Black sidesteps the variation 6...Nd4 7.Qd4 d6 8.f3 Bg7 9.Be3 - a continuation which the specialists assess as favourable to White.
Matulović:
This move is not considered best, as it allows the knight to retreat to c2. However, as seen in Bárczay - Matulović and Korchnoi - Matulović (Sarajevo, 1969), after 7.Nc2 Bg7 8.Be2 Nd7!, Black has good prospects.
7.Be2
Botvinnik:
If 7.f3, with the aim of developing the c1-bishop at e3, White runs into slight difficulties. For example: 7...Qb6 8.Nc2 Bg7 9.Rb1 Nb4.
7...Nd4 8.Qd4 Bg7 9.Be3
Botvinnik:
Also possible was 9.0-0 0-0 10.Bd2 avoiding simplification, as per the recommendation of Smyslov and Korchnoi.
9...0-0 10.Qd2 Be6 11.f3
Matulović:
A more common move is 11.Rc1, with the possible continuation 11...Qa5 12.0-0 Rfc8 13.b3 a6 with chances for both sides.
11...Qa5
Botvinnik:
If 11...Rc8, then 12.b3 b5 13.e5 de5 14.Qd8 Rfd8 15.Nb5 with a slight advantage for White.
Matulović:
The best move at this point. 11...Rc8, which also looks enticing, could be followed by 12.b3, and now 12...b5 would be weak because of 13.e5! with complications in White’s favour.
12.Nb5
Botvinnik:
In this way White enters a slightly better endgame, thanks to the central position of his king and the possibility of seizing the bishop-pair.
Matulović:
This plan was seen in O’Kelly - Matulović (Palma de Mallorca, 1967). With correct play, Black equalises.
12...Qd2 13.Kd2 Nd7
Botvinnik:
This move Matulović had already employed several times. Black is ready to give up the bishop-pair in return for the central square d4 for his knight.
Matulović:
(!) A good move, with which Black begins the manoeuvre of his knight via e5 to c6, thereby gaining control of the central squares d4 and e5.
14.Rab1
Matulović:
In the game Benkő - Matulović (Venice, 1969) White decided on 14.Nc7, but after 14...Rac8 15.Ne6 fe6 16.Rab1 Ne5 17.Rhc1 Nc6 Black, who is threatening ...Bd4, has equal chances.
14...Ne5
Botvinnik:
It was necessary to play 14...Rfc8 (preventing 15.Nc7) and if 15.Na7, 15...Ra7 16.Ba7 b6 with advantage to Black. After Black’s last move, it was necessary to play 15.Nc7 Rac8 16.Ne6 fe6 17.b3 Nc6 18.f4!, and the endgame is much better for White, on account of the threat of Be2-g4 or the advance of the pawns on the king’s flank: g2-g4-g5, h2-h4-h5.
15.Rhc1
Botvinnik:
Now the initiative passes to Black and White’s advantage is gone.
15...Rfc8 16.b3 Nc6
Matulović:
Black has had no difficulty in carrying out his plan, and now has a good position. White no longer has the advantage.
17.f4 f5
Botvinnik:
Matulović finds the correct solution - this is introduced by the exchange of the black pawn at g6 for the active e4-pawn. After this, Black obtains a pawn majority in the centre.
18.Bf3 fe4 19.Be4 Bf5
Botvinnik:
(!). The logical completion of the manoeuvre begun at the 17th move.
20.Bf5 gf5
21.Ke2
Botvinnik:
White does not want to reconcile himself to the loss of the advantage that he had in the opening, and, as often occurs, it is just at such a moment that he chooses a bad move! With his last move, White loses at least two tempi, since the king should be at d2 to defend the rook at c1.
The only correct course was 21.h3 h5 22.g4 hg4 23.hg4 fg4 24.Rg1 with the simplification of the game.
21...Kf7 22.Rd1
Botvinnik:
(?) A new mistake! The rook should have been left at c1, since after 22...a6 the knight could retreat to c3 and then move to a4. After White’s new inaccuracy, Black leads the game by force into a favourable ending.
22...a6 23.Nd4 Nd4 24.Bd4 b5
Botvinnik:
(!) It is now clear why it was a mistake to remove the king from the d2-square, and the rook from the c-file.
25.Bg7 Kg7 26.Rdc1
Botvinnik:
Peace was concluded on White’s proposal. For the sake of objectivity, it must be noted that Black has the better endgame, since after ...Rc6 followed by ...Rac8, ...Kf6, ...a6-–a5 White is forced to oppose the pressure.
Matulović:
At this juncture I accepted the draw offered by Botvinnik. Perhaps with 26...Rc5 I might have tried to continue, but it is doubtful whether this would have been succesful.
½ : ½
[Botvinnik/Matulović]
Board 9
Miguel Najdorf – Mikhail Tal [D 41]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.Nf3
Tal:
The Argentinian grandmaster refrains from his beloved 1.d2-d4, clearly wishing to avoid various sorts of ‘Benoni’ schemes (1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 c5). However, it must be recognised that I had no particular desire to go in for an immediate sharpening of the game. And in this case, a Queen’s Gambit would have been probable.
1.Nf3 Nf6 2.c4 e6 3.Nc3 d5 4.d4 c5
Najdorf:
After an unusual beginning the game has moved on to well-trodden paths
5.cd5 Nd5 6.e3 Nc6 7.Bc4
Najdorf:
These moves have been in use for a long time.
Tal:
The choice between this move and 7.Bd3 is a matter of taste. In recent times the continuation chosen by Najdorf has been encountered most often. In particular, Larsen played this way in the 1st game of the 1969 match, where I replied 7.Bc4 Be7 and after 8.Bd5 ed5 9.dc5 Black had to overcome certain difficulties. I naturally did not wish to repeat this game.
7...cd4 8.ed4 Be7 9.0-0 0-0 10.Re1
10...Nc3
Najdorf:
In my game against Larsen (Palma de Mallorca, 1969), at this point I withdrew my knight to f6.
Tal:
After the game my opponent was very critical regarding this move, which to me however seems to be not bad. While ridding White of concerns associated with the isolated d-pawn, Black intends to ‘press’ along the c-file against the backward pawn at c3, recognising, of course, that he will have to reckon with serious threats on the king’s flank. However, also having its pluses is 10...Nf6, going over to the lines of the Queen’s Gambit Accepted.
11.bc3 b6 12.Bd3 Bb7 13.Qc2 g6
Najdorf:
13...h6 is not advisable because of 14.Qe2.
14.Bh6
Najdorf:
14.Qd2 was another possibility.
14...Re8 15.Qd2
Najdorf:
In the same position against Szabo (Groningen, 1946), Botvinnik played 15.Bb5. The text move is more aggressive and more in line with my style.
Tal:
Also interesting was 15.h4, and if 15...Bh4 16.d5 on this I intended to continue 16...Qd5 17.Nh4 Qh5. The position after the move in the text had already been encountered in my game with L. Grigorian (White) from the USSR People’s Spartakiad (1967). Then, it seems, I played 15...Bf6 16.Qf4 Bg7 17.Ng5, which gave White a definite advantage.
RR: There seems to be some confusion here on Tal’s part. Against Grigorian, he continued, as in the present game, 15...Rc8, and on 16.Rac1 Rc7 17.Bf4 Rd7, etc. - Griffin.
15...Rc8 16.h4
Tal:
The sacrifice of a pawn is ficticious - after 16...Bh4 17.Nh4 Qh4 there follows with great strength 18.Bg5. Into White’s plans comes the further weakening of the opponent’s king’s flank.
16...Na5
Najdorf
: We have a familiar set-up in this position. White is active on the king’s side, and Black seeks chances on the queen’s. It would be a mistake now to play 16...Bh4 because of 17.Nh4 Qh4 18.Bg5 with a decisive attack.
Tal:
It is necessary to deflect the opponent and therefore Black immediately brings the c3-pawn under fire.
17.Ng5
Najdorf:
Was 17.h5 stronger? After 17...Bf3 18.gf3 Qd5 19.hg6 hg6 20.Be4 Qh5 21.Kg2 Nc4 22.Qc1 Ba3 23.Qf4 Bd6 Black could gain a draw by repeating moves.
Tal:
In reply to 17.Ne5 Black could without particular risk play 17...Bh4. A very sharp situation arose after 17.h5, permitting the doubling of the pawns, but in so doing Black is deprived of the strong light-squared bishop. The Argentinian grandmaster prefers a somewhat more restrained path.
17...Bf8
Tal:
Already there is perhaps nothing else. The proximity of the bishop and knight is very much not to the liking of Black’s king.
18.Bf8 Rf8 19.h5
Najdorf:
Mobilising my rook would not yield the desired result: 19.Re3 Nc4 20.Bc4 Rc4 21.Rg3 e5! etc.
19...Nc4
Tal:
Just in time! It transpires that White’s attack is not so dangerous. Instead of the move in the text I also examined the exchange sacrifice 19...Rc3, but rejected it on account of the fact that after 20.Qc3 (amusingly, the intermediate 20.hg6 will not do on account of 20...Rd3!) 20...Qg5 21.Be4 Be4 22.Re4 Qh5 23.Re5 the poor position of the knight at a5 guarantees White the better chances.
20.Bc4
Najdorf:
At this juncture I was disappointed. I had intended to proceed with the attack by 20.Qf4, but then I realised that the situation after 20...h6! is not clear at all. Here are the variations:
a)
21.Nh3 g5! 22.Qg3 Nb2;
b)
21.Ne4 g5 22.Qg3 Qc7 23.Qf3 f5 24.Bc4 Qc4 25.Nf6 Rf6 26.Qb7 Rc7 with advantage for Black;
c)
21.Ne6 fe6 22.Qh6 Qf6! and White’s assault is repulsed.
Tal:
Dangerous looks 20.Qf4 with the unanbiguous intention of transferring the queen to h4. On 20.Qf4 unfavourable is 20...Nb2 21.hg6 hg6 22.Qh4 and also 20...e5 21.Qg3.
The ‘essence’ of Black’s defence is the move 20...h6! and it is unfavourable for White to sacrifice a piece - 21.Ne6 fe6 22.Qh6 Qf6! 23.Re2 Qg7, while after 21.Ne4 g5 22.Qg3 Qc7 chokes White’s initiative. The exchange of the active bishop is no little achievement for Black.
RR: - A few years later, the game Langeweg - Faragó (IBM Tournament, Amsterdam 1976) indeed continued 20.Qf4 h6!, and then 21.Ne4 g5 22.Qc1 Kg7 23.f4 g4 24.f5 Be4 25.Re4 ef5 26.Rf4 Nd6 27.Bf5 Nf5 28.Rf5 Qc7, and a draw was agreed. - Griffin.
20...Rc4 21.hg6 hg6 22.Qf4
Najdorf:
With the definite threat of 23.Qh4.
Tal:
New reserves enter the attack. The king’s flank is all the same under threat.
22...Qd5
Najdorf:
22...Kg7 would have been followed by 23.Qf7!.
Tal:
Clearly, the only move. Besides the ‘brutal’ 23.Qh4, the threat of Re1-e3-h3 is very unpleasant. Winning a tempo with the threat of mate, Black is in time to consolidate the position.
23.f3
Tal:
In reply to 23.Ne4 there could follow 23...Qf5.
23...Kg7 24.Ne4
Najdorf:
I was faced with a difficult decision. Why not 24.Re5?. This might have been followed by 24...Qd7 25.Kf2 Rh8 26.Rae1 Bd5. As the king’s side has been fortified, the pressure against the c3-pawn now represents a real threat. White could continue with 27.R5e3, and after 27...Qc7 neither opponent would have a real advantage.
Tal:
Before this move Najdorf, having thought for a long time, unexpectedly offered a draw. I was seized by the excitement of the struggle, and I decided to continue the fight. However, it seems to me that more dangerous was 24.Re5 Qd7 25.Rae1, maintaining the tension. In a series of variations the elegant leap of the rook to h5 is threatened, as well as the eventual manoeuvre Ng5-h3-f4.
24...Qf5
Najdorf:
After 24...f6 25.Nd6? would not do because of 25...e5!. However, 25.Kf2 would have been very attractive for White. With the move in the text Black hopes to thwart his opponent’s attack, for the last minor piece can thereby be exchanged. The position is equalised and I realised that I had not managed to take advantage of the first move. So I offered a draw, but Tal refused my offer.
25.Qg3
Tal:
Interestingly, the move after having offered a draw, White decides to sacrifice a pawn, but to retain the queens. The endgame is somewhat more pleasant for Black, but after 25.Qf5 ef5 26.Nd6 Rc7 27.Nb7 Rb7 28.d5! White maintains the balance without any particular difficulty. The move played by Najdorf is significantly sharper, but perfectly possible. At any rate, neither at the board nor in subsequent analysis did I manage to find a path to victory. A draw - this is quite another matter...
25...Be4 26.fe4 Qa5
Najdorf:
Nor would 26...Qh5 have been better. This could have been followed by 27.Re3 Rh8 28.Rf1 and the check at h2 would do no harm to White.
27.Re3 Rfc8 28.Rf1
Najdorf:
Clearly, White cannot defend his c-pawn, but he can still attack his opponent’s king, ensuring at least a draw.
Tal:
The pawn at c3 is indefensible - on 28.Rc1 there follows 28...Rd4. White has strong trumps - the f-file (half-open), and also the h-file (open!).
28...Rc3
Najdorf:
Already while moving the knight to e4 I had in mind the saving manoeuvre that later occurred.
29.Ref3
Najdorf:
(!)
29...R8c7
Tal:
29...Rf3 30.Qf3 Rf8 is too passive.
RR: Indeed, after 31.Qf6 Kg8 32.Rf3 White wins by force. - Griffin.
30.Qf4
Najdorf:
(!)
30...Kg8
Najdorf:
Black does not allow White to put him in check.
Tal:
I had already decided to force a draw - 30...Rd7 31.Rc3 Qc3 32.Qe5 Kg8 33.Qb8 Kh7 34.Qh2. Then, however, I decided to hold off for one more move. The move in the text reckons on the natural 31.Qf6 Rd7.
31.Qd6
Najdorf:
(!)
Tal:
(!) This move is significantly stronger. Now it was already obligatory to force the draw, which was achieved with 31...Qg5 32.Rc3 Rc3 33.Qb8 Kg7 34.Qa7 Qe3 35.Kh1! (weaker is 35.Kh2 Qg3 36.Kh1 Rc7!). Black had already intended to play 31...Qg5, but suddenly for a second there flashed the variation 31...Rf3 32.Rf3 Rc1 33.Kh2 b5. Impulsively, there followed...
31...Rf3
Tal:
...and here already I noticed my mistake, which Najdorf exploited instantly.
Najdorf:
(?) Black could have played 31...Qg5! and after 32.Rc3 Rc3 33.Qb8 Kg7 34.Qa7 the game would end in a draw.
32.Qd8!
Najdorf:
(!) A really unpleasant intermediate move!
Tal:
This is the whole point. White’s queen bursts into the enemy camp. Already now there is no draw for Black.
32...Kg7 33.Rf3
Najdorf:
Suddenly White begins an attack that cannot be repulsed. Together with 34.Qc7, White threatens 34.Qf6 and 35.Rh3.
33...Qe1 34.Kh2 Rc3
Tal:
The only possibility of preventing the transfer of the rook to h3, but now the f7-pawn proves to be defenceless. The final moves before the time control were made in circumstances of mutual time trouble, particularly acute for Black.
35.Qf6 Kh6 36.Qf4 Kh7
Tal:
Black cannot continue 36...Kh5 37.g4 Kh4 38.Qh6 Kg4 39.Rf4#. Najdorf does not force events, reckoning (and not without justification) on finding a forcing path to victory in analysis.
37.Qf7 Kh6 38.Qf4 Kh7
Najdorf:
It is clear that 38...Kh5 would lead to mate after 39.g4 Kh4 40.Qh6.
39.Qf7
Najdorf:
Of course I could have played 39.Rf1 right away but I was mainly worried about the time control.
39...Kh6 40.Qf8 Kh5
Najdorf:
In this position the game was adjourned. White wins by force.
Tal:
In this position the game was adjourned. That evening it was established that White wins, and that the path to victory lies through a two-fold (unfortunately not a three-fold) repetition of the position.
41.Qh8
Tal:
The sealed move.
41...Kg5 42.Qe5 Kh6 43.Qf4 Kh7
Najdorf:
See the note to move 39.
44.Rf1
Tal:
In this lies White’s idea. Having covered the g3-square, White with gain of tempo removes the rook from exchange.
44...Qe2
Najdorf/Tal:
In reply to 44...Qe3 decisive is 45.Qh4 Kg7 (45...Qh6 46.Rf7!) 46.Rf4!.
45.Qf7 Kh6 46.Qf8 Kh5
Najdorf:
A similar finale would have resulted from 46...Kg5 47.Qf6 Kh5 48.Qe5 Kh6 49.Rf4 Qe1 50.Rg4 Kh7 51.Qf6 etc.
47.Rf4
Tal:
Threatening mate in two moves 48.Qh8 Kg5 49.Qh4#. The reply is forced.
47...Qe1 48.Qh8 Kg5 49.Qe5 Kh6 50.Rg4
Tal:
Already, that’s that. There is no saving Black from a quick mate.
Black resigned.
1 : 0
[Najdorf/Tal]
Board 10
Paul Keres – Borislav Ivkov [C 87]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.0-0 Be7 6.Re1 d6
Keres:
A continuation that normally leads to a variation of the Steinitz Defence Deferred.
7.Bc6 bc6 8.d4 Nd7
Keres:
Leading to a difficult defence therefore Black ought to try 8...ed4.
9.Nbd2 0-0
Ivkov:
(?!) I played the same way against Smejkal in the four-game playoff match at the Prague Zonal tournament.
RR: this had taken place earlier in 1970. - Griffin.
I was interested to know what might have been a better move for Smejkal. Now I think I know... Otherwise, another possibility in keeping with the position is 9...f6 10.Nc4 Nb6 11.Na5 Bd7, as demonstrated in the game Keres - Reshevsky (Los Angeles, 1963).
10.Nc4
10...Bf6
Keres:
A new idea of holding the e5-pawn, but, judging from everything, not the most succesful. Evidently, better is the old continuation 10...f6.
RR: Many years previously, v. Boleslavsky in the 20th USSR Championship (Moscow, 1952) Keres himself had tried the continuation 10...ed4?! 11.Nd4 Nb8 here. - Griffin.
11.Be3
Ivkov:
(!) The above-mentioned game against Smejkal continued instead 11.c3 a5! 12.Qa4 ed4 13.cd4 c5 14.e5 de5 15.de5 Be7 16.Rd1? Qe8 17.Qc6 Nb6 18.Qe4 f5!, with advantage for Black.
11...Qe8
Keres:
Black has run into difficulties with the development of his pieces. He aims at all costs to avoid the illogical exchange at d4 and is thereby forced to reckon with the possibilities of Nc4-a5 or d4xe5. White, by simple means, strengthens his position.
12.Qd2 Qe6 13.Qc3
Ivkov:
(!) The pressure on e5 is increased, and at the same time the weak point at c6 is sounded out.
13...Rb8 14.b3 Re8 15.Na5
Keres:
White recognises that after 15...ed4 he cannot take with the knight at d4 on account of the reply (16.Nxd4) 16...c5! (16...Qe4? 17.Bd2!), since Black then obtains good play.
With the move 15.Rad1 White could have continued to keep the opponent in a passive position.
Ivkov:
15.Rad1, maintaining the tension, would also be good.
15...ed4 16.Bd4
Ivkov:
Fortunately, White could not play 16.Nd4 because of 16...c5 - the idea behind the move 13...Rb8.
16...c5 17.Bf6 Qf6 18.Qf6 gf6
Keres:
Also after 18...Nf6 19.e5 de5 20.Ne5 White retains the advantage.
Ivkov:
18...Nf6 does not promise much either, but the formation of four pawn ‘islands’, regardless of the closed character of the position, is no consolation.
19.Nd2 Ne5 20.f4 Ng6 21.g3 Bd7
Ivkov:
Even after 21...f5 22.e5 de5 23.Nc6 Rb6 24.Ne5 Ne5 25.Re5 Re5 26.fe5 f6 Black’s situation is not hopeful, but in any case it was the only way to proceed.
22.Nac4 Bc6 23.Ne3
Ivkov:
(!)
23...Kf8
Keres:
Mistaken was 23...Be4? since after 24.Ng4 Kg7 25.Ne4 f5 26.Ngf6 Black loses the exchange, or, after 26...Re6? 27.Nh5 followed by 28.Nef6, a piece.
Ivkov:
23...Kh8 seems better.
24.Kf2 Be4
Keres:
(?) Black falls into a trap, since he reckons only with 25.Ne4 Re4 26.Nd5, which gives White the possibility of going into a better endgame. However, a surprise follows.
Ivkov:
(??) This is certainly a blunder, but Black’s position is difficult in any case.
25.f5
Ivkov:
This is the move which I unfortunately noticed too late, when I had already touched the e4-pawn. In my haste I forgot to say “J’adoube”...
25...Bc6
Keres:
(!) Black has no other way out, since after 25...Nh8 26.Ne4 Re4 27.Nc4! the knight at h8 is doomed to the role of an extra for the rest of the game. After the move in the text White, with a piece for two pawns, has an almost winning position.
Ivkov:
(?) To retreat the knight was impossible, but instead he had to play 25...Bb7.
26.fg6 fg6
Ivkov:
At least Black has some compensation: the balance between his pawns has been re-established.
27.Nec4
Keres: (?) White conducts the following part of the game most uncertainly. It was possible to consolidate White’s advantage very simply with the move 27.c4 followed by Ne3-d5.
Ivkov:
(?) White’s first mistake. 27.c4 followed by Ne3-d5 should have been played.
27...Kf7 28.a3 Re1 29.Re1 g5 30.Ne3 h5 31.Nf5
Ivkov:
(?)
Keres:
In the present case too 31.c4 followed by Ne3-d5 was the simplest solution.
31...Re8 32.Re8
Ivkov:
(?) To his other mistakes White adds one more and now seriously jeopardises his chances of victory. The rooks had to be retained, in view of the presence of two knights for White, and the reduced material.
32...Be8
33.h4
Keres:
(!) A good move, which fixes the h5-pawn on a square of the colour of the black bishop.
33...d5
Keres:
A final attempt to render the situation of the f5-knight difficult, by means of ...d5-d4.
34.b4
Keres:
The possibility of exchanging pawns significantly facilitates Black’s defensive task. White achieves much more with the move 34.Ne3 Ke6 35.Nf3 etc.
34...Bd7 35.Ne3 cb4 36.ab4 gh4
Ivkov:
In time pressure I feared that 36...Ke6 might be followed by 37.g4, creating a passed pawn on the h-file. For example, 37...hg4 38.h5 c6 39.Nb3 Kf7 40.Nd4 and Ne3-f5.
37.gh4 Ke6 38.Nf3
Keres:
Under the pressure of time trouble White plays inaccurately. Necessary was 38.Nb3 Ke5 39.c3 with complete control over the dark squares. Black now gains serious chances of saving the game.
38...Kd6 39.c3 c5 40.Ke2 Kc6 41.Kd3
Keres:
Here the game was adjourned and Black sealed his move. Thanks to the reduction in material and his strong bishops, Black has good prospects of saving the half-point. White’s trumps in the continuation were the weak pawn at h5 and the circumstance that the dark squares are under his control. Nonetheless, for complete control White is forced to permit the creation of a dangerous passed pawn on the a-file. The following endgame was so complicated that even analysis could not establish with certainty the hopelessness or otherwise of Black’s situation. At any rate, one way or another White retains good prospects of victory.
41...Be8
Keres:
The sealed move and the best one in the given position. Hopeless was 41...d4 on account of 42.cd4 cb4 43.Kc4 a5 44.d5 etc. or 41...Kd6 on account of 42.bc5 Kc5 43.Nd4 and Black would have ended up in a difficult situation as a consequence of the threat of 44.Nb3 followed by 45.Kd4. Nothing is changed by the continuation 43...Kd6 44.Nef5 Ke5 45.Ng7 since after 45...Bg4 there follows 46.Ke3, while on 45...f5 there is 46.Nh5 f4 47.Nf3 etc.
Ivkov:
This was the sealed move. With 41...d4 42.cd4 cb4 43.Kc4 a5 44.d5 Black would have been driven back, and the queen-side pawns blocked.
42.bc5 Kc5 43.Nd4 Bg6 44.Kd2 a5! 45.Ng2!
Ivkov:
45.Nb3 Kb5 would not have worked: 46.Nd5 Kc4 47.Nf4 Kb3 48.Ng6 a4 49.Nf4! (or 49.Kc1? Kc3 50.Nf4 Kd4 51.Nh5 Ke5) 49...a3 50.Ne2 f5! 51.Nc1 Kc4 52.Kc2 f4, and White cannot win.
45...Kd6
Keres:
After 45...a4 White achieves the good set-up 46.Nf4 Be8 47.Nf5.
46.Nf4 Be8 47.Nd3 Bg6
Ivkov:
(!)
48.Nb5
Keres:
With this move White begins the inaccurate implementation of the intended plan, in mutual time trouble.
He ought to continue 48.Nf4 Be8 49.Nc2, with which it was possible to achieve the position which appears in the further course of the game.
48...Kc6 49.Nf4
Keres:
(?) On 49.Na3 there could follow 49...Bd3 50.Kd3 Kc5 after which, most probably of all, White can no longer win the game. However, the move in the game is mistaken. White should have continued 49.Nd4 Kd6 50.Nf4 etc.
Ivkov:
White should have repeated the position with 49.Nd4.
49...Be8
Keres:
(?) Feeling the shortage of time, both opponents considered the endgame after 49...Kb5! 50.Ng6 Kc4 51.Nf4 d4 52.Nh5 (but not 52.cd4 Kd4 53.Nh5 Ke5 with a draw) 52...dc3 53.Kc2 to be won for White. However, after 53...a4 54.Nf4 a3 55.Kb1 Kb3 56.Ne2 a2 57.Ka1 f5 58.h5 (or 58.Nd4 Kc4 59.Nf5 Kd3!) 58...f4 59.Nc3 Kc3 60.h6 f3 61.h7 f2 62.h8=Q Kd2 Black would have just achieved the draw.
Ivkov:
(?) Black lets slip a good chance to draw, by giving up his bishop for the knight. For example: 49...Kb5! 50.Ng6 Kc4! 51.Nf4 d4! 52.Nh5! (or 52.cd4 Kd4 53.Nh5 Ke5 with a draw) 52...dc3 53.Kc2 a4 54.Nf4 a3 55.Kb1 Kb3 56.Ne2 c2 57.Ka1 f5 58.h5 f4 59.h6 f3 60.Nc1 Kc4 and a draw. White can still try 57.Kc1 a2 58.Nd4 Kc4! 59.Nc2 Kd5 60.Kb2 Ke5, but there is no win. It is difficult to explain why at that moment I did not consider these possibilities, while in analysis I saw them all.
50.Na3 Kd6 51.Ke3 Ke5
Keres:
Black is in time, at the critical moment, to prevent White’s king from breaking through to the d4-square.
52.Nc2 a4 53.Nd4
Keres:
Also possible was 53.Nd3 Kf5 54.Kf3 Ke6 55.Nd4 Kd6, but the effect of this continuation would have been no different from that of the move in the text.
53...a3
Keres:
In this way Black considerably facilitates White’s task. Correct was 53...Kd6! 54.Nd3 Bg6, after which it is not quite clear whether White can improve his position.
Ivkov:
(?) In the time scramble it seemed to me that by sacrificing the most important pawn I could advance with the king, and that White’s knight would find itself in a very unpleasant position at the very edge of the board. However, the pawn should have been left at the a4, with ...Be8-g6 threatening an exchange. The continuation might have been 53...Kd6 54.Nd3 Bg6 55.Nb4 Ke5 56.Nf3 Kf5!? (Instead, playing passively allows White’s king to reach d4, which may be seen in the variation 56...Kd6 57.Kd4 Bf7 and White transfers his knight from f3 to f4) 57.Nd5 Bf7 58.c4 a3 59.Nd4 Kg4 60.Nf6 Kh4 61.c5 a2, etc. Thus, Black’s plan should have consisted of active play, along with the possible sacrifice of the d-pawn in order to break through with the king to one side or the other. However, the advance of the a-pawn was not fully justified at this point. As a result, the real complexities of the endgame did not manifest themselves.
54.Nd3 Kd6 55.Nc2 a2 56.Ndb4
56...Bg6
Keres:
It is difficult to say whether Black gained better prospects from the continuation 56...Ba4 57.Na1 Kc5 58.Na2 Kc4. There could then follow 59.Kd2 d4 (if instead 59...f5, then 60.Nc1 d4 61.Ne2! dc3 62.Nc3 etc.) 60.cd4 Kd4 61.Nc3 Bd7 (the piece sacrifice 61...f5 62.Na4 Ke4 63.Nc2 Kf4 64.Ne3 would have been incorrect) 62.Nc2 and White retains very good winning prospects.
Ivkov:
(??) The last, and most serious mistake. Black had almost secured a draw, but, pressed for time, he made this ill-considered move. There was a good chance with 56...Ba4!, after which White would have to play extremely carefully in order to realise his material advantage. The variations are as follows: 57.Na1 Kc5 58.Na2 Kc4 59.Kf4 d4! 60.cd4 Kd4. In this position White cannot proceed with 61.Kf5 because of 61...Ke3 with a draw. Thus, there are only two possibilities for White to continue this endgame: 61.Nc1 Kc3 62.Kf5 Kb2 63.Nab3 Be8 64.Kf6 Kc2 65.Kg5 Kb2, and White cannot untie his knights. But that would not be all! A draw is still a long way off 66.Ne2! Kb3 67.Nf4 Kc4 68.Nh5 Kc5 69.Nf4 Kd6 70.Kf6 and wins. Therefore: 65...Bf7 66.Ne2! Kb3 67.Nf4 Kc4 68.Nh5 Kd5 69.Nf4 Ke5! (69...Kd6? 70.Kf6 and wins) 70.Ng6 Ke6 71.h5 Bg8! and the king goes to f7. Besides this, there is a third continuation of this variation: 63...Bd7! 64.Kf6 Bg4 65.Kg5 Kc2 66.Ne2! Kb3 67.Nf4 Kc4 68.Nh5 Bd1 69.Nf6 Kc5! 70.Ng4 Bg4 and the black king gets to f8. The last variation to be explored begins with 61.Nb4! and then 61...Kc3 62.Nd5 Kb2 63.Nf6 Ka1 64.Nh5 Kb2 65.Nf6 Kc3 66.h5 Kd4 67.h6 Bc2
68.Ng4! Bb1 69.Kg5 Kd5 70.Kf6 Kd6 71.Ne5 Bh7 72.Kg7 and White wins.
Unfortunately, it seems that Black is lost after 56...Ba4!. However, there can be no question about the difficulty of the tasks confronting White. Moreover, the above variations would be more appopriate to a theoretical study than to practival play, and therefore it is a pity that Black did not take advantage of 56...Ba4! The further resumption of the game was more or less forced and was not necessary at all.
57.Na1 Kc5
Keres:
On 57...Bb1 there would have followed 58.Kd4 and the d5-pawn is in a hopeless situation, while the c3-pawn could, in case of necessity, advance. A good continuation for White was 57...Ke5 58.Na2 f5 59.Nb3 f4 60.Kf2. The move in the text is no better than any other continuation.
58.Nb3
Ivkov:
(!)
Keres:
This is the point! Bad was 58.Na2, since White’s in this case after 58...Kc4 White’s knight would have been stalemated.
58...Kc4 59.Nd2 Kc3 60.Na2 Kb2 61.Nb4 Bf7 62.Kd4
Keres:
In the endgame White wins, since he is only faced with presenting the approach of the black king towards the h-pawn. Nonetheless, the path to victory does not lack interest.
62...Kc1 63.Nf3 Kd1 64.Ke3
Keres:
(!) Mistaken would have been 64.Nd5 on account of 64...Ke2! etc. White may take the d5-pawn only once the black king has been cut off.
64...Bg8 65.Nd3 Kc2
Keres:
65...Bf7 66.Nf4 led to the same continuation as that which occurs in the game.
66.Nf4
Keres:
White could also continue 66.Nc5 and 67.Nd7.
66...Bf7 67.Kd4 Kd1
68.Kd3!
Keres:
The decisive manoeuvre. Black finds himself in zugzwang.
68...Kc1 69.Ne2 Kb2 70.Nc3 Bg8
Keres:
Also after 70...Kc1 71.Kd4 the black king cannot cross the d-file.
71.Kd4 Kc2 72.Nd5 Bd5
Keres:
A final attempt. Otherwise, in the case of the advance of the f-pawn, the white king reaches g5 with an easy win.
73.Kd5 Kd3 74.Nd4 Ke3 75.Nf5 Kf4 76.Ke6
Black resigned.
Keres:
After 76...Kg4 77.Kf6 Kf4 White wins with the move 78.Nh6. An interesting and substantive endgame.
1 : 0
[Ivkov/Keres]
(back to the text)
Larsen:
The position has now become Nimzowitsch’s variation of the Sicilian Defence with colours reversed: 1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nf6 3.e5 Nd5 4.Nc3 Nc3 5.dc3. In this position Nimzowitsch, in his time, played 5...b6?, which was later displaced by 6.e6!. White has an extra tempo in comparison with the above position, and besides it is not quite certain that the bishop’s position at c5 is the best one.
ROUND 3
Thursday 2nd April, 1970
A dramatic day that saw the Rest of the World team reverse the result of Round 2, leaving the match wide open.
On top board, Spassky gained the better position against Larsen from a Queen’s Indian Defence, and had a clear advantage in the queenless middlegame that resulted. However, after he missed a very strong continuation at the 25th move the position became double-edged. Despite being in serious time trouble, the Dane managed to stay in the game and keep the pressure on the Spassky. Possibly as a result of playing for the win when the position no longer merited it, the World Champion blundered shortly before the time control, and his position immediately collapsed.
On board 2, Petrosian employed an unusual line in the Caro-Kann Defence against Fischer that resulted in the sort of closed, strategic position in which the ex-World Champion excelled. Fischer was unable to make any headway and even stood slightly worse in the middlegame. However, he was never in any real danger of losing and a draw was agreed at the 32nd move.
Against Portisch on board 3, Korchnoi again opened with 1.e4. In a Spanish Game, he inexplicably omitted to play the standard c2-c3 at the 9th move, and allowed Black to exchange White’s important light-squared bishop for a knight. Black’s opening difficulties were immediately solved, but he played the middlegame in unconvincing fashion and allowed Korchnoi to build up a strong king-side attack. The game was adjourned in a position that favoured the Soviet player. However, on the resumption Korchnoi ran short of time and after missing a clear win at the 48th move, the position became unclear. After further mistakes by White, it was Portisch who took the full point.
On board 4, Hort and Polugaevsky played the only ‘grandmaster draw’ out of the 40 games played in Belgrade.
On board 5, Geller and Gligorić continued their theoretical duel in the Smyslov System of the Spanish Game. This time the Yugoslav grandmaster improved on an earlier game from 1968 between the same players. White was unable to extract any advantage from the opening, and a draw was soon agreed.
On the next board Reshevsky opened with 1.e4, clearly wishing to test Smyslov in the Chigorin Variation of the Spanish Game that the ex-World Champion preferred in those years. Introducing a relatively new idea at the 19th move, he emerged from the opening with the advantage and subsequently gave a textbook example White’s play in this structure. The game was adjourned in a winning position for White, and Smyslov’s resignation followed at the 54th move.
Taimanov and Uhlmann continued their theoretical dispute in the Grünfeld Defence on board 7; the East German grandmaster introduced a prepared improvement but soon stood worse and by the 23rd move an endgame was reached with a clear extra pawn for White. However, Taimanov failed to play in the most convincing manner and Uhlmann, displaying resourcesful and inventive defence, managed to salvage a draw.
On board 8 against Matulović, Botvinnik again employed the Pirc Defence and solved all of his opening difficulties satisfactorily. After some inaccurate play by White in a simplified heavy-piece position, Black gained a clear positional advantage which by the adjournment had been transformed into an extra pawn in a queen endgame. However, for once Botvinnik’s flawless technique deserted him, and the game eventually ended at the 93rd move when the Yugoslav forced a draw via a clever stalemating strategem.
On the ninth board Tal scored the Soviet team’s only win in this round, defeating Najdorf in fine positional style. The Argentinian grandmaster was unable to recover from some poor opening play on the black side of a Sicilian Defence, as a result of which he was left with a compromised queen-side without any compensation for this static defect.
On the last board Ivkov and Keres shared the point. In a quiet position arising from the Tarrasch Defence, Ivkov launched an attack on the black king, but it was parried by Keres by means of a clever pawn sacrifice, as a result of which he gained a clear advantage. However, in the time scramble that followed he failed to make the most of his chances, and a draw was agreed in the first adjourned session.
Board 1
Boris Spassky – Bent Larsen [E 12]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 e6 3.Nf3 b6 4.Nc3 Bb7 5.Bg5 Be7 6.e3 0-0
Spassky:
Usually played here is 6...Ne4, aiming at the simplification of the position.However, a peaceful outcome to the encounter did not suit Larsen, and he prefers a more complicated struggle.
RR: After 6...Ne4 Spassky had previously tried 7.Bf4!? Bb4 8.Qc2 v. Lutikov (27th USSR Championship, Leningrad 1960) and 7.Ne4 Be4 8.Bf4 0-0 9.Bd3 Bb4 10.Kf1 v. Polugaevsky (28th USSR Championship, Moscow 1961). - Griffin.
7.Bd3 c5 8.0-0
8...Nc6
Spassky:
(?) A mistake, which leads Black into a difficult position. He ought to play 8...cd4 9.ed4 d5, going over to standard positions of the Queen’s Gambit.
Larsen:
(?!) I played this move in order to complicate the game. However, it was not correct. With 8...h6 9.Bh4 the move 9...Nc6 might have been a good one.
9.d5 Nb4
Larsen:
The sequel 9...ed5 10.cd5 Nd5 11.Bh7 would give White a superior position.
10.d6 Bd6 11.Bh7
Larsen:
Another possibility was 11.Bf6 as Black cannot play 11...Qf6?? because of 12.Be4! winning a piece (RR: in fact, Black can escape with 12...Bh2 - Griffin.), while the continuation 11...gf6 12.Bh7 Kh7 13.Qd6 would lead to a sharp position with advantage for White because of the weakness of the d7-pawn.
11...Kh7 12.Qd6 Bf3 13.gf3
13...Ne8
Spassky:
(!) All of these moves are forced, while Black’s last move is his best chance.
Larsen:
What else? However, I had completely overlooked my opponent’s simple and very strong answer. After 14.Qg3 f6 the position would not be clear, and after 14.Qf8 Qg5 15.Kh1 Qh5 16.Rg1 Qf3 17.Rg2 g6 Black would retain good tactical chances.
14.Qe7
Larsen:
(!)
Spassky:
(!) This is stronger than 14.Bd8 Nd6 15.Be7 Nc4 16.Bf8 Rf8, and Black has sufficient compensation for the exchange.
14...Qe7 15.Be7 Rg8
Spassky:
Better was 15...Rh8, aiming to bring the rook into play immediately after 16...Kg8.
Larsen:
It is difficult to say whether 15...Rh8 would have been better. I thought that in one variation after ...Nf6, Bf6, a tempo might be gained with ...gf6.
16.Rfd1 Nc6 17.Rd7 Ne5 18.Rb7 Nc4
19.Ne4
Spassky:
(!) Black has a difficult position. Bad now is 19...Nb2 on account of 20.Bc5 with advantage for White.
Larsen:
(!) A very strong move. Now 19...Nb2 would be weak, since with 20.Ng5 Kg6 21.Kh1 White would attack vigorously. In this variation Black’s king would need the g8-square for retreat, so probably it would have been better to put the rook at h8 at the 15th move.
19...Na5 20.Rd7 Nc6 21.f4 Rc8 22.Rc1
Spassky:
Also good was the simple 22.Rad1 Rc7 23.Bh4 and Black can hardly save the position.
Larsen:
Probably the strongest. With the move in the game White prevents 22.Rc1, which would have been followed by 22...Rc7 23.Rc7 Nc7 24.Bc5, as well as 22...Ne7, as after 23.Re7 Rc7 24.Rc7 Nc7 White would win a pawn by 25.b4.
22...a5
Spassky:
22...Rc7 will not do in view of 23.Rc7 Nc7 24.Bc5. In the case of 22...Ne7 23.Re7 Rc7 instead, White obtains an advantage by means of 24.Rc7 Nc7 25.b4.
23.a3 Kg6
Larsen:
(?!) This move and the next five left me pressed for time. When I decided on this move by the king, I in fact that that the continuation 23...Ne7 24.Re7 Rc7 was objectively the best one, after which Black would still have good drawing prospects.
24.Kg2 Rh8
Larsen:
All things considered, 24...Ne7 was better here.
25.Kf3
Spassky:
I rejected the variation 25.Rg1 Ne7 26.Kf3 Kh6 27.Re7 Rc7 28.Rc7 Nc7 29.Nd6 Rf8 30.Nc4 Rb8 31.Rd1, considering it to be too prosaic. However, precisely this technical continuation gave White a clear advantage, sufficient for victory. Instead of this I decided to begin a combinational attack on the position of Larsen’s king, at the same time presenting my opponent with good counter-chances.
25...Rh2
Larsen:
It is better to have a worse position with an extra pawn, than a worse position without it.
26.Ng5
Spassky:
The tempting 26.Bf8 does not bring success in view of 26...Kh7 27.Rf7 Kg8 28.Ng5 Nd8!.
26...e5
Spassky:
(!)
Larsen:
In this way Black makes room for his knight and maybe for his king too.
27.Rg1 ef4
Larsen:
Is White threatening mate? No, so onward with courage! Actually, I was worried about the move that Spassky did play.
28.Bd6
Spassky:
(!) In this lies the idea of the move 25.Kf3. Now Black has to solve the problem of his king, which, taking into consideration the approaching time trouble, is a far from simple matter.
28...Nd6
Larsen:
Though I did not wish to exchange my knight for the bishop, whose passive position was my only consolation, there was no other choice. At this point I had only nine minutes left before the time control.
29.Rd6 f6 30.Ne6
30...Kf5
Spassky:
Characteristic of Larsen. Possibly, the objectively strongest continuation was 30...Kh7. But Larsen boldly meets the complications head-on.
Larsen:
At the edge of the board the king was in just as much danger as in the centre. With five minutes remaining I decided to attack. I did not see how my king could be mated, for example 31.Ng7 Ke5, since 32.Re6 (32.Rgd1? Nd4!) 32...Kd5 would be good for Black. Instead, Spassky played more strongly.
31.Nf4
Spassky:
I spent a lot of time on the calculations of the variations associated with 31.Ng7 Ke5 32.Re6 Kd5 33.Rd1 Kc4 34.Nf5 Kb3 and ultimately preferred a clear path, leading to White’s advantage.
31...Ne5
Larsen/Spassky:
Mate in 2 moves was threatened 32.e4 Ke5 33.Rd5#.
32.Ke2 Re8
Spassky:
(!) In serious time trouble Larsen conducts the defence with great skill. Bad was 32...g5 on account of 33.Nd5.
Larsen:
(?) In time pressure one notices less. I saw that 32...g5?? would have been followed by 33.Nd5 with the advantage for White, so I decided on this ‘clever’ move when I had only two minutes left for eight moves. But I really felt that my position was not so weak; only the clock made me nervous. The right continuation was 32...Nc4! and after 33.Rd5 Ke4 34.Rg7 Ne3 35.Re7 White would have to hold the draw. Another good move was 34...Re8.
33.Rb6
Spassky:
Some commentators suggested here the move 33.Ne6, having in mind the variation 33...Re6 34.e4 Ke4 35.Re6. However, White’s position is hardly sufficient for a win. After the move in the game, White retains a slight advantage.
Larsen:
(?) At this moment Spassky had eleven minutes! With 33.Ne6! White would still have some prospects, since Black must play 33...Nc4. In the ensuing rook endgame after 34.Ng7 Ke5 35.Ne8 Black would have trouble ensuring a draw.
RR: In fact, 33.Ne6? is unsound. After 33...Re6 34.e4 Black should continue, instead of 34...Ke4, 34...Kf4! when after 35.Re6 Nf3! both 36...Ng1 and 36...Nd4 are threatened. After 36.Rd1 (36.Kf1 Ng1 37.Kg1 Rh5) 36...Nd4 37.Rd4 cd4 38.Rb6 Rh3 White’s position is difficult. - Griffin.
33...Nc4 34.Rb3
Spassky:
34...Re3 was also threatened.
34...g5 35.Nd5 Re5
Larsen:
In answer to 36.Rc1 I indended to play 36...Nd6. My position was good, but the darned clock!
36.Rd1 g4
Larsen:
Suddenly another attacker has appeared. If I only had as much time as Spassky, who still had five whole minutes...
37.Kf1
Spassky:
(??) The natural move 37.Nb6 retained a slight advantage for White. The move in the game is instead a catastrophic mistake, violating the normal course of the struggle in this dramatic and rather interesting game.
Larsen:
I could not believe my eyes! Probably 37.Nb6 would secure a draw, although Black would have an interesting possibility in 37...a4!. After 38.Nc4 ab3 39.Ne5 fe5 the rook ending would perhaps be a win for Black, since the threat of a breakthrough with ...g4-g3 or ...c4-c3 would be very strong. However, after 38.Na4 g3 White’s position would be very difficult. For instance: 39.Kf3? g2
(RR: simply 39...gf2 followed by 40...Ne3 appears to win by force.- Griffin.)
40.Rbd3 Re4 with numerous threats, and 39.Rf1 would have been followed by 39...Nd6 40.Nc3 Kg4 etc.
37...Rh1 38.Ke2 Rd1
White resigned.
0 : 1
Larsen:
What really happened? I do not know how I won this game. Whenever reporters ask me who influenced me most I almost always mention Lasker and Nimzowitsch. This game would probably be closer to Lasker’s style.

Board 2
Robert Fischer – Tigran Petrosian [B 15]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.e4 c6 2.d4 d5 3.Nc3
Fischer:
I did not wish to repeat the variation I played in the first game. I have explained in my notes on that game where Black could have played better.

Petrosian:
A relatively little-studied continuation, perhaps no worse than the approved 3...de4.
4.e5
Fischer:
I think that this is a bad move. 4.h3 is probably the strongest, but I thought that Black might have some improvement, so I chose the other sequel avoiding simplification.

Fischer:
The best move, otherwise Black is hemmed in on the king-side.

In this way Black secures for himself the possibility of exchanging the c8-bishop, after which the light-squared pawn formation in the presence of the dark-squared bishop allows him to believe in the durability of his position and in the hope of creating sufficient counter-play.

22...Qa5

White cannot play 30.Kf3?? because of 30...Ne5, or if 30.Kf2, 30...Bf8 etc.

Board 3
Viktor Korchnoi – Lajos Portisch [C 90]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.0-0 Be7 6.Re1 b5 7.Bb3 d6
8.h3
Kortschnoi:
(?) Having been watching the game Geller - Gligorić, I forgot about my ‘Spanish’ bishop.
Portisch:
(?) This move is a fingerfehler. Korchnoi, who was watching the opening of the other games, automatically moved the pawn, forgetting that he had not yet played c2-c3.
8...Na5
Korchnoi:
Now Black has easy play and every chance of achieving an advantage in the middlegame.
9.Nc3 0-0 10.d3
Portisch:
In a closed position the lack of the ‘Spanish’ bishop is not so important, so it is easy to understand why Korchnoi chose this move.
10...Re8 11.Bg5
Korchnoi:
The only thing of which White can dream is to exchange as many pieces as possible, and in particular, one of the black bishops, for example 11...Bb7 12.Bf6! Bf6 13.Nd5.
However, even after White having achieved this possibility, Black would have retained the advantage - he still has the strong light-squared bishop.
11...c6
Portisch:
If 11...h6 then 12.Bf6 Bf6 13.Nd5 with an approximately equal game.
12.Ne2 h6 13.Bd2 Nb3 14.ab3 Bf8 15.Ng3 c5
Korchnoi:
Played in order not to permit d3-d4. Black ought not to have hurried with this move. More accurate is 15...g6. In the case of 16.d4, Black could reply 16...ed4 17.Nd4 Bb7, threatening ...c6-c5 and creating serious pressure on White’s centre.
16.c4
Korchnoi:
For White it is in principle favourable to close the position; after all, the strength of bishops is in open situations.
Portisch:
(!) Against the threat of ...d6-d5.
16...b4
Korchnoi:
(?) For the above-mentioned reason, the move played by Black deserves serious censure. Correct was 16...Rb8, with a minimal advantage for Black. Instead now the advantage passes to White.
Portisch:
(??) A serious positional mistake. Black blocks the queen-side and gives White a free hand on the king’s wing, where all the action will take place. Instead of this mistake, Black could maintain the tension with 16...Rb8, having at least equal prospects. Thus, for White f2-f4 would be a more uncertain follow-up than in the game, since he would have to watch out for the weak pawn at b2.
17.Nh2
Portisch:
(!)
17...Nh7 18.Ng4 Ra7 19.f4 ef4
Korchnoi:
Black had evidently intended, either now or at the following move, to play the queen to h4, but at the last moment refrained from it on account of 20.Nf5!.
20.Bf4 Nf6
Portisch:
After 20...Qh4 there would follow the very strong move 21.Nf5!.
21.Nh2 Nd7 22.Qd2 Ne5 23.Rf1 Kh7 24.Rf2 g6 25.Raf1 Bg7 26.Kh1
Portisch:
Of course White does not take the pawn with 26.Be5 since after 26...Be5 27.Rf7 Rf7 28.Rf7 Kg8 followed by ...Qg5 Black would have excellent play for his pawn.
Korchnoi:
To win a pawn by 26.Be5 Be5 27.Rf7 Rf7 28.Rf7 Kg8 29.Rf3 is not good. After 29...Qg5! Black’s game is better.
26...Ree7 27.Be3
Portisch:
(!)
27...Qh8
Korchnoi:
Black seeks the possibility of activating his pieces, but he does not succeed in doing so.
Portisch:
This manoeuvre appears to be out of place, but Black had to do something to counter the threat of d3-d4.
28.Rf4
Portisch:
(!)
Korchnoi:
The manoeuvre of the white rook has the aim of provoking a new weakening on the king’s flank.
28...Kg8 29.Rh4 h5 30.Bg5 Reb7 31.Rhf4 Nc6 32.Bf6
Portisch:
(!) Black’s most active piece is lost in an exchange, and now the square f6 will be very weak.
32...Qh6 33.Bg7 Kg7 34.Nf3 Kh8 35.h4
Korchnoi:
Possible was 35.Rf7 Qd2 36.Rf8, winning a pawn, but hardly the game, since for Black there appears strong pressure on the queen’s flank. Most energetic of all was 35.d4, opening the centre to White’s clear advantage (35...g5? 36.Rh4!).
With the move in the text White gives control of the square g4 to the black pieces makes the realisation of the advantage more complicated.
Portisch:
(!) With 35.Rf7 Qd2 36.Rf8 Kg7 37.Nd2 White might have acquired a pawn, but Black would have some compensation in 37...Be6, and what is more important, the pressure would have been released.
35...Bg4 36.Ng5 Qg7 37.Rf6 Rd7 38.Qf2 Kg8 39.Qf4 Ne5
Portisch:
39...Rac7! would have been better, since the threat of 40.Rd6 was not a serious one because of 40...Qe5!, and Black naturally gains compensation for the pawn. Naturally, White need not necessarily have taken the pawn, but since Korchnoi was running short of time, it was a good moment to try to set a trap.
RR: Had Korchnoi fallen into this ‘trap’, the game could have come to an abrupt end. After 41.Rg6! White wins immediately. - Griffin.
40.Qd2 Nc6
41.R1f2
Korchnoi:
The sealed move. White’s position is better, but to win is not a simple matter. White now intends the transfer of the knight from g3 to d5. Were he to succeed in this, he would easily achieve victory. Deserving attention too is the pawn sacrifice 41.e5 Ne5 42.d4, but it was difficult to decide on it as the sealed move, while I did not want to repeat the position, since on 41.Qf4 Ne5 42.Qd2 Black could have given up a pawn with 42...Be6!, and obtained counter-play.
Portisch:
(!) I was worried about the possibility of 41.e5!?, which would best be answered by 41...Ne5 42.d4 cd4 43.Qd4 Rac7! 44.N5e4 Be6 with complications. Likewise after 44.N3e4 Bf5 45.c5 Ng4 46.cd6 Rc8! the situation is not clear. Therefore, Korchnoi’s sealed move is better, since Black’s position remains difficult, while White can quietly prepare a decisive blow.
41...Rac7 42.Kg1 a5 43.Nf1
Portisch:
(!) White consistently carries out his plan, even at the cost of possible sacrifices.
43...Bf5
Portisch:
(?!) Under the circumstances, Black had no other choice, for otherwise he would not have been able to prevent Nf1-e3-d5.
44.R6f5 gf5 45.ef5 Ne5 46.Ne3 Rc8
Korchnoi:
Or 46...f6 47.Ne6 Qg3 48.Nd5 with a clear advantage.
47.f6 Qh6
Portisch:
Now 47...Qg6 would be followed by 48.Rf5, and Black is helpless!
48.Nf5
Korchnoi:
(?) In time-trouble haste, White hurries to regain the sacrificed exchange and loses the advantage. Correct was 48.Rf5 Re8 49.Ne4 Ng6 50.Rg5 Re5 51.Nf5 and Black is in difficulty.
Portisch:
(?) Pressed for time White, forfeits his advantage with this move. After 48.Rf5! White could have won.
48...Qg6 49.Ne7 Re7 50.fe7 Re8 51.Ne4
Korchnoi:
(?) Better was 51.d4. Now however, the advantage is gained by Black.
51...Ng4 52.Rf3 Re7 53.Qf4 Re6 54.Rf1
Portisch:
This move, too, is the result of the time scramble.
54...Ne5
Korchnoi
: (!) Black threatens 55...Nd3, and no satisfactory defence to this is apparent.
55.Nd6
Portisch:
(?) Now White obtains an unsatisfactory endgame.
In this position almost any other move would have been better.
55...Nd3 56.Qf7 Qf7 57.Nf7 Nb2 58.Nd8
Korchnoi:
White’s position is hopeless. The natural 58.Ng5 loses after 58...Re3 59.Rf3 Rf3 60.gf3 a4 61.ba4 Na4 62.Ne4 b3 63.Nd2 b2 64.Nb1 Nb6 65.Kf2 Nc4 66.Ke2 Kf7 67.Kd3 Ne5 68.Kc2 Nf3 etc. or 59.Ra1 a4 60.ba4 Ra3!.
Portisch:
If 58.Ng5 then Black would win with 58...Re3 59.Rf3 Rf3 60.gf3 a4 61.ba4 Na4.
58...Rb6
Portisch:
(!)
59.Rf5 Nd3 60.Rd5 Nc1 61.Rc5 Nb3 62.Rb5
Korchnoi:
More tenacious is 62.Rc6.
Portisch:
The prospects are no better with 62.Rg5 since after 62...Kf8 63.c5 Nc5! 64.Rc5 b3 Black’s pawns would have the final say, and the result would be the same after 62.Rc8 Kg7.
62...Rb5 63.cb5 Nc5
Portisch:
White has no defence, for 64.Nc6 would be followed by 64...b3 65.Ne7 Kf7 66.Nd5 a4 and Black’s pawns advance.
White resigned.
0 : 1
[Korchnoi/Portisch]
Board 4
Vlastimil Hort – Lev Polugaevsky [A 46]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.d4
Hort:
In this game everything went the way I did not want it do. First of all, I began 10 minutes late as I had over-slept, and after that I could not make myself concentrate. My other problem during the game was how to proceed in our match with the score of 1½:½, playing White.
I chose a variation that Dr. Trifunović often played. To my regret, I must admit that I lacked courage in this game and preferred to wait for my opponent to take the initiative.
1...Nf6 2.Nf3 e6 3.Bg5 h6 4.Bh4
Polugaevsky:
White is in a quiet mood. The principled continuation was 4.Bf6 Qf6 5.e4, with a complicated game. White has the centre, while Black has the two bishops.
The position is dynamic. Such a path has already been encountered in practice.
4...b6
Polugaevsky:
Black did not want to risk too much with 4...g5 5.Bg3 Nh5.
5.Nbd2 Bb7 6.e3 Be7 7.h3
Hort:
7...g5 along with ...g5-g4 would have been rather uncomfortable.
Polugaevsky:
White is attentive. If immediately 7.Bd3, then 7...g5 8.Bg3 g4.
7...Ne4
Hort:
This move shows that Polugaevsky was not in a fighting move either. The text move indicates that Black intends to prevent 8.Bd3.
Polugaevsky:
The simplest path to equality.
8.Be7 Qe7 9.Ne4
Polugaevsky:
Equivalent to playing for a draw. White could obtain a more complicated position by means of 9.Bd3, but this, evidently, did not come into his plans.
9...Be4 10.c3 0-0 11.Bd3 Bd3
Hort:
At this juncture Black offered a draw, saving his strength for the next game where he would have the white pieces.
Polugaevsky:
The opponents agreed on a draw. The position has been so simplified that further attempts on one of their parts could hardly have changed anything.
Agreed drawn.
½ : ½
[Hort/Polugaevsky]
Board 5
Efim Geller – Svetozar Gligorić [C 93]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
Geller:
In the present game Gligorić significantly strengthened the defence and found, in comparison with the previous games between us, perhaps the best disposition of his forces.
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.0-0 Be7 6.Re1 b5 7.Bb3 d6 8.c3 0-0 9.h3 h6 10.d4 Re8 11.Nbd2 Bf8 12.Nf1 Bb7 13.Ng3 Na5 14.Bc2 Nc4 15.b3 Nb6 16.Bb2 c5
Geller:
In this way Gligorić played against me in Sochi and this move, in my opinion, is the best in the given situation.
Gligorić:
In the 1st round Black played 16...Nbd7 at this point, but with the immediate advance of the pawns Black will feel safer on the queen-side.
17.de5 de5 18.c4
18...Nbd7
Geller:
But this move is already new. In the above-mentioned game 18...Qc7 occurred.
Gligorić:
(!) An improvement in comparison with the game between the same players in Sochi (1968), where 18...Qc7 was played.
19.Qe2
Gligorić:
After 19.cb5 ab5 20.Bd3 Qb6 21.Qe2 Bc6 Black easily protects the important squares.
19...b4 20.Rad1 Qa5
Gligorić:
(!) Gaining a tempo to transfer both rooks to the e-file.
21.Bb1 Re6
Geller:
(!)
Gligorić:
(!) An excellent manoeuvre, protecting the king’s side and at the same time lending support to the sensitive e5–pawn.
22.Nf5 Rae8 23.Ne3 Nb8
Gligorić:
It is now easy for Black to conduct the principal manoeuvre for consolidating this type of position.
24.Nd5 Nc6
Gligorić:
With the pieces deployed in this manner, the position is absolutely equal.
25.Rd2
Gligorić:
Draw. After 25...Nd4 White can adopt the same tactics with 26.Qd1.
Agreed drawn.
