Board 6
Samuel Reshevsky – Vasily Smyslov [C 98]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.e4
Reshevsky:
Though I rarely begin a game with 1.e4, this time I chose this possibility for psychological reasons, believing that it would surprise Smyslov.
1...e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 a6 4.Ba4 Nf6 5.0-0 Be7 6.Re1 b5 7.Bb3 d6 8.c3 0-0 9.h3 Na5
Smyslov:
I have had to test various systems of defence in the Spanish Game. In the present encounter I decided to refrain from the move 9...h6, since recently Reshevsky has often played this way with Black.
10.Bc2 c5 11.d4 Nc6 12.Nbd2 Qc7
Smyslov:
An ancient treatment of the variation, having its roots in the creative legacy of Chigorin.
13.dc5 dc5 14.Nf1 Be6 15.Ne3 Rad8 16.Qe2 c4
Smyslov:
This continuation had been met with several times in my practice, and evidently it guarantees Black a level game.
17.Nf5 Rfe8 18.Bg5 Nd7 19.Be7
Reshevsky:
(!) Usually 19.Ne7 is played here, the idea being to keep the two bishops. The strategy begun with the text move, though perhaps not new, is in any case rarely used.
RR: The continuation 19.Ne7 Ne7 20.a4 had been seen in the game Vasiukov - Smyslov (37th USSR Championship, Moscow 1969). - Griffin.
19...Ne7 20.Ng5
Reshevsky:
The intention, then, is to open an attack on Black’s castled position. White’s idea is to put Black on the defensive with Qe2-h5, or Qe2-g4 along with Re1-e3.
20...Ng6
Reshevsky:
The purpose of this move is to get White to move his pawn to the g3-square. After that White’s rook cannot reach the g-file! Otherwise after 20...h6 there would be some weaknesses on Black’s king-side. For instance, 21.Ne6 fe6 22.Ne3 etc.
21.g3 h6 22.Ne6 fe6 23.Ne3 Nc5
Smyslov:
The doubled pawns on the e-file reliably cover the squares d4 and d5, while the slight weakening of the pawn chain can hardly be exploited by White.
24.Rad1 Rd1 25.Rd1 Rd8 26.b4
Reshevsky:
(!) White has the superior position. The points is that Black must take on b3 and thus allow White’s bishop to become active. If Black avoids taking and plays, say, 26...Nb7, White will continue with 27.a4 and bring his bishop into play on the king’s wing. Meanwhile Black’s minor pieces will be poorly placed.
26...cb3
Smyslov:
Also deserving attention is 26...Rd1 27.Qd1 Nd7, not releasing White’s bishop into freedom.
27.Rd8 Qd8 28.ab3 Qd6 29.b4 Nd7 30.Bb3 Nf6 31.Ng4
Smyslov:
After lengthy consideration Reshevsky finds an interesting manoeuvre, the merits of which I did not appreciate during the game.
31...Ng4
Reshevsky:
(?) A losing move. 31...Nd7 should be played. Even after that White would stand better, but also a long fight ahead to take advantage of his chance.
Smyslov:
(?) This reckless exchange leads Black into great difficulties.
Correct was to reply 31...Nf8 32.Nf6 (or 32.Qf3 Ng4 33.Qg4 Kf7) 32...gf6 33.Qe3 Qc7! and Black’s position is perfectly defensible.
32.Qg4 Nf8 33.Qf5
Reshevsky:
(!) The winning move. Black is in zugzwang.
Smyslov:
(!) Here is the insidious move that places Black in a position of zugzwang. If now 33...Kh8 34.Qf7 and Black is left only with king moves. I preferred to give up a pawn and to go over to a difficult queen endgame.
33...Qc7
Reshevsky:
If 33...Kh8 34.Qf7 and Black is left in zugwang.
34.Be6 Ne6 35.Qe6 Kf8
Smyslov:
Somewhat better was 35...Kh7.
36.Qa6 Qc3 37.Qb5 Qe1 38.Kg2 Qe4 39.Kh2 Ke7 40.Qc5 Ke6 41.Qc8
41...Kf7
Smyslov:
The sealed move. If 41...Ke7, then 42.Qg4. The endgame is won for White, thanks to the strong passed pawn.
42.Qd7 Kg8 43.b5
Reshevsky:
The queen must be on d7 at the moment when White plays b4-b5 because if the queen had been at c8, Black’s queen would have gone to d4.
43...Qc2 44.Qd5 Kh7 45.Kg2 e4 46.Qd4
Reshevsky:
The winning move!
46...Kg8 47.b6 Qb3 48.Qc5
Reshevsky:
The threat is 49.Qc8 and 50.b7.
48...Qb2 49.Qc6
Smyslov:
(!) Weaker is 49.Qc8 Kh7 50.b7 e3 51.Qf5 Kg8! and Black can still hold on.
49...Kf7 50.h4 g6 51.Qc7 Ke6
Smyslov:
Or 51...Kf6 52.b7 e3 53.Qf4 and 54.b8Q.
52.Qh7 Qf6 53.Qh6 Qf3 54.Kg1
Black resigned.
1 : 0
[Reshevsky/Smyslov]
The 3rd round in progress.
Board 7
Mark Taimanov – Wolfgang Uhlmann [D 80]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 d5 4.Bg5 Ne4 5.Bh4 c5 6.cd5 Nc3 7.bc3 Qd5 8.e3
Taimanov:
Thus, the theoretical debate, begun in the duel of the first game, continues! Then, after 8...cd4 9.Qd4 Qd4 10.cd4 there arose an endgame that is clearly favourable for White. It was obvious that if Uhlmann had again gone in for this variation, he must have prepared some sort of improvement. What, then, was it?
8...Bg7 9.Qf3 Qd8
Uhlmann:
(!)
Taimanov:
Here is the answer to the question that was posed. Uhlmann avoids the exchange of queens, considering, probably, that in the middlegame Black has more active possibilities than in the endgame. I can frankly say that I expected just such a development of events and for my part had prepared a counter-surprise.
10.Bb5 Nd7 11.Ne2
11...0-0
Taimanov:
This natural move is, in my opinion, the principal cause of Black’s later difficulties. In preparation for the game I considered the preliminary exchange 11...cd4 12.ed4 (12.cd4? Qa5), and only now 12...0-0, to be obligatory.
Uhlmann:
A serious possibility was 11...cd4 12.ed4 0-0 13.0-0 Nf6, equalising.
12.0-0 a6 13.Bd3 Rb8
Taimanov:
Of course, not 13...Ne5 14.de5 Qd3 in view of 15.Be7.
14.a4 b6
Uhlmann:
14...Nf6 would have been better.
15.Rfd1 Qe8
Taimanov:
Too optimistically played. Uhlmann counts on preparing the undermining ...e7-e5, but clearly under-estimated White’s reply. He ought to have more quickly completed development with 15...Bb7, although in this case too after 16.Be4 Be4 17.Qe4 Nf6 18.Qd3 Qc8 19.Bg3 Ra8 20.Rab1 White’s position is better.
16.Be4
Taimanov:
(!) A manoeuvre that is very unpleasant for Black. Now, in view of the threat of 17.Bc6, his development is difficult and he suddenly proves to be in a dangerous situation.
16...e5
Taimanov:
(?) A consistent, but mistaken decision. He ought to have reconciled himself to a worse, but still sound position after 16...Nf6 17.Bf6 ef6 (17...Bf6 18.Bc6) 18.Bc6 Qe7 19.Nf4.
Uhlmann:
(?) The best defence was 16...Nf6 17.Bc6 Bd7 18.Bd7 Nd7 19.Qc6 with a better position for White. If White decides instead on 17.Bf6, then after 17...ef6 he again has slightly the better prospects
17.Bc6 Qe6 18.Bg3
Uhlmann:
(!)
Taimanov:
(!) A tragi-comic situation. With a full board, Black is virtually in zugzwang and does not have a satisfactory defence against the threat of 19.Bd7 Bd7 20.Be5 winning a pawn. Uhlmann thought for more than an hour in this critical situation, but he could not find a way to free himself.
18...Rd8
Uhlmann:
After 18...Re8 there follows 19.de5 and if 19...Be5 then 20.Nf4 Qe7 21.Bd7 Bd7 22.Nd5 Qe6 23.Be5 Qe5 24.Nf6 winning a piece. If Black after 20.Nf4 plays 20...Qf5 then there follows 21.e4, winning. Black is almost in zugzwang.
19.de5 Be5 20.Bd5 Qe7 21.Qf7 Qf7 22.Bf7 Kf7 23.Be5
Taimanov:
Thus, with a little combination White has won a pawn, retaining the better position. For him to win the game should now have been a matter of uncomplicated technique. But in the further course of events, much that was unexpected was still to take place...
23...Rb7 24.Bf4
Taimanov:
A logical manoeuvre. White prepares the strengthening of the centre with f2-f3 plus e3-e4.
24...Re8 25.f3 c4
Taimanov:
In the case of 25...Ne5 26.Be5 Re5 27.e4 White’s task would have been quite simple.
26.Rd4
Taimanov:
(?) Retrospectively, this move merits censure. But during the game it was difficult to hold back from it. It was very tempting to provoke the move 26...b5, while a pawn sacrifice on the part of Black seemingly did not bring him any direct benefits. All the same, he should not have been greedy. Simply 26.e4 Nc5 27.Be3 Nd3 28.Rdb1 guaranteed a slow but sure victory.
Uhlmann:
A much stronger move would have been 26.e4, possibly followed by 26...Nc5 27.Be3!, and the sacrifice of the knight with 27...Ne4 would be misjudged because of 28.fe4 Re4 29.Rf1! Kg8 (or 29...Ke8) 30.Bh6, winning.
26...Nc5
Taimanov:
(!) This move does honour to my opponent’s intuition. Uhlmann has subtle sensed the character of the position and takes the only correct decision - activation of his forces at any cost!
27.Rc4 Be6 28.Rb4 a5 29.Rb2 Bd7 30.Rba2
Taimanov:
I had confidently gone in for this position when winning the second pawn. Black’s initiative has seemingly reached a dead end and if White were to play e3-e4 and Bf4-e3 the game would be decided. Alas, it turns out that Black’s resources have still not been exhausted.
30...Nb3 31.Rd1 Nc5 32.Rd4 Ne6 33.Rc4 g5
Uhlmann:
(!)
34.Bd6 Nc5 35.Nd4
Taimanov:
Having repulsed the threat of 35...Be6, White has seemingly solved all of the problems. But all the same Uhlmann finds a possibility of counter-play!
Uhlmann:
White should have played 35.Rd4, as after 35...Re3 36.Kf2 Re8 37.Ng3 he would still have a winning position.
35...Na4
Uhlmann:
(!)
36.Rca4 Ba4 37.Ra4 Re3
Taimanov:
This position has arisen virtually by force after the win of a second pawn by White. It may be stated that the sigificant part of White’s advantage has been lost. But was it really humanly possible to have predicted this? I think that neither Uhlmann, who played this part of the game in inspired fashion, nor I, who was ‘sailing with the tide’, knew what would come from the fascinating skirmish between the wooden pieces that seemed to have taken on a life of their own.
38.Nb5 Re1 39.Kf2 Rb1 40.c4
Taimanov:
(?) While this is already an obvious mistake. This is not accidental.
When the situation on the board has unexpectedly changed, a chessplayer often loses confidence. Chances of victory, although already problematic, were retained only by the move 40.Nd4.
40...Rd7
Taimanov:
Now 41...Rb5 is threatened, and White is forced to open new highways for the black rooks.
41.Be5 Rd2 42.Ke3
Taimanov:
42.Kg3 will not do, in view of 42...Rg1 43.Kg4 Kg6!.
Uhlmann:
If 42.Kg3 then 42...Rg1 and White might have serious difficulties.
42...Rg2 43.Ke4 Rb4
Uhlmann:
(!)
Taimanov:
(!) A correct decision. Although Uhlmann goes in for the exchange of an active rook for a passive one, he creates a dangerous passed pawn that restricts the activity of the white pieces.
44.Rb4 ab4 45.Nd4 Ke7
Taimanov:
Again an excellent plan. There is only one white enemy left, the c4-pawn which could gain in strength it its opponent, the pawn at b6, were to fall. Therefore Uhlmann brings up the king, preventing the manoeuvre Ke4-d5-c6.
46.Bc7
Taimanov:
It is clear that on 46.Kd5 there would have followed 46...Kd7, while on 46.Nc6 Ke6 is sufficiently good.
46...Kd7 47.Bb6
47...b3
Taimanov:
(!) Again, very resourcefully played! In the case of the automatic 47...Rh2, Black could still have experienced difficulties
48.Nb3 Rb2
Taimanov:
Now a slight material advantage passes to the side of Black. Admittedly, it proves insufficient.
49.Be3
Uhlmann:
After 49.Nc5 Kc6 50.Ba7 Ra2 White secures a draw with 51.Ne6.
49...Rb3 50.Bg5 Rc3 51.Bh6 Rc4 52.Kf5
Taimanov:
White’s king rushes towards the h7-pawn - Black’s only reserve. It is difficult to hinder this.
52...Rc2 53.h4 Rf2 54.f4 Ke8 55.Kg5 Rg2 56.Kh5 Rg6 57.Bg5 Kf7 58.f5 Rc6 59.f6 Ke6 60.Kh6
Taimanov:
The aim is achieved and a draw is unevitable.
60...Rc7 61.Be3
Taimanov:
This sealed move underlines most convincingly of all White’s defensive possibilities. After 61...Kf6 62.Bd4 Kf5 63.h5! the black rook is tied to the defence of the h7-pawn, while the king cannot take this function, since it cannot cross the 7th rank without loss. For example, with the position of the black rook at f7 and the king at e7, there follows the move Bg7 and the h7-pawn cannot be defended. Therefore without resuming play the opponents agreed on a draw.
Uhlmann:
After 61...Kf6 62.Bd4 Kf5 63.h5 (not immediately 63.Bg7 because of 63...Rg7 64.Kg7 h5) there is no win for Black.
½ : ½
[Taimanov/Uhlmann]
Board 8
Milan Matulović – Mikhail Botvinnik [B 08]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.e4 g6 2.d4 Bg7 3.Nf3 d6 4.Nc3 Nf6 5.Be2 0-0 6.0-0 Bg4
Matulović:
At this point in the Belgrade ‘Liberation’ tournament (1969) Botvinnik played 6...Nc6, hoping to complicate the situation. The text move is more usual and sounder.
Botvinnik:
Here Black usually plays 6...c6 or 6...Nc6.
7.Bg5
Matulović:
I had experience of this move, as Polugaevsky used it against me in the 1968 Olympiad in Lugano.
7...Nc6 8.h3
Matulović:
It seemed to me that this was a better follow-up than 8.Re1, which Polugaevsky played, since he did not gain any advantage.
Botvinnik:
During the game it seemed to me that 8.Qd2 is stronger, since after the move in the text Black manages to establish control over the centre.
8...Bf3 9.Bf3 Nd7 10.Ne2 h6 11.Be3
Matulović:
11.Bh4 gives White nothing as Black could, with 11...g5! 12.Bg3 e6 and the threat of ...f7-f5, gain an excellent game.
11...e5 12.c3 Nb6 13.b3
Botvinnik:
(?) After this, Black acquires a slight initiative. Stronger was 13.Qc1 Kh7 14.d5 Ne7 15.c4 and, in view of the fact that Black is without a light-squared bishop, his natural manoeuvre ...f7-f5 is insufficiently strong.
13...d5
Matulović:
A brilliant thrust in the centre, made possible by White’s passive opening play. Black has no problems, and he now takes over the initiative.
14.ed5 Nd5 15.Bd5
Botvinnik:
Convinced that the opening advantage has been lost, Matulović aims at simplification.
15...Qd5 16.de5
Matulović:
16.c4 Qa5 17.d5 Ne7 18.Qd2 would have left things equal.
16...Qe5 17.Nd4
Matulović:
White misjudges, convinced that the position is balanced. A better line would have been 17.Qc2 Rad8 18.Rad1, equalising.
17...Qd5
Botvinnik:
The queen should defend the c6-knight.
18.Qc1 Kh7 19.Rd1 Rad8 20.Qc2 Rfe8
Matulović:
Black develops steadily, but the position is too simplified for this to yield results.
21.Rd2
Botvinnik:
Better was 21.Nc6 Qc6 22.Rd8 Rd8 23.Rc1 followed by c3-c4, and the position is level.
21...Nd4 22.Bd4 Bd4 23.Rd4 Qe5
24.Rad1
Matulović:
This careless move induces Botvinnik to play to win, and this game becomes the longest in the whole match. Now Black without difficulty creates an isolated pawn at d4, which will be the source of all White’s troubles. After 24.Rd2!, threatening 25.Rad1, the position would be equalised, with a draw the only possible result.
24...Rd4
Botvinnik:
Black obtains a slight advantage, since for a weak white pawn appears on the d4-square.
25.cd4
Matulović:
White had overlooked the fact that 25.Rd4 would be followed by 25...Qe1 26.Kh2 Re2, and White would have to resign.
Botvinnik:
25.Rd4 Qe1 26.Kh2 Re2 will not do.
25...Qe2 26.Rd2
Matulović:
There is a saying that all rook endgames end in a draw. I was afraid, however, that this might be the exception. After 26.Qe2 Re2 27.Rc1 c6 28.d5! cd5 29.Rc7 White’s active rook would most probably ensure a equal endgame.
26...Qe1 27.Kh2 c6 28.Qc4
Matulović:
White tries a few tricks to activate his pieces, and is succesful for a while.
28...Re7 29.Rd3 Kg7
Botvinnik/Matulović:
Bad was 29...Qf2 on account of 30.Rf3.
30.Rf3 Rd7 31.Qc5 a6 32.Rf4 Rd5 33.Qc2
Matulović:
(?) A serious mistake which, owing to the opponent’s time pressure, passed unpunished. White should have waited with 33.Qc4 to see what Black intended to do.
33...Qe6
Matulović:
It is interesting that Botvinnik did not notice the possibility of 33...Rd4!, leading to the win of a pawn and obviously to a winning position. Now after 34.Rd4 there follows 34...Qe5, and after 34.Qb2 c5 35.Rd4 Qe5 etc.
Botvinnik:
Interestingly, neither Black nor White had seen that best of all now is 33...Rd4 34.Rd4 Qe5.
34.g3 g5
Botvinnik:
Black aims to secure for himself the f6-square.
35.Re4 Qf6 36.h4
Matulović:
Now White definitively overlooks the loss of a pawn, and a difficult struggle lies ahead. After 36.Qb2 c5 (otherwise White plays 37.Re5) 37.Re5! Rd4 38.Rc5 there would be a draw.
Botvinnik:
White once again commits a mistake. Possible was 36.Qb2 c5 37.Re5! and the pawn is saved.
36...Rd4 37.hg5 hg5 38.Rd4 Qd4 39.Qf5 Qd5 40.Qc8 c5 41.a4
Matulović:
In a difficult position White seizes a chance, blockading Black’s pawn on the queen’s side, and thereby considerably improving his position.
41...Kf6
Botvinnik:
The sealed move. At the same time, it is a mistake, greatly complicating the path to victory. Simplest of all was 41...b6 followed by ...Qb3 and Black wins without difficulty.
42.a5 Qf5
Matulović:
The king’s attempted break through with 42...Ke5 cannot succeed because of 43.Qe8 and the king has to withdraw, since 43...Kd4 leads to mate with 44.Qe3 and 43...Qe6 would be followed by 44.Qb8. The reduction in material favours Black.
Botvinnik:
Nor is anything promised by 42...Ke5 43.Qe8 Kd6 44.Qd8 Kc6 45.Qe8 Qd7 46.Qe4 with equality.
43.Qb7
Botvinnik:
This and Black’s reply opens the position of the defending king, which is always dangerous in queen endgames.
43...Qf2 44.Kh3 Qf1 45.Kh2 Qe2
Botvinnik:
If 45...g4, then 46.Qc6 Kg7 47.Qd6 with equality.
46.Kh3 Qe6 47.Kh2 Qe2 48.Kh3 Kg6 49.Qb6
Matulović:
White has moved his pawn to a5, and as a result there is some hope of salvation. For example, ...Qe6 is no longer a threat, as will be seen later in the course of this endgame.
49...Kh5 50.Qc5 Qe6
Botvinnik:
Stronger was 50...Qf1 51.Kh2 f5 with active play for Black. The exchange of pawns at the b3-square leads to an elementary draw.
51.Kg2 Qb3 52.Qf5
Matulović:
The position is objectively drawn. White has only one thing to worry about, namely, not to permit an unfavourable exchange of queens. And yet for another 40 moves Botvinnik tried to win the game.
Botvinnik:
(!) Ultimately, Black will try to improve the position of his pieces, but this is not a simple matter, since his queen is forced to defend the pawns at a6 and f6, while in the case of the exchange of the pawn at f6 for the pawn at g4 a drawn position is obtained.
52...Qa2 53.Kf3 Qa3 54.Kg2 Qa2 55.Kf3 Qb3 56.Kg2 Qb7 57.Kh2 Qb2 58.Kh3 Qg7 59.Qc5 Qf6 60.g4
Matulović:
White has an ideal position. The king will defend the g4-pawn and the queen, which has an outpost at b6, the a5-one. To revive his chances Black must advance his king to the middle of the board, exposing it to checks.
60...Kh6 61.Qb6 Kg7 62.Kg2 Qe6 63.Qd4 f6 64.Qb4 Qd5 65.Kf2 Kf7 66.Qb6 Qc4 67.Kg3 Ke7 68.Kh3 Qf1 69.Kg3 Qd3
Botvinnik:
After 69...Kf7, in view of the weak position of the black queen, White pursues the black king over the whole board.
70.Kg2 Qe4
Botvinnik:
Nothing is given by 70...Qd6 71.Qb7 Ke6 72.Qe4 and the black king cannot pass.
71.Kg3 Qf4 72.Kh3 Qf1
Matulović:
After 72...Qf3 73.Kh2 Qg4 74.Qa6 White’s a-pawn becomes more dangerous than both black ones.
73.Kg3 Qe1 74.Kg2 Qd2 75.Kh3 Qd3 76.Kg2 Qd5
77.Kh3
Botvinnik:
A mistake, which almost costs White defeat. Better was 77.Kf1.
77...Qh1 78.Kg3 Qh4 79.Kf3 Qh3 80.Kf2 Qh2 81.Kf1 Qf4 82.Ke1 Qg3 83.Kf1 Qf4 84.Ke1 Qd6
Botvinnik:
Thanks to the position of the king at e1, this manoeuvre is now possible, since at the last moment the check at e3 would lead to the exchange of queens.
85.Qb7 Ke6 86.Qb3 Ke5 87.Qe3 Kd5 88.Qd3 Kc5 89.Qa3
Botvinnik:
The only possible check. White’s basic task is to prevent the black king from approaching the white a-pawn.
89...Kc6 90.Qa4 Kd5 91.Qb3
Matulović:
After this move Black cannot move to d4 because of 92.Qb6!.
Botvinnik:
Most accurate of all was 91.Qa2 Kd4 92.Qb2 (now on 92.Qd2 there is 92...Kc5) 92...Kc4 93.Qb6.
91...Ke4 92.Qc4
Botvinnik:
Matulović finds an ingenious path to the draw.
92...Kf3
Matulović:
If 92...Ke3 then 93.Qe2 Kd4 94.Qb2 Kc4 95.Qb6 etc. Also leading to a draw is 93.Qc3 Qd3 (93...Kf4 94.Qc4 Kg3 95.Qd3!) 94.Qe5 Kf3 95.Qf6 Kg4 96.Qe6.
93.Qd3
Agreed drawn.
½ : ½
[Botvinnik/Matulović]
Amicable conversation: Ivkov, Gligorić, Spassky, Keres and Najdorf, Belgrade.
Board 9
Mikhail Tal – Miguel Najdorf [B 80]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 cd4 4.Nd4 e6 5.Nc3 Qc7 6.g3
Najdorf:
There is a trap hidden in this move. If Black is not careful and plays 6.g3 Nf6? it would be followed with 7.Bf4! and 8.Ndb5.
6...a6
Tal:
A quiet continuation, after which, however, it is not so simple for Black to obtain the active counter-play characteristic of the Sicilian Defence. Recently this variation has become more popular (in particular, it occurred many times in the most recent USSR Championship), and for White there have been found new possibilities of fighting for an advantage. As was shown as long ago as the game Fischer - Tal (Bled, 1961), a serious mistake here is 6...Nf6 7.Ndb5 Qb8 8.Bf4 with a clear advantage.
7.Bg2 Nf6 8.0-0 d6
Tal:
The most accurate. Weaker here is 8...Be7 9.Re1! d6 10.Nc6 bc6 11.e5! de5 12.Re5 0-0 13.Bf4 and impossible is 13...Bd6 14.Re6!.
9.Re1 Bd7
Tal:
In this lies the point of the previous move. Now White does not break through immediately in the centre.
Najdorf:
After 9...Be7 I was afraid of 10.Nc6 bc6 11.e5 de5 12.Re5. And after 9...Nd4 10.Qd4 Be7 11.e5 de5 12.Re5 the position did not appeal to me (12...Bd6 13.Bf4! Be5 14.Be5 Qe7 15.Rd1 etc.).
10.Nc6
Tal:
Initiating play on the queen’s flank. In the case of 10...Bc6 the leap of the knight to d5 must be reckoned with, but the capture with the pawn creates some objects for attack.
10...bc6
Najdorf:
It was hard to decide. If I had continued with 10...Bc6 by opponent would no doubt have answered with 11.Nd5.
What should one say about the position after 11...Bd5 12.ed5 e5 ? The follow-up 13.f4 Be7 14.Qe2 0-0 15.fe5 de5 16.Qe5 Qe5 17.Re5 Bc5 18.Kh1 Ng4 very likely would not be favourable for White, but simply 13.Be3 followed by 14.Rc1 would give White a slight positional advantage. Accordingly, I decided on the text, despite its shortcomings.
11.Na4
Tal:
White’s plan is simple - to try to open lines with the advance of the c-pawn. Unexpectedly for me the Argentinian grandmaster did not prevent this and soon ran into serious difficulties.
Najdorf:
A thrust aimed at Black’s c-pawn.
11...e5
Tal:
Matulović plays 11...Rb8 here, on which I intended to play 12.f4. Clearly, 12.c4 does not then achieve its aim on account of the simple 12...c5.
Najdorf:
It goes without saying that 11...c5 would not be good because of 12.e5, although Tal considers that 11...Rb8 was called for. (If 12.c4 then 12...c5.)
12.c4 Be7
Tal:
Already here 12...c5 was necessary. In this case White’s positional advantage (the square d5) would have been insignificant.
Najdorf:
Actually I should not have permitted the c-pawn to advance. The right move was 12...c5 but even after that White is better off.
13.c5
Najdorf:
(!)
13...0-0
Tal:
The complications after 13...d5 14.ed5 cd5 (if 14...Nd5, then 15.Bd5 cd5 16.Nb6!) 15.Nb6 Rd8 16.Nd5 are favourable for White. But now there is created a position with a rather large and stable advantage. There no compensation for the weaknesses on the queen’s flank in Black’s position. The next moves are readily understood.
Najdorf:
If 13...d5 then 14.ed5 cd5 15.Nb6.
14.cd6 Bd6 15.Bg5
Najdorf:
Why didn’t White play 15.Be3? In any case I could have returned the bishop to e7. Thus, Black later gains a tempo with ...h7-h6. The freeing of the square h7 is important, because of the possibility of the manoeuvre ...Nf6-h7-g5.
15...Be7 16.Qc2 h6 17.Be3 Rab8 18.Rac1 Rfd8
19.h3
Tal:
An unecessary move, not only losing time but also presenting Black with an object for counter-play.
Its root cause was indecisiveness. I was completely unable to settle my choice on one of the two equally tempting plans - 19.Nc5 or 19.Bc5, and then decided to leave the square c5 for the knight, and preliminarily ‘insured myself’ against the possible ...Ng4.
Stronger, of course, was the immediate 19.Bc5, and it is very difficult for Black to defend. Najdorf immediately exploits the respite.
Najdorf:
A completely useless move, and the pawn at h3 is exposed to danger in certain variations.
19...Nh7
Najdorf:
A manoeuvre by which Black transfers the knight to e6, which is important for the defence of the vulnerable square at c5, and for possible activity at d4 on the part of the knight.
20.Bc5
Tal:
The manoeuvre ...Nh7-f8-e6(-d4) would have permitted Black to consolidate. Already now White has no objection to simplification, but at some point the relatively undefended state of the h3-pawn can get in his way. Some clear moves follow.
20...Be8 21.Red1 Rd1
Najdorf:
Otherwise White exchanges at d8 and Black cannot recapture with his rook on account of 23.Bb6.
22.Rd1 Ng5 23.Be7 Qe7 24.Nc5
24...Ne6
Tal:
For Black there was an interesting possibility - to first play 24...Rb5! and only after 25.Rc1 (25.Na6 Ne6 or 25...Ra5) 25...Ne6 26.Ne6 Qe6 27.Bf1 Rb4 and the black rook is very actively place, since there is not the move 28.Bc4 on account of 28...Qh3. Evidently, in this way Black could have equalised the position and demonstrated that in chess, even the slightest mistake is punishable. Bypassing this possibility, Black is later forced to carry out a difficult, thankless defence.
Najdorf:
(?) A much better move was 24...Rb5 and after 25.Rc1 Ne6 26.Ne6 Qe6 Black would have been in a much better position than in the game. Had White continued 25.Na6, Black would have recovered the pawn with 25...Ra5.
25.Ne6 Qe6 26.b3
Najdorf:
The material on the battlefield has been somewhat reduced in the exchanges, but this does not do much for Black, who must still attend to his weak pawns on the queen’s wing. Black’s defence is extremely difficult.
26...Qe7
Najdorf:
Thus Black takes c5 from his opponent’s queen and at the same time prepares for the exchange of rooks at d8.
27.Qc3
Najdorf:
(!) An exceptionally strong move. First of all, the exchange of rooks is prevented (27...Rd8 28.Rd8 Qd8 29.Qe5 etc.), while at the same time there is the threat of 28.Qa5. If Black tries to re-organise his defence with 27...Qc7, this would be followed by 28.Bf1 a5 29.Rd5!.
27...Rb4
Tal:
An excellent resource. Black cannot permit the queen to a5, while in the case of 27...Qc7 possible is 28.Bf1 a5 29.Rd5! winning a pawn. It is unfavourable for White to now advance 28.a3 on account of 28...Rd4!. The immediate attempt to exploit the weakness on the 8th rank - 28.Rd3 a5 29.Qd2 (here on 29.a3 possible is 29...Rb5) did not give anything real after 29...a4. Having thought for around a half-hour, White found, as it seems to me, the most effective plan.
Najdorf:
Probably best, since White’s bishop is tied to the defence of the e4-pawn.
28.h4
Tal:
Since Black is tied to the defence of the weak a- and c-pawns, White begins to harrass the king and, what is most important, includes his light-squared bishop in the game.
28...f6
Tal:
Probably the most tenacious of all was the passive 28...h5.
Najdorf:
With 28...a5 with the intention of 29...a4 Black would have had better chances. If White continued with 29.a3, the follow-up would be 29...Rb8 with an attack on the a3-pawn, while 30.Qa5 would be followed by 30...Rb3 31.Rd8 Kf8 and Black is not exposed to any danger, although White would still have the better prospects with his passed a-pawn.
29.Rd3
Tal:
Now, with a ‘live’ bishop, White’s possible attack along the d-file is very dangerous.
29...Kh7
Tal:
The Argentinian thought for a long time, and giving White the possibility to activate himself still further, also tried to give his pieces air to breathe.
It is clear that White’s following move, based on a tactical finesse, was a ‘rebuke’ for him.
Najdorf:
Now 29...a5 would no longer be feasible, because the position after 30.a3 Rb5 31.Bf1 Qa3 32.Rd8 Qe7 33.Ra8 would not have been very comfortable.
30.Bh3
Najdorf:
The e4-pawn is indirectly protected.
30...Bg6
Najdorf:
Already tired from being continuously on the defensive, I chose a line which in fact precipitated a favourable outcome for White. Anyway, it would have been impossible to continue the game for long with passive defence.
31.Rd7 Qf8 32.Qc6
Tal:
Seeingly the most convincing. White wins a pawn, obtaining two connected passed pawns on the queen’s flank, while his king feels sufficiently secure.
32...Re4
Najdorf:
Even 32...Be4 would not offer counter-play after 33.Qa6.
33.Qa6 Re1 34.Kh2 f5
Tal:
34...Be4 35.Bg2 is not dangerous for White. I think that 34...h5 gave Black greater chances.
Najdorf:
34...e4 would be followed by 35.h5! Bh5 36.Qa5. 34...h5 would be slightly better, though after 35.Rd2 Black would have no counter-chances, and the two passed pawns on the queen’s wing would decide the game in White’s favour.
35.Rd6
Tal:
Also good was the immediate 35.Qa5 Re2 36.Rd2. For the time being I set a little trap, into which Black falls. If Black had replied 35...Qf7, I would probably have returned to simplifying lines.
Najdorf:
With a few powerful moves my opponent forces a decision.
35...Bh5
Najdorf:
Nor would 35...Qf7 be any better because of 36.Qa5 and 37.Rd2.
36.Qd3
Tal:
It now becomes clear that White’s attack unfolds more rapidly.
36...e4 37.Qd5 Bg4
Tal:
Losing too is 37...Bf3 38.Bf5 (also possible is 38.Qf5 Qf5 39.Bf5 g6 40.Bg6 Kg7 41.g4) 38...Kh8 39.g4 Rh1 40.Kg3 Rg1 41.Kf4 Rg4 42.Ke3.
Najdorf:
A final attempt would be 37...Bf3 but after 38.Bf5 Kh8 39.g4 Rh1 40.Kg3 Rg1 41.Kf4 Rg4 42.Ke3 Black could not defend himself against the threat of 43.Rd8.
38.Rd8 Qf6 39.Qg8
Tal:
Zeitnot, zeitnot... White could not hold back from the temptation to cross the 40–move frontier with checks. At this point he still had around 3 minutes remaining. Probably, this was enough in order for him to have found the immediately winning continuation 39.h5! Bh5 40.Bf5.
Najdorf:
(?) A small error in a winning position. After 39. h5! Bh5 40.Bf5 Black could resign.
39...Kg6 40.Qe8 Kh7 41.Bg4
Tal:
Here, unfortunately, 41.h5 is less clear on account of 41...Bh5!. However, it is obvious that the position is completely winning and White chooses the path that is safest in practice.
41...fg4 42.Qg8 Kg6 43.Rf8
Najdorf:
I sealed 43...Qe7, but I did not continue the game, because 44.Re8 wins easily.
43...Qe7
Tal:
The sealed move. Black resigned, without resuming play.
This decision is quite understandable: 44.Re8 Qd7 45.Re6 Kf5 46.Ra6 etc.
1 : 0
[Najdorf/Tal]
Board 10
Borislav Ivkov – Paul Keres [D 34]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.Nf3 d5 2.c4 e6 3.d4 c5
Keres:
The Tarrasch Defence once again came into fashion after the World Championship match Petrosian - Spassky (1969). It is perfectly correct, and fully merits having been saved from oblivion.
4.cd5 ed5 5.g3 Nc6 6.Bg2 Nf6 7.0-0 Be7 8.Nc3 0-0 9.dc5
Ivkov:
The Petrosian - Spassky games have shown that the best move for White is 9.Bg5.
Keres:
After this exchange White cannot count on any advantage. Undoubtedly, the continuation recommended by theory, 9.Bg5, is better.
9...Bc5 10.Bg5
Ivkov:
(?) This move costs White his initial advantage. 10.Na4 should have been played.
Keres:
If White plays 10.Na4 Black can, with the reply 10...Bb6 11.Bg5 d4, obtain a satisfactory game.
10...d4
Ivkov:
(!) So, the square d4, which is usually in White’s possession, has been lost to Black.
11.Bf6 Qf6 12.Ne4
RR: Later, in the tournament at Geneva in 1977, the line 12.Nd5 Qd8 13.Nd2 was introduced here by Jan Timman, against none other than Borislav Ivkov. It subsequently became one of the main battlegrounds in this opening. - Griffin.
12...Qe7 13.Nc5 Qc5
14.Qd2
Ivkov:
14.Rc1 could have been played right away, but I thought during the game that the queen’s move was better, since it prevents the move of the bishop to f5 or e6. After 14...Bf5 15.Qg5 would be uncomfortable, and 15.b4 might also follow. Therefore Black’s bishop is best posted at g4.
14...Bg4
Ivkov:
(!)
Keres:
On 14...Bf5 the reply 15.Qg5 could be unpleasant.
RR: - Nonetheless, a few weeks after the present game, against Bobotsov (match Bulgaria v. Estonian SSR, Tallinn 1970), Keres preferred this continuation. White continued instead 15.Rac1 Qb6 16.Rfd1 Rad8 17.Qf4 Bg6 18.Rd2 , etc. - Griffin.
15.Rac1 Qb6 16.Ng5
Ivkov:
(!?) Risky, as it is quite clear that an attack on Black’s king would not be very profitable. The move 16.Ng5 can only be ascribed to an attempt to transfer the knight to c5.
Keres:
An interesting idea with a double threat. White intends the transfer of the knight via e4 to c5 and simultaneously prepares operations on the king’s flank.
16...Rad8
Ivkov:
(!)
Keres:
This move leads to great complications, which Black need not fear. However, continuing 16...h6 17.Ne4 Rfe8 18.Rfe1 Rad8 19.Nc5 Rd6, Black could retain a good game.
RR: In fact, the play so far had proceeded as in Ivkov’s game v. Minev (14th Olympiad, Leipzig 1960). That encounter continued instead 16...h6 17.Ne4 Rad8(?!) 18.Nc5 Bc8 19.Rc4 Rfe8 20.Rfc1, etc. - Griffin.
17.Qf4
Ivkov:
17.Ne4 might have been followed by 17...d3 (as well as by 17...Rfe8). However, another interesting possibility was 17.Qd3 g6 18.Ne4 Bf5 19.Qd2.
Keres:
The continuation 17.Qd3 g6 18.Ne4 Bf5 19.Qd2 promised less on account of 19...Be4 20.Be4 Ne5 etc.
17...Bh5 18.Qh4
Keres:
The sacrifice of the knight 18.Nh7 is not dangerous on account of the reply 18...Rfe8, although the acceptance of the sacrifice 18...Kh7 19.Qh4 g6 20.g4 Kg7 21.gh5 Rh8 also secures a satisfactory game for Black.
18...Bg6 19.Be4
19...Ne5
Ivkov:
(!!) I had in particular studied the moves of the knight to b4 and e7, as well as the rather dangerous move 19...h6, which lead White in all variations to the better position. However, with 19...Ne5! Black turns the whole situation to his advantage.
Keres:
(!) Undoubtedly the best reply. Bad was 19...Be4 on account of 20.Qe4 g6 21.Qh4, while the complications provoked by 19...h6 20.Bg6 fg6 21.Ne6 d3! 22.Nf8 Nd4!, although at first sight leading to a more satisfactory game for Black, all the same do not give a completely clear picture, on account of the sudden reply 23.Ne6! g5 24.Qe4 etc. After the move in the text White, continuing 20.Bg6 Qg6 21.Qf4 Rfe8 22.Nf3, could achieve equality, but he was evidently not satisfied with this.
20.Nh7
Ivkov:
(?!) I deliberately chose this line, with its difficult complications, instead of playing 20.Bg6 Qg6 21.Qf4 Nc6! with a somewhat inferior position.
20...Rfe8
Keres:
On 20...Be4 White replies with the simple 21.Qe4.
21.Ng5
Keres:
White has no choice, since on 21.Bg6 Qg6 22.Ng5 unpleasant is 22...d3!, and in general the loss of a piece is threatened. Black could now, continuing 21...Be4 22.Qe4 Ng6 23.Qf5 Qf6!, transpose to a favourable endgame, but he hoped to extract more from this position.
21...d3 22.ed3 f5
Ivkov:
22...Nd3 was also quite good.
Keres:
Also deserving attention is the simple move 22...Nd3, since after the exchange, the bishop would have proved to be stronger than the knight.
23.Bg2
Ivkov:
It was difficult to make up my mind between this move and 23.Bh1, after which I would have had to watch my first rank very carefully. After the text move White’s queen will be out of the game for quite some time.
23...Rd4 24.Qh3 Nd3
25.Nf3
Ivkov:
(!?) 25.Rc4 would not have worked because of 25...Nf2.
Keres:
(?) After this mistake, Black obtains a positional advantage. It was necessary to continue 25.Rc2!, after which before Black there arose the difficult task of increasing his positional superiority. For example, after 25...Ne1 26.Rc1 Rd2 White has the move 27.Bd5! etc., whereas for the attempt 25...f4 White has an antidote: 26.Rc8. The exchange of rooks (25.Rc4) will not do, on account of the tactical possibility 25...Nf2! etc.
25...Nc1
Ivkov:
This is the simplest, although in the time scramble I was more anxious about the retreat of the rook along the d-file.
26.Nd4 Qd4 27.Rc1 Re2 28.Rf1
Keres:
After 28.Rc8 Kf7 29.Rc7 Ke6 White would have been unable to defend the f2-square.
28...Rb2
Ivkov:
Black has recovered the sacrifice material with a divident and White’s only hope lies in the slightly weakened position of Black’s king.
29.Bf3 Ra2
Ivkov:
On 29...b5 there is 30.Bh5!.
30.Bb7 a5 31.Qg2
Ivkov:
White’s queen finally has some breathing space. However, his position is still critical.
31...Bf7 32.Qf3 g6
Ivkov:
This is perhaps Black’s first slip in a chain of mistakes.Keres: Thanks to the strong passed pawn Black now has a clear advantage, and before White there arises the difficult task of neutralising this advantage, preventing the advance of the a-pawn.
33.h4
Ivkov:
(!) At long last, White is threatening something.
33...Ra1
Ivkov:
Too cautious. It might have been possible to try 33...Qe5 and the advance of the a-pawn.
34.Ra1 Qa1 35.Kh2
Ivkov:
35.Kg2 Qe5 36.Bc6? a4! was weak.
35...Qe5
Ivkov:
Or 35...Qd4 36.h5 Qh8 37.Kg2.
Keres:
After the game, Ivkov suggested 35...Qd4, and on 36.h5 gh5 37.Qf5 a4, with the further advance of the pawn. In mutual time trouble, Black did not want to advance his king too much. Also deserving attention is the immediate 35...a4.
36.Bc6 Kg7
Ivkov:
(?) Unnecessary, to say the least. Black might have tried 36...Qc5.
37.Qa3 f4
Keres:
(?) With the exchange of pawns Black loses his prospects of a win. Much better was 37...Qe6 with the idea of 38...Qa2. White would have had to avoid the exchange of queens, since in the bishop endgame he loses.
38.Qa4 fg3
Ivkov:
On 38...Qc5 there is 39.Kg2.
Keres:
38...Qc5 did not promise much, on account of 39.Kg2!.
39.fg3 Qb2 40.Bg2 Qc3
Keres:
This was the final move before the time control. Black could have retained some winning prospects, playing 40...Qa2 41.Qd4 Kg8.
41.Qb5
Ivkov:
(!)
41...Qc7
Ivkov:
This was the sealed move, and I must admit that it was the only one that I did not pay attention to. However, Black can play whatever he wants in this position, since a win is no longer possible due to the open position on the king’s side.
Keres:
The sealed move, after which it becomes clear that a draw is inevitable. Greater prospects were promised by 41...Qd2 with the possible 42...Qa2.
42.h5
Ivkov:
(!) The most important move for White’s defence: by sacrificing a pawn he clears the way in front of Black’s king, which has no shelter from the repeated checks.
Keres:
(!) The most simple way of undermining all of Black’s plans. However, perfectly sufficient was 42.Qb2 Kh7 43.Be4 Kg8 44.Qd4!, or 42...Kg8 43.Qd4 etc.
42...gh5 43.Qg5 Kf8 44.Qh6 Ke8 45.Bc6 Ke7 46.Qg5 Kd6 47.Bg2
Ivkov/Keres:
(!)
47...Kd7 48.Qf6
Ivkov:
(!) It is not necessary for White to force a truce, i.e. to check repeatedly.
At this point, it would even be a mistake to play 48.Bh3 Ke8 or 48.Qb5 Ke7 49.Qg5 Ke8 50.Qe3 Qe7 51.Bc6 Kf8 52.Qh6 Kg8, and he would have been unable to check any more. After 48.Qf6 Keres saw that he could not improve his position and offered a draw.
Keres:
(!) On account of the shaky situation of the black king, all further attempts by Black are in vain. This was the end of this fighting game, one that did not lack interest.
½ : ½
[Najdorf/Tal]
ROUND 4
Saturday 4th April, 1970
In an exceptionally tense final round, the USSR team managed to draw the match, to bring them victory in the overall contest by the narrowest of margins.
On top board Larsen faced Leonid Stein, who was standing in for Spassky on account of the latter’s indisposition. The Soviet grandmaster at first equalised in a King’s Indian Defence after some slightly inaccurate play by White, but then chose the wrong continuation at the 14th move. Larsen steered his way advantageously through the enormous complications that resulted, and emerged with the better endgame, which he duly converted to a win.
On second board, Petrosian gained a slight but persistent advantage with the white pieces against Fischer’s Grünfeld Defence. However, his American opponent displayed perfect defensive technique, remaining well within the margin of safety throughout the game. A draw was eventually agreed after the 52nd move.
On board 3 Korchnoi again found himself in difficulties straight out of the opening – an English - against his Hungarian opponent. After missing a clear win at the 19th move, Portisch (White) agreed to a draw via a repetition of moves in a position where he had the exchange for a pawn with questionable compensation for Black. He was short of time and later explained that he was content to win his ‘micro-match’ v. his opponent.
On the fourth board, in contrast to his play in the 2nd round, Hort chose a classical defence against Polugaevsky’s queen’s pawn opening. However, he again failed to equalise, and a draw was agreed at the 36th move only after the Soviet grandmaster had missed several promising continuations.
Gligorić v. Geller on board 5 was another King’s Indian Defence. On this occasion the Yugoslav grandmaster managed to achieve a significant advantage, and by move 30 it seemed that he was on course to record the win. However, he subsequently went wrong and by the time the game was adjourned, his winning chances had disappeared. A draw was agreed without resumption.
On board 6, Smyslov scored what was to prove a decisive victory against Friðrik Ólafsson, who was standing in for Reshevsky. He gained a clear advantage from the opening – a Catalan – and was a piece for a pawn up at the adjournment. After the resumption, despite the enormous pressure – this was the last game to finish – he confidently overcame the technical difficulties to win the game and secure victory in the match overall.
On the next board, Taimanov proved to be out of sorts against Uhlmann, and committed a series of oversights in the middlegame. By the time the game was adjourned, his position was hopeless and he resigned the game without resuming.
Botvinnik v. Matulović on Board 9 saw the Yugoslav grandmaster adopt the Leningrad variation of the Dutch Defence. Botvinnik gained a clear advantage but failed to capitalise on his chances and the game was ultimately drawn at the 63rd move.
On the 9th board, Najdorf v. Tal was a fairly uneventful King’s Indian Defence that saw a draw agreed at the 25th move.
The Keres v. Ivkov encounter on bottom board proved to be a very one-sided affair. Keres took full advantage of some anti-positional play by his opponent, and won convincingly.
Board 1
Bent Larsen – Leonid Stein [E 68]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
Note:
Commentary to this game is provided by Rudolf Marić, in place of Stein.
1.Nf3 Nf6 2.g3 g6 3.Bg2 Bg7 4.c4 0-0 5.0-0 d6 6.d4 Nbd7 7.Nc3 e5 8.e4 ed4 9.Nd4 Re8
Marić:
The most accurate move at this point is 9...Nc5, since after the move in the text White can obtain the better game, continuing 10.Be3 Nc5 11.f3!.
10.Re1
Larsen:
Probably 10.h3, the main theoretical variation, was better, however, I wanted to avoid well-known variations.
Marić:
A continuation that is encountered less often. Possible was 10.Be3, and 10.h3 has been encountered innumerable times in practice.
10...Ng4
Marić:
The undefended knight at d4 gives the possibility of undertaking this tactical thrust. Evidently, Larsen already intended the prepared reply and decided on this original move 11.f3 instead of 11.h3. The question, however, is whether it is possible to consider the move 11.f3 to be wholly correct.
11.f3
Larsen:
(!?) A risky move, but in my opinion, a playable one.
11...Nge5 12.b3 Nc5 13.Be3 f5
Larsen:
(!) An energetic answer which prevents White from carrying out his plan: to build up a strong position with Qd2 and then Rad1.
After the text move White must risk complications if he does not wish to be left with the e4-pawn isolated and the black knight occupying an ideal square at e5. It is clear that 14.ef5?? does not work because of 14...Ned3, with a win for Black.
Marić:
An excellent move, eloquently speaking of the fact that White has handled the opening inaccurately. Now the move 14.ef5 is a crude blunder, on account of the tactical possibility 14...Ned3 15.Re2 Re3! and White’s rook perishes.
14.Qd2 fe4
Marić:
A blunder. Black could decide the struggle in the centre in his favour with the intermediate tactical move 14...Ned3!, since after the forced 15.Re2 fe4 16.Ne4 (if 16.fe4 Ne5 17.Bg5, then 17...Nc4! etc.) 16...Ne4 17.Qd3 Nc5, securing a good game. After the move in the text, White, in his turn - also by means of accurate moves - can avoid the creation of an isolated pawn and retain a somewhat better game.
15.Bg5
15...Qd7
Larsen:
Whereas Stein spent a long time considering his 13th move, he played this one a tempo, so the conclusion is that he had already planned it. 15...Bf6 would have been awkward for me, because it would lead to an equal position after 16.Bf6 Qf6 17.Ne4 Ne4 18.Re4 c5 19.f4! cd4 20.Qd4 Bf5 21.fe5 de5 22.Qd5 Kh8 23.Re2 Re7.
Instead 18...Bf5?! would be a doubtful move, because of the only good reply 19.Rf4! (after 19.Ree1 Black might continue 19...Nd3, or after 19.Re3 Nc6, in both cases with a better position for Black. 15...Nc4?! would be very dangerous for Black because of 16.bc4 Bd4 17.Qd4 Qg5 18.Nd5.
Marić:
Still another mistake, which is the cause of the subsequent difficulties. In this case Black ought to play 15...Bf6!, and on 16.Bf6 Qf6 17.Ne4 Ne4 18.Re4 Black, with the move 18...Bf5!, obtains a level game. For White the move 19.Rf4 would have been forced (if 19.Re3 Nd3 20.Nc2 Re3 21.Qe3 Nb4! etc.), and after 19...Nc6 (but absolutely not 19...c5 20.Nf5 gf5 21.Qc3 Re6 22.Bh3 and White wins) 20.Rd1 Nd4 21.Rd4 Re7, Black’s chances are not bad.
16.Ne4 Ne4 17.Re4 Nc6
Larsen: A critical position! Now after 18.Re8 Qe8 19.Be3 Qe3 White would lose a piece, while after 18.Be3 Re4 Black would equalise. This means that White must sacrifice, and after 12 minutes’ thought I decided to do so, though I could just as well have done so right away.
Marić:
Here there was the interesting possibility 17...d5 18.cd5 Qd5, and after 19.f4 Nf3! 20.Bf3 Re4 White loses. Correct would have been 19.Rae1! and Black has no reliable defence against the threat of 20.f4.
RR: Interestingly, the later game Pr. Nikolić - Gulko (Hastings, 1989/90) continued instead 17...Nf7(!) 18.Re8 Qe8 19.Be3 c5, etc. - Griffin.
18.Nc6!
Marić:
For White there was no other continuation, since all of the remaining ones led to the better game for Black. For example, 18.Be3 Re4 19.fe4 Qe8 or 18.Re8 Qe8 19.Be3 Qe3! etc.
18...Re4
Larsen:
18...Ba1 would be followed by 19.Ne7 Kh8 20.Qe1! with a double threat at a1 and g6. 19...Kg7 is not better because of 20.Bh6.
Marić:
Had Black taken the exchange White could have gained an irresistible attack:
18...Ba1 19.Ne7 Kh8 20.Qe1! Bg7 21.Ng6 hg6 22.Re8 Kh7 23.Qe4 etc.
19.fe4
19...Qc6
Larsen:
After the game everybody wanted to known what would have happened if Stein had taken the rook. I find this a very dangerous move, since there is no way to protect the king after 19...Ba1 20.Ne7. After 20...Kg7 the continuation might be: 21.Qf4 Qe8 22.Nd5 Qf7 23.Nc7 Qf4 24.Bf4 Rb8 25.Bd6 Kg8 26.e5 with a great advantage for White.
Another possibility was 20...Kf8. During the game my plan was to continue with 21.Qf2 and after 21...Ke8 22.e5 with the streong threat 23.Nd5, and probably after 22...de5 23.Nd5 Bd4 24.Be3 the position would have been very promising and, according to Janošević, even winning. However, after 20...Kf8, 21.Qf4 Ke8 22.Qh4 would not be good for White because of 22...Qg4!, which permits Black to attack. Thus I think that after 20...Kf8 the best move was 21.e5! and after 21...Be5 22.Qf2 Kg7 (if 22...Ke8 23.Bd5) 23.Bh6! Kh6 24.Qf8 White would have the better chances (24...Bg7 25.Ng8), whereas 21...de5 22.Qc1 would be threatening. For instance: 22...Qd4 23.Kh1 Bf5 24.Nf5 gf5 25.Bb7, or 23...c6 24.Qf1 Ke8 25.Nc6 etc. It is also possible that 19...bc6 was better than the text move. I think that this is very likely the case. During the game I had in mind, after 19...bc6, an interesting possibility 20.e5!?. However, after 20...Be5 21.Re1 the situation is not quite clear, while after 20.Rf1 Black would most probably equalise.
Marić:
After this move, Black finds himself in the worse position until the end of the game. Larsen undoubtedly has something Lasker-like about him; he resorts to psychology much more often than any other modern grandmaster. The present game is the best example of this, since in general it rests on psychological nuances beginning with the first moves in the opening. Stein should not be criticised for the fact that he was not able to orient himself in the labyrinth of variations and to find in them the thread which led to salvation. He ought to have examined 19...Ba1 and tested the accuracy of the calculations of the Danish grandmaster (although I personally believe that Larsen undertook this manoeuvre in the main on intuition, and not on ‘mathematics’).
After 19...Ba1 20.Ne7 there arises the critical position and now Black has to decide where to put the king, with whom the white pieces are flirting. If 20...Kh8 then 21.Qf4 Bg7 22.Qf7 and mate is unavoidable. Leading to the same outcome is 20...Kg7 21.Qf4! Qe8 22.Nd5 Qf7 23.Nc7 Qf4 24.Bf4 Rb8 25.Bd6 Kg8 26.e5 and White has a decisive advantage. Citing these variations as an example, Larsen expressed the opinion that critical would have been the continuation 20...Kf8, after which the strongest, in his view, was the reply 21.e5. None the less this is questionable, since Black has significant possibilities of defending, although White has good compensation in the undeveloped state of Black’s forces.
After 21.e5 obviously best was the continuation 21...de5 (if 21...Be5, then 22.Qf2 Kg7 23.Bh6!! Kh6 24.Qf8 Bg7 25.Ng8!, and mate). Now 22.Qf2 will not do, since after 22...Ke8 White does not have time to continue 23.Bd5 Qe7! followed by ...Bd4. On 22.Qb4 there is the reply 22...c5, while on 22.Bd5 Bd4 23.Kh1 c6 etc. In the opinion of Larsen, continuing 22.Qc1 White retains good attacking prospects. Nonetheless, after 22...Bd4 23.Kh1 c6! 24.Qf1 Ke8 it is unclear what White can undertake, since on 25.Qf6 there follows 25...e4! and Black wins.
Is there another continuation for White besides 21.e5? Evidently, the most effective was 21.Qd1! Be5 22.Qf1 Ke8 (22...Kg7 23.Bh6! etc.) 23.Nc8! Qc8 24.Bh3 (in this lies the point of the manoeuvre Qd1-f1 24...Qb8 25.Be6 and mate. However, if the black king had found itself on the square f7 and not f8, this possibility would have been excluded. Thus, the exchange sacrifice from the practical viewpoint was undoubtedly correct.
20.e5!
Larsen:
No more complications, and with this move White in all variations retains a slight positional advantage.
Marić:
A passing pawn sacrifice of a pawn, which White soon regains, obtaining the better position. The critical moment in the game has passed, and Black is faced with a long and difficult defence.
20...Qc5 21.Kh1 Be6
Larsen:
(!) This is the best answer. After 21...Qe5? Black would soon lose because of 22.Re1, and 21...Be5 would be followed by the very strong move 22.Bd5 Kh8 23.Re1, or 22...Kg7 23.Rf1 with advantage for White.
Marić:
Black did not risk taking the pawn with the queen or with the bishop, since after 21...Be5 22.Bd5 Kh8 23.Rf1 the outcome of the game would have been decided.
22.Bb7 Rf8 23.Be3 Qe5 24.Re1
24...Qc3
Larsen:
This move took Stein more than half an hour. In the ensuing endgame White’s position is slightly better because of Black’s weak pawns on the queen’s wing. 24...Bh3 was an interesting possibility; however, after 25.Ba7 c5 26.Qd5 Kh8 27.Qe5 Be5 28.Bg2 (if 28...Bg2 29.Kg2 Ra8 30.Bc5) or after 26...Qd5 27.Bd5 Kh8 28.a4 White would have good prospects of winning.
Marić:
After thorough analysis, Black decides to transpose to an endgame, hoping to reduce White’s advantage by means of simplification.
25.Kg2
Larsen:
A useful move, and probably better than 25.Qc3 Bc3 26.Rc1 Ba5! since now 27.Ba7 would be followed by 27...c5, with an uncertain situation.
25...Qd2 26.Bd2 Bd4 27.Bf4 Kf7
Larsen:
Another possibility was 27...g5, which would probably have been followed by 28.Re6 gf4 29.Bd5 Kh8 30.g4 and, in spite of the opposite-coloured bishops, White would have a clear advantage. After the move in the game Black is put onto the defensive.
Marić:
Black obtained active play with the move 27...g5!?, since White after 28.Bg5 Rf2 29.Kh1 Bh3 would have ended up in a position of stalemate, whereas on 28.Re6 gf4 29.Bd5 Kh8 White’s winning prospects are minimised. Letting slip this chance, in time trouble, Black can no longer hope for salvation.
28.Bh6 Rb8 29.Bc6 Bf6 30.Be3 a6 31.Bd2 Rb6 32.Bf3 Rb8 33.Ba5 Bd8
Larsen:
Stein was already pressed for time, yet he did not fall into the trap 33...Rc8??, which would have been followed by 34.Re6!.
34.Rf1 Kg8 35.Bc6 Bf7 36.g4
Marić:
Black’s position is hopeless. White now quietly puts in place the final operation.
36...Be7 37.Kg3 Rd8 38.Rd1 Bf6 39.h4 Be5 40.Kg2 Rc8 41.g5 Kg7
42.Bd7
Larsen:
This was the sealed move. My analyses showed that I would win a pawn in any variation, and I was sure that this material advantage would be enough to win the game.
42...Rd8
Larsen:
Not 42...Rb8 because of 43.Bc7 Rb7 44.Bd6 etc.
43.Bg4 d5 44.Bf3 h6
Larsen:
(!?) Black immediately surrenders a pawn to reduce the material. However, after 44...d4 45.Re1 Bd6 46.Re4 Black loses either the d4- or the c7-pawn, and after 44...d4 45.Re1 Re8 White plays 46.Bb4! (this is certainly stronger than 46.Bc6 Re6 47.Bd7 Re7 48.Bc7 Rd7 with chances for Black to save himself) 46...Bd6 47.Re8 Be8 48.Bd6 cd6 49.Kf2 White would win the bishop endgame without any difficulty.
Marić:
A pawn is lost, since on 44...d4 there follows 45.Re1 Bd6 46.Re4! etc. If instead Black replies 45...Re8 there could follow 46.Bb4 with the threat of Bc6 etc.
45.gh6 Kh6 46.Bd2 Kg7 47.Bg5 Rd6 48.Bd5
Larsen:
This move is of great importance. There were many fine players in the audience who believed this endgame would end in a draw, but I was quite sure of winning it. I am not positive, but it seems to me that somewhere I had already seen something like it.
Marić:
White enters an endgame, having preliminarily studied it in all its finesses. The course of this part of this exciting and interesting struggle held the attention of more than 200 spectators. The endgame has undoutedly important theoretical interest in its own right.
48...Bd5 49.Rd5 Rd5 50.cd5 Kf7 51.Kf3 Ke8 52.Bf4 Bf6 53.Bc7 Bh4 54.b4 Kd7 55.Bb6 Bf6 56.Bc5 Bb2 57.a4 Ba3 58.a5 Bb2 59.Kf4 Bf6 60.Ke4 Bc3 61.b5
Larsen:
(!) In this position White’s passed pawns, supported by the bishop, now completely block Black’s forces, so that White’s king manages to remove from the board both of Black’s pawns.
61...ab5 62.a6 Kc8 63.d6 Kb8 64.Kd5 Bf6
Larsen:
64...b4 then 65.Kc4 etc.
65.Kc6 g5 66.Kb5 g4 67.Kc4 Ka8 68.Kd3 Be5 69.d7 Bc7 70.Ke4 g3 71.Kf3 Bd8
Larsen:
71...Kb8 would be followed by 72.Bd6!.
72.Kg3 Ba5 73.Kg4 Bd8 74.Kf5 Ba5 75.Ke6 Bc7 76.Kf7 Ba5 77.Ke8 Bc7 78.Be7 Ba5 79.Bd8 Bd2 80.Bb6 Bg5 81.Bc5
Black resigned.
1 : 0
[Larsen/Marić]
Board 2
Tigran Petrosian – Robert Fischer [D 90]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.c4 g6 2.d4
Fischer:
2.e4 is also possible so as to avoid Grüenfeld’s Defence
2...Nf6 3.Nc3 d5 4.Nf3 Bg7 5.cd5 Nd5 6.Bd2
Petrosian:
This modest-looking continuation appeared in tournament practice after the victory that Smyslov gained over Hort in the tournament in Skopje, in 1969. As far as is known to me, this was also played by the Egyptian master Basjuni in a Zonal tournament in 1957. Perhaps, to the atlas of geographical chess names one may also add Egyptian to the already established English, French, Russian, Spanish, and so on, openings. It may also be noted that until the present game no-one had managed to demonstrate a clear path to equality.
Fischer:
I do not think that this variation creates any problems for Black. The best variation is 6.Bd2 0-0 7.Rc1 and now 7...Nc3 8.Bc3 Qd5 or 6.Bd2 0-0 7.Rc1 Nb6 8.e3 N8d7 followed by ...e7-e5.
6...c5 7.Rc1
Fischer:
(!) Black cannot proceed now with 7...cd4 because of 8.Nd5.
7...Nc3 8.Bc3 cd4 9.Nd4 0-0 10.e3
10...Qd5
Petrosian:
(!) From here the queen dominates the whole board. If, for example, 11.Qb3, then after 11...Qe4! it is still not so simple for White to complete development.
Fischer:
(!) Another possibility was 10...a6, as Larry Evans suggested after the game, but after 11.Bc4 b5 12.Qf3 White’s position is slightly better. The text move equalises immediately.
11.Nb5
Fischer:
Black can best answer 11.Qb3 with 11...Qe4!. After 11.a3 Black can continue 11...Rd8.
11...Qd1 12.Rd1 Nc6 13.Bg7
Fischer:
After 13.Bc4 Black answers 13...a6, since 14.Bg7 does not work because of 14...ab5! 15.Bf8 bc4 16.Bh6 Ra2 with at least a draw, or simply 13...Bf5 which is similar to the moves in the game.
13...Kg7
Petrosian:
(?) Significant simplification has taken place and one could expect a rapid conclusion of peace. I decided to continue the struggle.
Maybe I would manage to exploit the circumstance that the white king is a few centimetres nearer to the queen’s flank, where some sort of developments could still occur.
14.Be2 Bf5 15.g4
Fischer:
The only chance for some initiative before Black plays 15...Rac8.
15...a6
Petrosian:
(!) Simplest of all.
Fischer:
Definitely not 15...Be6 because of 16.Nc7.
16.Nc3
Fischer:
16.Nc7 Rac8 17.Na6 Be4 (17...Be6 18.Nc5 Ba2 was dubious) 18.f3 Bf3 19.Bf3 ba6 with the better chances for Black because of White’s weaknesses on the king-side.
16...Be6 17.f4 Rfd8 18.Kf2 Nb4 19.a3 Nd5 20.Ne4 Nf6
Petrosian:
Fischer is playing extremely cautiously, preventing the possible complications after 20...Rac8 21.Ng5.
Fischer:
20...Rac8 was not good because of 21.Ng5, and 21...Rc2 is out of the question because of 22.Ne6 fe6 23.e4.
21.Nf6 ef6
Fischer:
(!) It was not necessary to weaken the pawn-structure. After 21...Kf6 22.g5 Kg7 23.Bf3 Rac8 24.Bb7 Rc2 25.Kg3 Rd1 26.Rd1 Rb2 27.Ba6 Rb3! Black regains a pawn 28.Rd3?? Rb6! gains a piece.
22.Bf3 Rac8 23.Rd8 Rd8 24.Rd1
24...Rc8
Fischer:
(?) 24...Rd1 25.Bd1 Kf8 was simpler, and Black would have no problems.
Petrosian:
The draw was more or less obvious after the exchange of rooks, followed by the march of the king to d6. But for some reason Fischer did not like this...
25.Rd2
Petrosian:
...or he moved away with the rook, hoping for a cunning, but rather obvious trap: 25.Bb7 Rc2 26.Kg3 Rb2 27.Ba6 Rb3 28.Rd3 White has an extra pawn, is it not so? - No. After 28...Rb6 Black has an extra piece.
25...Rc7 26.h4 h6 27.Kg3 b5
Fischer:
The threat was 28.Rd6 and 29.Rb6 with Black completely passive.
28.Rd6 Rc2 29.b4 Rc3 30.Ra6 Bc4
Fischer:
(!) This was the best. With 30...Re3 31.Kf2 Rc3 32.Be2 White would have a few more chances.
31.f5
Fischer:
31.Kf2 would have been followed by 31...Rc2 32.Ke1 Rc3 33.Kd2? Rd3 etc.
31...g5
Fischer:
31...Re3 32.Kf4 Re1 33.fg6 fg6 34.Ra7 Kf8 35.Be4 was another possibility but I did not like the passive position of Black’s king.
32.hg5 hg5 33.Kf2 Rc2 34.Kg1 Rc1 35.Kg2 Rc2 36.Kg3
Fischer:
White tries to get the game adjourned without any change in the position on the board.
36...Rc3 37.Kf2 Rc2 38.Ke1 Rc3 39.Ra5 Re3 40.Kf2 Rd3
Petrosian:
In this position the game was adjourned. White’s chances consist in winning the b5-pawn in exchange for the pawns at g4 and then f5, after which the pawn race decides.
The rook endgame after 41.Bc6 Kf8 42.Bb5 Bb5 43.Rb5 Ra3 is seemingly dangerous for Black on account of 44.Rd5 followed by 45.Rd4 and the march of the white king towards the b-pawn. But in home analysis Fischer discovered an instructive draw.
41.Bc6
Fischer:
This was the sealed move.
41...Kf8 42.Bb5 Bb5 43.Rb5 Ra3
Fischer:
When analysing this, I came to the conclusion that this was a drawing end-game.
44.Rd5 Rb3 45.Rd4 Ke7 46.Ke2 Ra3 47.Kd2 Rb3 48.Kc2 Re3
Petrosian:
It transpires that the white is not going anywhere, while at that moment when the white pawn gets to the 5th rank, there follows an attack on the pawn, after which either the black rook occupies a position behind it, or the black king is in time to blockade it.
Fischer:
(!) The key move.
49.Rd3 does not work because of 49...Re4, nor does 49.b5 Re5 50.Rb4 Kd6 51.b6 Re8 52.b7 Kc7 and the b-pawn is doomed.
49.Kb2 Ke8
Fischer:
The only move. Black cannot move his rook from the best square.
50.Kc1 Re2!
Fischer:
White tries one more trick. With 50...Ke7? 51.Kc2! (51...Ra3? 52.Rd3! and wins easily) 51...Ke8 52.b5 Re5 53.b6 Rb5 54.Rd6 Ke7 55.Rc6 the approach of White’s king would be decisive.
51.Kd1
Fischer:
With 51.b5 Re5 52.b6 Rb5 53.Rd6 Ke7 54.Rc6 Rb4! Black gains the g-pawn.
51...Ra2 52.Ke1 Rb2
Agreed drawn.
½ : ½
[Fischer/Petrosian]
Group photo with all players
Board 3
Lajos Portisch – Viktor Korchnoi [A 33]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.Nf3 c5 2.c4 Nf6 3.Nc3 Nc6 4.d4 cd4 5.Nd4 e6 6.g3
Korchnoi:
A fashionable continuation, according to contemporary theory, the most dangerous for Black.
6...Qb6 7.Nb3 Ne5
Korchnoi:
On 7...Bb4, unpleasant to me seemed 8.Be3 Qc7 9.Bg2 Ne5 10.c5 Nc4 11.Qc1 with advantage for White.
8.e4 Bb4 9.Qe2
Korchnoi:
All of this was encountered for the first time in the game Taimanov - Geller (22nd USSR Championship). There, if my memory does not betray me, 9...a5 was met with. This move seemed to me to be unconvincing. Also insufficient to me seemed 9...d6 in view of 10.Bd2! and White threatens to play f2-f4 and Nc3-b5 with a clear advantage, and therefore I replied...
9...0-0
Portisch:
In a game between Taimanov and Geller, Black played 9...a5 with complications.
Korchnoi:
Threatening ...d7-d5. White must play energetically.
10.f4 Nc6 11.e5 Ne8 12.Bd2
Portisch:
(!)
Korchnoi:
Preventing the move 12...d6, on which there follows 13.ed6 Nd6 14.c5! and White wins a piece. Instead of 13...Nd6 possible is 13...Nd4 14.Nd4 Qd4 but after 15.0-0-0 White stands clearly better.
12...f6
Portisch:
Black has no other move with which to obtain counter-play. 12...d6?? would be a great mistake because of 13.ed6 Nd6 14.c5 with the win of a piece.
13.c5 Qd8
RR: Later, 13...Qc7 was tried in a number of games here, beginning with Nei - Spassky (7th USSR People’s Spartakiad, Moscow 1979). After 14.Nb5 Bd2 15.Qd2 Qd8 16.ef6 Nf6 17.Bg2 b6 Black has fair counter-chances; interestingly, Korchnoi played both sides of this position in 1988, v. Gulko (Lugano Open) with White, and v. Morovic Fernández (4th match-game, Viña del Mar). - Griffin.
14.a3
Korchnoi:
Instead of this whole operation to exchange the dark-squared bishop, deserving attention, in my opinion, is the simple 14.Bg2 with superiority in position for White.
14...Bc3 15.Bc3 fe5 16.Be5
Portisch:
It would certainly have been risky to continue 16.fe5?! with the idea of later castling on the queen-side.
Korchnoi:
On 16.fe5 Black intended to reply 16...Qg5, holding up the opponent’s development. But still, this was possibly more consistent for White than the move in the text.
16...b6
Korchnoi:
But now for Black there was a point in continuing 16...Ne5 in the case of 17.Qe5 it was possible to play, analogously to in the game, 17...b6 18.Bg2 Ba6!. Also on 17.fe5, 17...b6 gave Black satisfactory play.
RR: Later, the game Dvoirys - Tal (USSR Team Championship, Voroshilovgrad 1985) continued instead 16...Ne5 17.Qe5 a5, etc.) - Griffin.
17.Bg2
17...Ne5
Portisch:
(?) 17...bc5 would be followed by 18.Bc6, but a better move was 17...Bb7! 18.0-0 Qc8! which, because of the threat of ...Ba6, would offer Black good prospects.
Korchnoi:
(?!) A strange move, the consequence of an absurd oversight! Continuing simply 17...Bb7 Black, having outstripped the opponent in development, would have presented him with the greatest problems. In this case it is not easy to demonstrate that White’s position is better. For example, 18.0-0 Qc8 (threatening ...Ba6) 19.Rfd1? Ne5 20.Qe5 Bg2 21.Kg2 Qc6 22.Kg1 Rf5 and Black has the better chances. Evidently correct is 19.Rf2!, and White retains the advantage.
18.Ba8 Nf7
Portisch:
After the game Korchnoi admitted that he had not noticed that after 18...Ba6 White could have taken the bishop, and that he was not obliged to play 19.Qe5, as he had thought.
Korchnoi:
I had intended to play here 18...Ba6 19.Qe5 Qa8 20.0-0-0 Bc4 21.Qc3 Bd5 22.Rhe1 Nf6 with excellent play, and suddently saw that White replies 19.Qa6 Qa8 20.0-0-0 and Black can resign. I had to play more modestly.
19.Bg2
Portisch:
(?) White goes wrong. After 19.Bf3! bc5 20.Nc5 Qb6 21.Qf2 Qb5 22.Be2 he would have a winning position.
Korchnoi:
White has a winning position. It was possible to simply castle, since the variation (19.0-0) 19...bc5 20.Nc5 Qb6 21.Qf2 d5 will not do, on account of 22.f5. However, the move in the text also does not spoil anything.
19...bc5 20.Nc5 Qb6
Portisch:
(!) I failed to notice now that after having won an exchange, the position isn’t as simple as I thought.
21.Qf2
Korchnoi:
Also not the best move. More energetic was 21.b4 a5 22.Na4 Qb8 23.b5 or 22...Qa7 23.Qf2 with a completely winning position.
21...Qb5
Portisch:
(!)
22.Bf1
Portisch:
(?!) After the game I was asked why I had accepted a draw by repetition. During the game, and later on when analysing it, neither I nor my opponent could find a follow-up which would give White the advantage. In view of my limited time, I did not wish to take any chances because I was hoping to win my four-game match against Korchnoi.
At first I thought 22.0-0-0 was a good move, but decided against it because of 22...Nfd6! with the threat of ...Nc4 together with ...Ba6, which gives Black attacking chances. 22.Bf3 would mean a loss of a tempo, and after 22...d6 23.Ne4 Ba6 it is not clear how White’s king can escape; therefore Black has compensation for the material loss.
Later that evening, after the game was over, I discovered the path that would have led to victory - 22.Rd1! with the direct threat of 23.Nd3 to consolidate the position with castling on the king-side.
Of course I would have played on had I noticed this possibility during the game, since I would not be risking anything then. Thus my only mistake was that I did not ask the captain of the team if he agreed with a draw. However, without 22.Rd1!, who knows what could have happened in the game, since both players were running short of time.
22...Qc6 23.Bg2 Qb5 24.Bf1 Qc6 25.Bg2
Korchnoi:
Instead of this repetition of moves, White could with full justification play for a win: 25.Rg1 Ne5 (here is what had scared by opponent - on 26.Bg2 there follows 26...Qc5) 26.Be2 d6 27.Qe3! and Black stands badly! Admittedly, at this point Portisch had remaining only 25 minutes for 16 moves in a complicated position, and one can understand him...
Agreed drawn.
½ : ½
[Korchnoi/Portisch]

Lev Polugaevsky – Vlastimil Hort [D 53]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.c4 Nf6 2.Nc3 e6 3.d4 d5 4.Bg5 Be7 5.e3 h6 6.Bh4 b6
Hort:
(?!) Black experiments with an accelerated version of Tartakower’s variation. This game is an interesting one from the theoretical point of view, though the queens were exchanged at an early stage of the game.
7.cd5 Nd5 8.Be7 Qe7 9.Nd5 ed5 10.Rc1 Bb7
Polugaevsky:
The opponents are playing the Makogonov - Bondarevsky system in the Queen’s Gambit. Black’s last move is inaccurate. Most often of all Black’s bishop goes to the square e6. Correct is 10...0-0. Now White could play simply 11.Nf3. But he prefers the manoeuvre Ng1-e2-f4.
11.Ne2
Hort:
This was the best move, since the knight moves to f4, where it can be more effective.
11...Qb4
Polugaevsky:
Black aims at simplification. Also possible was 11...0-0.
12.Qd2
Polugaevsky:
Deserving attention is the double-edged 12.Nc3.
12...Qd2 13.Kd2 Kd7
Polugaevsky:
(!) The best move in the given position. The king in the centre will defend the weaknesses on the queen’s flank.
14.h4
Polugaevsky:
(!) White aims, with the advance of the pawn, to blockade Black’s king’s flank, which will be useful to him in the future.
14...Nc6 15.Nf4 Kd6 16.h5 Ne7
Polugaevsky:
If 16...Nd8, then 17.Rh3 Ne6 18.Ne6 fe6 19.Rg3 with the advantage.
Hort:
Another manoeuvre which presented itself was 16...Nd8, which would however not be good because of the counter-manoeuvre Rh1-h3-g3.
17.Be2
Polugaevsky:
Deserving attention is 17.Bd3, preparing the transfer of the rook 18.Rh3-g3. But Black could, in his turn, reply 17...Bc8 and then 18...Bd7.
17...c5
Polugaevsky:
(?) A serious positional mistake, making Black’s situation very serious. It was necessary to restrict himself to waiting tactics with 17...c6 and then 18...Bc8 and 19...Bd7.
Hort:
(?) A positional mistake which exposes Black’s king. Another possibility was 17...c6 followed by the manoeuvre ...Ra8-c8-c7 with a slight advantage for White.
18.dc5
Polugaevsky:
(!)
18...bc5 19.Bf3 Rab8 20.Rhd1
Polugaevsky:

20...Rhd8 21.Ke1
Hort:
White’s position is much better; he is exerting pressure along the central files.
21...Bc6
Hort:
Another error. 21...a5 would be better, in order to prevent, with 22.Nd3 Rdc8 23.b3 a4, White’s knight from reaching the b2-square.
22.b3 a5
Polugaevsky:
Otherwise Black cannot create any counter-play at all.
23.Nd3 Rb5
24.Nb2!
Polugaevsky:
With this manoeuvre of the knight White puts before Black the necessity to sacrifice a pawn. Black’s following move is forced, since 25.Na4 and 26.Be2 is threatened.
Hort:
(!) White has a great advantage. One could say that Black’s prospects are very dim. This whole game shows that one must not play expressly to draw.
24...Kc7
Hort:
The best move and the only chance. Black surrenders a pawn in the hope of getting a draw.
25.Na4
Polugaevsky:
White could have not hurried to win the pawn, but after the preparatory 25.Rd2 there would all the same have followed 25...c4.
Hort:
25.Rd2 was probably better.
25...c4
Polugaevsky:
In a difficult situation Black does not lose spirit and, ridding himself of the weak pawns in the centre, obtains counter-chances.

27...Rd1
28.Bd1

A pleasant surprise for Black, who was sure that after 28.Kd1 Kd6 29.Bc6 Nc6 he was lost, although White would have to play very carefully.

Perhaps the only mistake by White in this game.

31.Rc3 Bd5 32.a3 Rb2 33.Rd3 Kc5

34.Rc3 Kd6 35.Rd3 Kc5 36.Rc3
Polugaevsky:
White had not noticed that after 36...Kd6 the position is repeated for the third time, but in fairness we note that he does not have a win.

Svetozar Gligorić – Efim Geller [E 92]
‘Rest of the World’ - USSR
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6 3.Nc3 Bg7 4.e4 d6 5.Nf3 0-0 6.Be2 e5 7.d5 a5
Gligorić:
The favourite method of Geller and Stein. Black posts the knight at a6 and then proceeds to develop the queen’s wing, preparing counter-action on the other side.
8.Bg5
Gligorić:
In the 2nd round White tried to gain the advantage with 8.Be3.

Gligorić:
9...g5 would weaken the light squares on the king’s wing, and accordingly Black avoids that move.
10.0-0
Gligorić:
(!) In Bukić - Geller (Budva, 1967) the continuation was 10.Nd2 Bd7 11.0-0 Qe8 12.b3 Nh7! 13.Rb1 h5! 14.h3 Bh6 15.a3 Nc5 16.b4 ab4 17.ab4 Na4 18.Na4 Ra4 with sufficient counter-play for Black. The move in the game plans a more active role for the white king’s knight.
10...Bd7
11.Ne1
Gligorić:
(!)
Geller:
In the year 1968, during the match USSR v. Yugoslavia, Gligorić twice played in this position 11.Nd2 against me. Also in our encounter in the international tournament at Belgrade (1969) he employed, admittedly in a slightly different position, this same ‘standardised’ move. It could be thought that on the basis of these encounters, and also as a result of the experience in the 2nd round, that Gligorić had become convinced that in such positions the knight must stand at d3. Indeed, here White’s knight supports in the best way White’s offensive on the queen’s flank.
11...Nc5 12.Nd3 b6
Gligorić:
12...Nd3 13.Bd3 b6 would give White the more agreeable position.
13.f3 Qe8
Geller:
A significant inaccuract, which leads to a loss of time for Black. Better was 13...Qc8.
14.Nc5
Gligorić:
Now is the moment for the exchange (when it is harder for Black’s knight to reach d6), since the manoeuvre b2-b3, a2-a3 and b3-b4 is much too slow; in the end Black simply replies ...Nc5-a4 and exchanges his knight under more favourable conditions.
14...bc5
Gligorić:
14...dc5 is also possible, but the circumstances are no longer as good as in the previous encounter between the same opponents in the 2nd round.
15.a4
Gligorić:
(!) Establishing the weakness of the pawn at a5 and providing a strong base for the knight at b5. White has some advantage.
15...Nh5 16.Nb5 Qb8
Geller:
This move too is a loss of time. Better was 16...Qc8.
17.Bd3
Gligorić:
(!) Because of the need to defend the queen’s wing the black pieces are not co-ordinating very well, so the most important thing now is to restrict Black’s counter-action on the king’s wing.
Geller:
It is essential to preserve this bishop from exchange.
17...Nf4
Gligorić:
This knight has no future here. Another possibility is 17...f5.
Geller:
(?) This move is a serious mistake, which puts Black’s game in a doubtful position. 17...f5 ought to be played, after which Black could have had counter-play in the centre and also on the king’s flank.
18.Bc2 f5 19.Ra3
Gligorić:
19.Kh1 was perhaps better.
Geller:
(!)
19...Rf7 20.Kh1
Gligorić:
20.Rb3 Qc8 would be pointless.
20...Qc8 21.Be1
Geller:
Gligorić has conducted this part of the game very finely and has achieved a significant positional advantage.
21...Nh5
Geller:
Alas! The mistake must be belatedly recognised.
22.ef5
Gligorić:
22...f4 would be unpleasant. But in this way White maintains pressure on both sides, and the bishop at c2 has an important task.
22...Bf5
Gligorić:
Otherwise, it would be difficult for Black to mobilise his pieces on the queen’s wing.
23.Bf5
Gligorić:
Maybe it would have been better not to make this exchange, but the text move is a good one.
23...gf5 24.g3 Nf6 25.Re3

The battle now centres on the king’s wing and White accordingly redeploys his pieces to better squares.
25...Qd7 26.Bc3 Kh8 27.Ree1 Ra6

28.Qd2 c6
Geller:
(!) Black is suffocating, and his last move, although not changing the assessment of the position, promises him some prospects of enlivening the play.
29.dc6 Qc6 30.Rd1 Ne8

Geller:
(?) Up to this point Gligorić had played irreproachably and has achieved a winning position. The continuation 31.Qd5! put Black in an unenviable situation.
31...Qc8! 32.Rd5
Gligorić:

32...Qe6 33.b3
Gligorić:
White can do nothing to prevent Black from achieving ...d6-d5.

With this move White perhaps lets slip all of his chances. Correct was 33.Rfd1.

(!)

In the case of the acceptance of the pawn sacrifice, with 38.Qc5 Rc6 39.Qe3 Rc2 40.Na3 Ra2 41.Nc4 f4! Black obtains a dangerous attack.

Vasily Smyslov – Friđrik Ólafsson [E 05]
USSR - ‘Rest of the World’
1.c4 e6 2.g3 d5 3.Bg2 Nf6 4.Nf3 Be7
Ólafsson:
I had already made up my mind to open up the game with 4...dc4, but I was not quite sure how to cope with Smyslov’s innovation 5.Na3!. That is why I delayed this capture.

A solid line is 6...Nbd7 followed by 7...c6 and 8...b6 or 8...b5.

Ólafsson:
After the game it seemed to be doubtful judgement on my part to play this opening against a leading expert in it. Another day I might try 7...c5!.

11.Rd1
Ólafsson:
Without doubt the most logical move. 11.Nc3 is not as strong on account of 11...Nb4 12.Qb1 c5. Taking the pawn on d4 would be bad for Black: 11...Nd4 12.Nd4 Bg2 13.Rfd1!.
11...Nb4
Ólafsson:
The idea of this move is to drive White’s queen to an inferior square, and to free Black’s game with ...c7-c5 later on. The freeing move ...c7-c5 meets with more obstacles than I expected.
12.Qc1
Ólafsson:
Of course 12.Qc7 Qc7 13.Bc7 is out of the question because of 13...Nc2.
12...Qc8
Ólafsson:
Up to this point I was quite satisfied with my game and I could not see how White could prevent the freeing manoeuvre ...c7-c5. In this position Smyslov thought for half an hour and made a marvellous move which illustrates that Black’s strategy is basically unsound.
13.Bg5
Smyslov:
(!) This manoeuvre hinders the advance 13...c5, on which there would have followed 14.Bf6 gf6 and the position of the black king is weakened.
Ólafsson:
(!) White would lose the exchange after 13.Bc7 Nfd5, or at least Black would get a very satisfactory game. The question is now whether Black can play 13...c5.
13...Nbd5
Ólafsson: I played this move as a safety measure as I did not want to weaken my king’s side after 13...c5 14.Bf6 gf6. Black is in fact very badly off in this position. White simply plays 15.Nc3 followed by 16.Qh6 and the various threats to Black’s king’s position should prove decisive.
RR: In fact, subsequently this line was shown to be relatively harmless for Black; it occurred, for example, in the game G. Agzamov - Karpov (50th USSR Championship, Moscow 1983), where Black continued 15...Rd8. - Griffin.
After 14...Bf6 15.dc5 there seems to be no way to regain the pawn on c5.
14.Nbd2
Ólafsson:
(!) Another strong move which I had overlooked. It does not directly prevent ...c7-c5, but the consequences of that move would be bad for Black: 14...c5 15.e4! Nb4 16.e5 Nfd5 17.Be7 Ne7 18.Qc5 Qc5 19.dc5. The point is now that Black cannot regain the pawn by means of 19...Nd3 because of 20.Nb3. Black’s position remains inferior. However, this may have been the best fighting chance for Black, as his next move leads to a strategically lost position.
14...h6
Smyslov:
If 14...c5, then 15.e4 Nb4 16.e5 Nfd5 17.Be7 Ne7 18.Qc5 Qc5 19.dc5 Nd3 20.Nb3 Nb2 21.Rd7 with advantage for White.

Ólafsson:

(!)

Smyslov:

25.Qe3 Rad8 26.Rd6 Rd6 27.cd6 c5

38...Qb7 39.Kg1 Nd6
Smyslov:
The battle is lost, and Black decides on extreme measures, giving up a piece for the dangerous passed pawn.
40.ed6 Qb6 41.Qf2 Qd6
Ólafsson:

42.Nc8

(!) The sealed move. In the case of 42...Rc8 43.Rc8 Qd1 44.Kg2 Qd5 45.Qf3 Qd2 46.Kh3 the king escapes from pursuit.

Black’s position is lost. However, he continues to offer desperate resistance, sensing the important significance of this encounter, which decided the fate of the match.
48...g5 49.Qd2 Kg7 50.Qd3 Qf6 51.Qd4 Qf5 52.Qd3 Qf6 53.Qe4 Qd8 54.Nf3 Qd1 55.Kf2 Qc1 56.Qe3
